> *just so the medicine tree of the Fully Awakened One."*¹⁴

This is, in brief, the harm that is the work of Māra.

---

## Part VII: Dispelling the Works of Māra

The dispelling of this is stated in the Ratnameghasūtra:¹⁵

> "And how, O son of good family, does the bodhisattva become skillful in the method of avoiding the work of Māra? Here, the bodhisattva entirely avoids unwholesome friends. He entirely avoids dwelling in an unsuitable place, the cultivation of service to worldly sciences and mantras, [excessive preoccupation with] gain, honor, reverence, service, and esteem.
>
> And whatever other secondary afflictions there are that obstruct the path to the factors of enlightenment—all these he entirely avoids. And he cultivates the antidotes to those."

---

## Part VIII: Characteristics of Unwholesome Friends

Here also the characteristic of an unwholesome friend is stated:¹⁶

> "The avoidance of evil friends is to be known through the avoidance of persons whose morality has failed. Likewise through avoidance of persons whose view has failed, whose conduct has failed, whose livelihood has failed. Through avoidance of persons who delight in communal life. Through avoidance of lazy persons. Through avoidance of persons who take pleasure in saṃsāra. Through avoidance of persons who have turned away from enlightenment. Through avoidance of association with householders—the avoidance of evil friends is to be known.
>
> And, O son of good family, by one avoiding these circumstances, a hostile mind should not be produced toward those persons, nor an angry mind, nor a contemptuous mind.
>
> Thus his mind should be established. For it was said by the Blessed One: 'Beings, according to their element—desire-element and so forth—flow together, are born, and stream forth; through association they are destroyed. Therefore I shall avoid association.'"

---

## Part IX: Confusion of Bodhicitta

The confusion of bodhicitta is also a harm.¹⁷ And its cause is stated in the Ratnakūṭa:

> "By four dharmas, O Kāśyapa, the bodhisattva's bodhicitta becomes confused. What are the four?
>
> 1. Through deceiving teachers, guides, and those worthy of gifts;
> 2. Through generating remorse in others who have no remorse;
> 3. Through spreading defamation and disgrace about beings who have set out in the Mahāyāna;
> 4. Through approaching others with deceit and cunning, not with superior intention."

---

## Part X: Avoidance of Bodhicitta Confusion

The avoidance of this is also stated here:¹⁸

> "By four dharmas, O Kāśyapa, the bodhisattva's bodhicitta becomes manifest in all births from the moment of birth; it does not become confused in between, up until sitting at the seat of enlightenment. What are the four?
>
> 1. He does not speak a deliberate lie even for the sake of [preserving] life—not even for the sake of laughter;
> 2. He stands before all beings with superior intention, having abandoned deceit and cunning;
> 3. He generates the perception of Teacher regarding all bodhisattvas;
> 4. He proclaims their praise in the four directions.
>
> And whatever beings he ripens, all of them he establishes in supreme perfect enlightenment, through non-aspiration to partial vehicles. By these four, O Kāśyapa..."

---

## Part XI: Dharma-Gift Protection

In the Siṃhaparipṛcchā it also says:¹⁹

> "One who never creates an obstacle to the gift of Dharma—
> By that, one quickly obtains meeting with the World-Protectors.
> Thus from the Dharma-gift one remembers [past] births—know this, O prince!
>
> By that one does not abandon bodhicitta in all births;
> Even in dream, that mind [is present]—how much more when awake!
>
> In whatever places of cessation, villages or cities,
> One takes up [the Dharma] for enlightenment—by that the mind does not abandon [bodhicitta]."

---

## Part XII: Four Dharmas of Non-Falling

In the Āryamañjuśrībuddhakṣetraguṇavyūhālaṃkārasūtra it also says:²⁰

> "Endowed with four dharmas, the bodhisattva does not fall from [his] vow...
> He becomes one whose pride is struck down; he becomes one who avoids jealousy and stinginess; and having seen the success of others, he does not become conceited."

---

## Part XIII: Unwavering Bodhicitta

This very thing—the most clear cause for the non-confusion of the suitable bodhicitta—is what is stated here in the Ratnakūṭa:²¹

> "Through mental cultivation of bodhicitta in all postures, through making bodhicitta foremost."

As the reading in the Candrapradīpasūtra states:²²

> "I declare and announce to you:
> However much a person thinks repeatedly,
> So his mind becomes inclined to those;
> He is supported by those thoughts."

---

## Part XIV: Discouragement as Harm

Discouragement is also a harm.²³ The avoidance of this is seen in the Ratnamegha:

> "Here the bodhisattva does not generate the thought: 'Enlightenment is hard to attain for one who is a human being. And this vigor of mine is small. I am lazy. And enlightenment requires many kalpas, many hundreds of kalpas, many thousands of kalpas, like one whose head is on fire [requiring immediate attention]. I am not capable of shouldering such a burden.'
>
> Rather, the bodhisattva should generate such a thought: 'Whatever tathāgatas, arhats, samyaksaṃbuddhas have awakened, are awakening, or will awaken—they too have awakened, are awakening, and will awaken by just such a method, by just such a practice, by just such vigor.
>
> They were not already in the state of tathāgata when they awakened. I too will strive in the same way, exert myself in the same way, with vigor common to all beings, with vigor that has all beings as its object—so that I may awaken to supreme perfect enlightenment.'"

---

## Part XV: The Bodhisattva's Stumbling

Another harm is seen in the Ratnakūṭa:²⁴

> "Trust in beings who have not been ripened is a stumbling of the bodhisattva. The proclamation of exalted Buddha-dharma to beings who have become unsuitable vessels is a stumbling of the bodhisattva. The proclamation of the Lesser Vehicle to beings of exalted aspiration is a stumbling of the bodhisattva.
>
> Disdain toward those who are properly mindful and endowed with morality and good dharmas, [combined with] gathering those of bad morality and unwholesome dharmas, is a stumbling of the bodhisattva."

---

## Part XVI: Lack of Aspiration as Harm

Lack of aspiration is also a harm.²⁵ As stated in the Rāṣṭrapālasūtra:

> "One for whom aspiration toward the Buddha, Dharma, and Saṅgha does not exist—
> In the training and precepts, for that one there is no aspiration.
> One of evil thought, opening to the three lower destinies—
>
> He, fallen from here, through the force of karma, is among humans, yet ignorant, confused.
> In the hells, in animal destinies, in hungry ghost destinies, he finds suffering."

---

## Part XVII: Avoidance of Lack of Aspiration

The dispelling of this is seen as stated in the Ratnakūṭa:²⁶

> "Regarding those profound [teachings] into which his intellect does not penetrate, he does not reject them, having made the Tathāgata himself the witness: 'The Tathāgata knows thus; I do not know. The Buddha's enlightenment is infinite; through the diversity of aspirations, the teaching of Dharma of the Tathāgatas proceeds to beings.'"

---

## Part XVIII: Skill in Avoiding Harm While Serving

One who is engaged in service should become skillful in avoidance of harm.²⁷ For in the Bodhisattvaprātimokṣa, service is enjoined regarding a fellow practitioner in Dharma-hearing, and regarding worship of the Tathāgata.

The procedure for this has come from the Ratnarāśisūtra:²⁸

> "There, by the monk who performs service, the minds of the entire Saṅgha of monks should be satisfied.
>
> There, for those monks who are forest-dwellers and dwell in secluded lodgings, by the service-performing monk absolutely no assignment of work should be given.
>
> But if work belonging to the Saṅgha, being trainee-work, should fall to a forest-dwelling monk, by this service-performing monk that should be done himself, or another monk should be requested—the forest-dwelling monk should not be oppressed.
>
> For the monk who lives on alms-round, by that service-performing monk a share of excellent food should be given.
>
> For the monk who is a practitioner of yoga, by that service-performing monk suitable requisites should be brought—medicines and equipment for sickness. And in whatever place that yoga-practitioner monk dwells, in that place no loud noise should be made. The yoga-practitioner monk should be protected by the service-performing monk. His bedding and seat should be propped up. Excellent foods, pleasing, suitable for the yoga-practitioner's stage, should be offered...
>
> For those monks who are devoted to vast learning, enthusiasm should be given. They too should be protected. For those monks who will be Dharma-preachers, the manner of reception should be performed. Dharma-hearers should be encouraged. The assembly-circle should be prepared. The discourse-circle should be purified...and one should be abundant in applause...
>
> The perception of lordship should not be produced regarding any matter. Even the smallest task should be done according to the intention of the Saṅgha, not according to one's own intention...
>
> That belonging to the Saṅgha should not be mixed with that belonging to the Saṅgha of the Four Quarters. Likewise the mutual non-mixing with stūpa-property is prohibited...
>
> Whoever, O Kāśyapa, is a service-performing monk [and] with angry mind issues a command to virtuous ones worthy of gifts because of [his sense of] lordship—by that unwholesome karma he becomes one bound for hell...
>
> If one who threatens a monk with punishment through fear—by that unwholesome karma, the hell named Many Terrors becomes his place of rebirth...until his body is pierced by thousands, blazing, burning, aflame...
>
> His tongue becomes extended a hundred yojanas; in that tongue-faculty many hundreds of thousands of blazing iron stakes are driven...
>
> Whoever, O Kāśyapa, is a service-performing monk [and] hoards incoming gains belonging to the Saṅgha, does not give them at proper times, gives while having harassed and troubled, and does not give to some—by that unwholesome root, the hungry ghost destiny called 'Shank' [or 'Leg'], made of excrement and clay, becomes his place of rebirth.
>
> There, other hungry ghosts take food and make it disappear from him. He, being harassed, looking at that food with unblinking eyes, afflicted by hunger and thirst, experiences painful feeling, and even for a thousand years there is no obtaining of that food..."

---

## Part XIX: The Saṃgharakṣita Narrative

In the Saṃgharakṣitāvadāna also, harms are stated:²⁹

> "Those beings whom you, Saṃgharakṣita, saw in the form of walls—they were monks. By them, that which belonged to the Saṅgha—the wall—was destroyed by phlegm. By the ripening of that karma, they became wall-shaped.
>
> Those beings whom you saw in the form of pillars—they were monks. By them, a pillar belonging to the Saṅgha was destroyed by mucus. Therefore they became pillar-shaped.
>
> Those beings whom you saw in the forms of trees, leaves, flowers, fruits—they too were monks. By them, trees, leaves, flowers, and fruits belonging to the Saṅgha were consumed for personal enjoyment. Therefore they became tree-, leaf-, flower-, and fruit-shaped.
>
> Those beings whom you saw in the forms of ropes and brooms—they were monks. By them, ropes and brooms belonging to the Saṅgha were used for personal enjoyment. Therefore they became rope-shaped and broom-shaped.
>
> The being whom you saw in the form of a ladle—he was a novice. He was making porridge-scum. Guest monks had arrived. Seen by them, he was asked: 'Novice, will this porridge be for the Saṅgha?' He, with mind overcome by stinginess, said: 'Don't you see the scum being made? The porridge has been drunk.' They, [thinking] 'The time has passed,' became discouraged and departed with dejected faces. By the ripening of that karma, he became ladle-shaped.
>
> The being whom you saw in the form of a mortar—he too was a monk. Work [grinding] was due for his bowl. And there a single novice, an arhat, was appointed to the grinding of beans. He said to him: 'Novice, give me a little of the chaff for grinding.' He said: 'Venerable one, wait a moment. I am busy. Afterward, having ground it, I will give it.' He, having become angry, said: 'Novice, if I had the power to strike, I would put you in a mortar and pound you—let alone the chaff!'
>
> That novice reflected: 'This one is overcome by intense mental disturbance. If I give him a reply, he will become angry to an even greater degree.' So he remained silent.
>
> When the disturbance had passed, he approached and said: 'Venerable one, do you know who I am?' He said: 'I know you to be a novice who went forth under Kāśyapa the Perfectly Enlightened One. I too am an elder monk.'
>
> The novice said: 'Even though that is so, nevertheless what was to be done by me, having gone forth, has been done. What has been done? The abandonment of afflictions. I, with all bonds cut, am released from all bonds. Harsh speech has been uttered by you. Acknowledge the transgression as a transgression. Perhaps this karma may go to diminution, thinness, and exhaustion.'
>
> By him the transgression was not acknowledged as a transgression. By that karma, he became mortar-shaped."

And so forth through the various forms: pot-shaped, split down the middle being carried by a thread, and others.³⁰

---

*Thus ends the Third Chapter, Protection of Dharma-Preachers and Others, in the Śikṣāsamuccaya.*

---

## Footnotes

¹ **[Structural]** Chapter 3 begins to elaborate the specifics of protection (*rakṣā*) introduced generally in Chapter 2.

² **[Kārikā]** Root verse 7 defines protection of one's person as the avoidance of harm (*anarthavivarjana*).

³ **[Philosophical]** Two aspects: (1) avoiding harm to oneself, (2) ensuring one does not harm others. Protection is relational.

⁴ **[Source]** *Āryagaganagañjasūtra*: "Sūtra of the Noble Gaganagañja" (Space-Treasury). These verses constitute a collective vow by bodhisattvas for the age after the Buddha's parinirvāṇa.

⁵ **[Source]** *Āryasaddharmapuṇḍarīka*: The Lotus Sūtra. This passage concerns the "safe way of living" (*ācāragocara*) for Dharma-preachers.

⁶ **[Source]** *Prajñāpāramitā*: The Perfection of Wisdom literature, specifically passages on Māra's interference.

⁷ **[Key Concept]** *Mārakarma*: The works or deeds of Māra—any obstruction to the Dharma-path, whether external opposition or internal discord.

⁸ **[Source]** *Āryagaganagañjasūtra* again, defining Māra's work broadly.

⁹ **[Key Concept]** *Mārāṅkuśa*: "Māra-hooks"—ten specific traps that ensnare the bodhisattva. The seventh concerns misuse of forest-dwelling; the tenth, false spiritual friends; the eleventh, association with those who have fallen.

¹⁰ **[Source]** *Ratnamegha*: "Cloud of Jewels Sūtra"—on the proper mode of vigor that does not lead to exhaustion.

¹¹ **[Philosophical]** *Akālapratikāṅkṣaṇatā*: Expecting [results] before the proper time is Māra's work—impatience undermines practice.

¹² **[Philosophical]** The paradox: one must practice giving of the body gradually through habituation, or one will never be able to give it when truly called upon.

¹³ **[Sanskrit]** *Piśitāśana*: Flesh-eaters, i.e., carnivorous demons or beings who would consume the bodhisattva's given body destructively.

¹⁴ **[Verse]** The bodhisattva's body is like a medicine tree whose seed should be protected—if harvested too early or by wrong agents, the tree (awakening) is lost.

¹⁵ **[Source]** *Ratnameghasūtra*: Method for avoiding Māra's work through avoidance of wrong conditions.

¹⁶ **[Key Concept]** *Akalyāṇamitralakṣaṇa*: The characteristics of an "unwholesome friend" (*pāpamitra*)—those who divert from the path through wrong views, conduct, or livelihood.

¹⁷ **[Key Concept]** *Bodhicittasaṃpramoṣa*: The confusion or theft of bodhicitta—when the awakening mind becomes obscured or lost.

¹⁸ **[Source]** *Ratnakūṭa*: The "Heap of Jewels" collection contains both the causes of confusion and the method of avoidance.

¹⁹ **[Source]** *Siṃhaparipṛcchā*: "The Lion's Questions"—on the benefits of never obstructing the gift of Dharma.

²⁰ **[Source]** *Āryamañjuśrībuddhakṣetraguṇavyūhālaṃkārasūtra*: Four dharmas ensuring the bodhisattva does not fall from vows.

²¹ **[Philosophical]** *Bodhicittaparikarman*: Mental cultivation of bodhicitta in all postures—the clearest cause of non-confusion.

²² **[Source]** *Candrapradīpasūtra*: "Moon-Lamp Sūtra"—the mind becomes inclined toward what it repeatedly thinks.

²³ **[Key Concept]** *Avasāda*: Discouragement, depression, loss of heart—a harm because it abandons the path.

²⁴ **[Key Concept]** *Skhalita*: Stumbling or error—four types involving mismatched teaching to students.

²⁵ **[Key Concept]** *Anadhimukti*: Lack of aspiration or faith—a fundamental harm leading to lower rebirths.

²⁶ **[Philosophical]** The remedy: take the Tathāgata as witness for what one does not understand, without rejecting it.

²⁷ **[Key Concept]** *Vaiyāvṛtyakuśala*: Skill in service—one serving the Saṅgha must know how to avoid harm while performing duties.

²⁸ **[Source]** *Ratnarāśisūtra*: Extensive guidelines for the service-performing monk, protecting forest-dwellers, yoga-practitioners, and Dharma-preachers.

²⁹ **[Source]** *Saṃgharakṣitāvadāna*: Narrative of Saṃgharakṣita seeing monks reborn as objects due to misuse of Saṅgha property.

³⁰ **[Philosophical]** These vivid examples illustrate karmic consequences of even small violations against communal property—a strong deterrent.

---

## Key Concepts

- **Anarthavivarjana** (अनर्थविवर्जन): Avoidance of harm
- **Mārakarma** (मारकर्म): Works/deeds of Māra
- **Mārāṅkuśa** (मारांकुश): Māra-hooks (ten traps)
- **Bodhicittasaṃpramoṣa** (बोधिचित्तसंप्रमोष): Confusion/theft of bodhicitta
- **Avasāda** (अवसाद): Discouragement
- **Skhalita** (स्खलित): Stumbling, error
- **Vaiyāvṛtya** (वैयावृत्य): Service (to the Saṅgha)
- **Pāpamitra** (पापमित्र): Evil friend
- **Akālaparibhoga** (अकालपरिभोग): Untimely consumption

---

## Soul-Check Hints for Blues Version

- **"Māra's work" is everywhere**: Discord between teacher and student, impatience, wrong associations, discouragement
- **The Gaganagañja vow is powerful**: Bodhisattvas promise to withstand abuse, hire assassins, kings seizing them
- **Ten Māra-hooks**: Forest-dwelling laziness, false friends urging quick nirvāṇa, association with the fallen
- **The mortar-story is vivid**: A monk who spoke rashly to an arhat-novice became a mortar—acknowledge your transgressions!
- **Discouragement is harm**: Don't think "I can't do this"—think "All buddhas did it this way"

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-4'></div>

# Chapter 4: Avoidance of Harm (Anarthavarjana)

---

*The fourth chapter of Śāntideva's Compendium of Training, on the great harms enumerated in the sūtras—root offenses, karmic consequences, the dangers of sensual desire, and the rejection of true Dharma.*

---

## Introduction: The Great Harms

Other great harms also are stated in the sūtrāntas.¹

---

## Part I: The Root Offenses of Kings

As stated in the Ākāśagarbhasūtra:²

> "There are, O son of good family, these five root offenses (*mūlāpatti*) for a consecrated king (*kṣatriya mūrdhābhiṣikta*), by which root offenses the consecrated king destroys all previously planted roots of virtue, becomes fallen in [his] ground (*vastupatita*), defeated (*parājika*), bound for bad destinies in the sight of all gods and humans.
>
> **First Root Offense:** Whoever, O son of good family, steals stūpa-property, or that belonging to the Saṅgha, or that belonging to the Saṅgha of the Four Quarters, or that which has been donated—whether taking it himself or having it taken.
>
> **Second Root Offense:** Whoever, moreover, rejects the Dharma—whether spoken for the Śrāvaka vehicle, the Pratyekabuddha vehicle, or the Mahāyāna vehicle—rejects and repudiates it.
>
> **Third Root Offense:** Whoever, moreover, [attacks] one who has gone forth in my name, shaven-headed, wearing the dyed robe—whether observing the training or not, whether of bad conduct or virtuous—takes away his dyed robes, or causes them to be taken; or makes him a householder; or strikes his body with weapons; or casts him into prison; or deprives him of life.
>
> **Fourth Root Offense:** Whoever, moreover, a king, deliberately deprives his mother of life, or his father, or an arhat, or a Blessed One's disciple; or splits a harmonious Saṅgha; or, with malicious mind, deliberately sheds the blood of a Tathāgata, Arhat, Samyaksaṃbuddha. By any of these five immediate [heinous deeds], he produces one or another action.
>
> **Fifth Root Offense:** Whoever, moreover, a king, becomes a proponent of no-cause (*ahetuvādin*), disregards the other world, takes up the ten unwholesome paths of action and establishes himself in them, and establishes many other beings in the ten unwholesome paths of action, leads them, settles them, fixes them.
>
> **Sixth Root Offense:** (abbreviated) Whoever causes the breaking of villages, regions, cities, or kingdoms..."

---

## Part II: The Eight Root Offenses for Beginners

> "For beginner bodhisattvas (*ādikarmiṇa*) who have set out in the Mahāyāna, there are eight root offenses, by which root offenses, stumbling, the beginner bodhisattvas destroy all previously planted roots of virtue. Fallen in ground, defeated, fallen from the happiness of gods, humans, and the Mahāyāna, bound for bad destinies, they sink long in saṃsāra, bereft of spiritual friends.
>
> **First Root Offense for Beginners:** Those beings who, due to formerly performed bad actions, are reborn in this defiled world of five corruptions—they, having little roots of virtue, relying on a spiritual friend, hear this supremely profound Mahāyāna. Although of limited intelligence, they generate the thought [aimed at] supreme perfect enlightenment.
>
> To these beginners, certain bodhisattvas who hear this supremely profound sūtrānta connected with emptiness (*śūnyatāpratisaṃyukta*) proclaim it extensively, just as they heard it, just as they obtained it, with proper wording and meaning, to others of former understanding.
>
> But those who have not exerted themselves, those childish ordinary people, hearing it, become terrified, frightened, extremely frightened. Being frightened, they turn away, and having turned away from the thought [aimed at] supreme perfect enlightenment, they fix their minds on the Śrāvaka vehicle.
>
> This is the first root offense of the beginner bodhisattva, by which root offense that son of good family destroys all previously planted roots of virtue, fallen in ground, defeated, [fallen] from the happiness of heaven and liberation. His bodhicitta is deceived. He becomes bound for bad destinies.
>
> Therefore, by the bodhisattva mahāsattva, first having known the inclinations and latent tendencies of other persons, the Dharma should be taught gradually to beings according to their inclinations, just as one enters the great ocean gradually...
>
> **Second Root Offense:** Furthermore, a beginner bodhisattva says to someone: 'You are not capable of practicing in the six perfections. You are not capable of awakening to supreme perfect enlightenment. Quickly generate the thought [aimed at] the Śrāvaka vehicle or the Pratyekabuddha vehicle. By that you will escape from saṃsāra.'...
>
> **Third Root Offense:** Furthermore, a beginner bodhisattva says to someone: 'What is the use of the Prātimokṣa, of morality well-protected? Quickly generate the thought [aimed at] supreme perfect enlightenment. Study the Mahāyāna. Whatever unwholesome karma has been accumulated by your body, speech, and mind through the condition of afflictions—by that study there is purification without karmic ripening.'...
>
> **Fourth Root Offense:** Furthermore, a beginner bodhisattva says to some: 'Abandon the talk of the Śrāvaka vehicle. Do not hear it, do not study it, do not teach it to others. Conceal the Śrāvaka talk. You will not obtain great fruit from it. You are not capable of making an end of afflictions from it. Have faith in the Mahāyāna talk. Hear, study the Mahāyāna, and teach it to others. By that you will pacify all paths to bad destinies, and quickly awaken to supreme perfect enlightenment.' If they become doers of his words, they would grasp such a view. For both there is a root offense...
>
> **Fifth Root Offense:** Furthermore, beginner bodhisattvas become double-tongued. They display things otherwise. And they study, recite, retain, and teach this Mahāyāna for the sake of fame, reputation, honor, gain, and esteem. They teach to others only what they have merely heard. And they say: 'We are Mahāyānikasno others.' They envy others for the sake of gain and esteem... For the sake of jealousy, they proclaim themselves with higher human dharmas. Then by that ground, fallen, defeated, from the happiness of the Mahāyāna, they incur this great grave offense, by which they become bound for bad destinies...
>
> **Sixth Root Offense:** Furthermore, in the future, householders and renouncers, beginner bodhisattvas, [will come who take] those profound sūtrāntas connected with emptiness, adorned with dhāraṇī, patience, samādhi, and stages—the domain of bodhisattvas who are great learned men, who have exerted themselves—they will retain, study, recite [these sūtrāntas], and, having taught them extensively to others, will proclaim: 'I have awakened to these dharmas by my own understanding. And furthermore, out of compassion I teach them to you. You must practice in such a way that you will become a witness to these profound dharmas. Your knowledge and vision will be just like mine.' But he does not give thus. By mere study I teach such profound dharmas—not through direct realization. For the sake of gain and esteem he sells himself..."

---

## Part III: Remedy for Root Offenses

The escape from these [offenses] is stated in this same sūtra:³

> "If those bodhisattvas, having heard the name of the bodhisattva Ākāśagarbha, should wish to see him, wishing to confess their root offenses from fear of falling into bad destinies—if they should bow to the bodhisattva Ākāśagarbha and recite his name, that son of good family, according to his fortune, will stand before him in his own form, or in the form of a brahmin... or in the form of a girl he will stand before him.
>
> To that beginner bodhisattva he confesses those offenses as they have arisen. He displays the profound skillful means and the practice in the Mahāyāna. He establishes him on the irreversible stage...
>
> If he does not give him a face-to-face vision, then that beginner bodhisattva who is transgressed, rising in the last watch from his seat, standing facing east, should burn incense and invoke the god Aruṇa.
>
> And he should speak thus: 'Aruṇa, Aruṇa, O greatly compassionate, O greatly fortunate one, you who have risen high in Jambudvīpa—cover me with compassion! Quickly awaken the greatly compassionate Ākāśagarbha by my word! In my dream, display to me that means by which I may confess the offense, by which I may obtain skillful wisdom in the noble Mahāyāna.'
>
> At that time, he should go to sleep on his bed. At the very moment Aruṇa rises, here in Jambudvīpa the bodhisattva Ākāśagarbha arrives, in his own form. Standing before that beginner bodhisattva in his dream, he confesses that root offense by the Mahāyāna means. And he displays to him such skillful knowledge that by that skillful means that beginner bodhisattva right there obtains the samādhi called 'non-confusion of bodhicitta,' and becomes firmly established in the Mahāyāna..."

---

## Part IV: Summary Verses on Root Offenses

For the sake of easy grasping and retention of these root offenses, and for the collection of those going together [in theme], summary verses are stated:⁴

> *From stealing from the Three Jewels, the offense of defeat is considered.*
> *From rejecting the true Dharma, the second is declared by the Sage.*
>
> *From stealing the robes of a monk, even of bad conduct, from striking,*
> *From casting into prison, from expelling [from the order]—*
>
> *From committing the five immediates, from grasping wrong view,*
> *Or from breaking villages and so forth—root offense is declared by the Victor.*
>
> *From teaching emptiness to beings of unprepared understanding,*
> *From turning those set out for Buddhahood away from enlightenment—*
>
> *From making them abandon the Prātimokṣa to engage in Mahāyāna,*
> *Or from grasping that the Disciple vehicle does not abandon desire and so forth—*
>
> *From others' grasping, or again from displaying one's own qualities,*
> *From disparaging others for the sake of gain, esteem, and reputation—*
>
> *From falsely saying 'I am one tolerant of the profound,' again,*
> *From having ascetics punished, or from giving from the Three Refuges—*
>
> *Or from taking what is being given, again from abandoning tranquility,*
> *From announcing to reciters the enjoyment of one in retreat—*
>
> *These root offenses are indeed causes of great hells.*
> *Before the noble Ākāśagarbha, standing in dream, they should be confessed.*
>
> *From abandoning bodhicitta, from not giving to a suppliant,*
> *From intense stinginess and greed, from striking beings out of anger—*
>
> *Though being entreated earnestly, one does not forbear toward beings,*
> *Or from following others out of affliction, from praising the semblance of true Dharma.*

---

## Part V: The Teaching on Harming Monastics

In the Āryakṣitigarbhasūtra it is also stated:⁵

> "That monk who has gone forth in my name, O great brahmin, who is of bad conduct and evil practice—whether a chaff-monk, impure, a non-ascetic with the profession of an ascetic, a non-celibate with the profession of a celibate—fallen, defeated, conquered by various afflictions—
>
> Yet even now that monk of bad conduct and evil practice is a displayer of spiritual friends, a treasure of merit, for all gods up to all humans. Even though he has become unworthy, yet by virtue of his shaven head and the donning of the dyed robe, by the mere seeing of his deportment, he becomes a propper of various roots of virtue for many beings, a shower of the path to good destinies.
>
> Therefore, for one who has gone forth in my name, whether virtuous or of bad conduct, I do not permit even wheel-turning kings—let alone by unjust means—to give a blow of punishment to the body of one gone forth in my name with a stick; or to cast him into prison; or to make a cutting or breaking of limbs; or to deprive him of life.
>
> Even though he is called 'dead' in this Dharma-Vinaya, yet that person is like yellow orpiment or musk..."

---

## Part VI: Karmic Consequences of the Ten Unwholesome Paths

The ten unwholesome paths of action are to be seen as harms with bitter ripening, from the Saddharmasmṛtyupasthāna.⁶ A small portion of the sūtra is indicated:

### Killing

> "Just as there are birds called Fire-Crest-Walkers who dwell in the midst of flame-crests and are not burned, and, being excited, having broken the skulls of hell-beings, drink their blood. So also the birds called Skull-Interior-Walkers, having broken the head, drink the ignited brain-matter. So the birds called Tongue-Flesh-Eaters, having split the tongue, eat it on all sides. And that tongue, eaten, is born again, more tender than a lotus petal.
>
> Similarly [there are birds] called Tooth-Pullers, Throat-Pipe-Extractors, Lung-Eaters, Stomach-Contents-Eaters, Spleen-Turners, Intestine-Cavity-Gnawers, Spine-Walkers, and the birds called Marma-Seekers, who, having broken all the marma-cavities, having cut the marmas, entering the cavities, drink the marrow-essence of those crying out...
>
> Thus the place of Avīci [extends] 300,000 yojanas. This portion [of Avīci] is called 'Devoured by Birds.' There, with other hell-beings, for many hundreds of thousands of years, he is eaten and is [reborn] again.
>
> Somehow released from all that, wrapped in the net of suffering, he goes to the second place called 'Dog-Pit-Fall,' seeking protection, seeking refuge, seeking safety... Surrounded on all sides by eleven flame-masses, without companion, bound by the bonds of the karma-noose, surrounded on all sides by enemies, having entered a wilderness, having entered a calamity more than all the heaps of hell, he runs to that place called 'Dog-Pit-Fall.'
>
> When he falls, his foot melts utterly. Thrown up, he is reborn, more tender, more smooth. Overcome by harsh, intense feelings... For many hundreds of thousands of years, falling and being thrown up, [he is thus]... Eaten by vultures, crows, hawks, and owls...
>
> Somehow released from that... he runs to the place called 'Wheel-Mark-Cavity.' There, wheels with a thousand spokes appear, with diamond hubs, with sharp flames, spinning swiftly. Just as he arrives, those wheels, reaching his body, spin... burning, crushing each limb separately. And on the sole of his foot, stakes pierce him..."

### Stealing

> "That evil-action-doer, like a firebrand-circle, like a gandharva city, like a mirage, sees a great mass of wealth—jewels, clothes, grain, heaps. For him, overcome by greed, deluded by karma, the thought arises: 'This is mine.' Thus deluded, that evil-doer, having leapt across burning coal-pits, runs after that treasure. He is seized by karma-created Yama-men. Entering the midst of a weapon-net, he is split limb by limb, quartered, burned, reduced to bones. And even in that state, his greed, operating since beginningless time, does not diminish..."

### Sexual Misconduct

> "That evil-doer, somehow released from the weapon-crisis, somehow having leapt across the coal-pits, deluded by karma, reaches another place called 'False Vision.' There he sees a karma-created woman, whom he saw before when his memory was lost. Having seen her, the fire of desire, practiced since beginningless time, arises. He runs toward those women. And those women are made of iron, karma-created. By them he is seized. Having seized him, from lips onward he is so consumed that not even a mustard-seed measure remains of his body. He is reborn again, consumed again...
>
> Yet from that craving-fire he does not turn back. He rushes again toward those women... Then those women, with blazing iron-vajra bodies, taking that man, with bodies full of flame-garlands, grind that hell-being like a fistful of sand. He is reborn again..."

> *Women are the root of bad destinies, of the destruction of wealth in every way.*
> *Those men who are subject to women—how can there be happiness for them?*
>
> *Woman is destruction among destructions, in this world and the next.*
> *Therefore, women should be avoided if one wishes happiness for oneself.*

### Lying

> "He is seized by those Yama-men. Having seized him, they split open that mouth, extract the tongue. And that tongue, by the force of karma, becomes 500 yojanas in measure. At the time of its coming forth, those Yama-men throw him onto a burning iron ground. And 1,000 burning ploughs with blazing tips appear, karma-created. Powerful bullocks carry those ploughs on his tongue, entering into it. There rivers of pus, blood, and worms flow..."

### Slander

> "Just as for lying, with this difference: the tongue is 300 yojanas. Those Yama-men, taking sharp blazing swords, cut the tongue. In another place he is eaten by jackals. He experiences extremely bitter feeling. He cries out, screams, [makes] inarticulate sounds, being without a tongue..."

### Harsh Speech

> "They seize that tongue. Having split open the mouth, they take it. Having cut it with sharp-edged weapons, they throw it into his own mouth for food. And he, tormented by hunger, with face emaciated by hunger, eats that very tongue of his, smeared with his own blood and saliva. And that tongue, cut, is reborn again by the force of karma...
>
> The Yama-men speak an instructive verse:
>
> *'From the bow of the tongue, a sharp arrow of words by you*
> *Was seen as "harsh speech"—this is the fruit that has come.'"

### Idle Chatter

> "That burning copper-liquid, licking the tongue, burns it. Having burned the tongue, it burns the throat. Having burned the throat, it burns the heart. Having burned the heart, it burns the intestines. Having burned them, it burns the stomach. Having also burned the stomach, it exits from below.
>
> The Yama-men speak a verse:
>
> *'Unconnected words before and after, meaningless, incoherent,*
> *Unbound—what was spoken by you—this is the fruit that has come.*
>
> *That which is never truly speaking, which is not devoted to study—*
> *That is not seen as a tongue by the wise, but merely a lump of flesh.'"

---

## Part VII: The Dangers of Sensual Desire

All harms have sensual desire as their root; therefore, one should feel disgust toward them.⁷ As stated here:

> "There is, they say, a hell called Fire-Pit. By what karma do beings take rebirth there? By one who is a non-ascetic with the profession of an ascetic, who, having heard the sounds of women's dancing and singing, or of ornaments, with improper attention, with distracted mind, hearing it, has ejaculated impurity laughing, playing, sporting...
>
> There those hell-beings are crushed limb by limb by iron rain, and by coal rain they are cooked, burned...
>
> Thus, from the memory of savoring past sensual pleasures, the hell called Paduma is taught; from the memory of one's own past [pleasures]. There those hell-beings are cooked in cauldrons. They are beaten in troughs with iron pestles..."

---

## Part VIII: The Seven Kinds of Sexual Union

In the Saptamaithunsaṃyuktasūtra it is also stated:⁸

> "Here, O brahmin, a certain one professes himself to be a celibate. He does not engage in intercourse with a woman here. But gazing upon a woman's form with his eye, he sees it, savors it, becomes attached to it, and so remains. This, O brahmin, is called a celibate joined with the dharma of sexual union, not separated.
>
> Similarly, regarding playing, sporting, laughing with a woman—savoring [this], he practices impure celibacy.
>
> Similarly, regarding savoring the private parts of a woman...
>
> Similarly, for one behind a wall or behind a cloth, savoring the sounds of a woman's dancing, singing, and so forth—[this is] joining with sexual union.
>
> Similarly, savoring another's [woman] endowed with the five strands of sensuality...
>
> Similarly, from dedicating celibacy to [rebirth in] a god-realm and so forth—joined with the dharma of sexual union, not separated..."

---

## Part IX: The Kāma-Rejection Teaching

Because these sensual pleasures, even when gone to the domain of memory and aspiration, cause such harm, it is stated in the Kāmāpavādakasūtra:⁹

> "Restrain your mind from sensual pleasures, O monk! This path is fearful, dangerous, thorny, dense, a wrong path, a bad path, a path of feeling, frequented by bad men. This path is not frequented by good men.
>
> Do you not think thus: 'Why are sensual pleasures said by the Blessed One to be of little savor, of much suffering, much affliction, much trouble?' The danger here is greater.
>
> Sensual pleasures, O monks, are a disease, a boil, a dart, a misfortune, the root of misfortune, bait on a fishhook, death. Sensual pleasures are impermanent, empty, of the nature of deceiving falsehood, dream-like. Such are sensual pleasures—merely the prattle of fools...
>
> Just as a trap for deer, just as a net for birds, just as a copper pot for fish, just as glue for monkeys, just as a mass of fire for moths—so are sensual pleasures...
>
> For one going about in the pursuit of sensual pleasures, being turned about in the mouth of lions for a long night—there is no end known. Being turned about in the mouth of slaughterers, cow-eaters—there is no end known. Being turned about in the mouth of frogs and snakes—there is no end known.
>
> Of those long pursuing sensual pleasures, seized as thieves, beheaded—there is no end known. Adulterers, fence-breakers, village-destroyers, region-destroyers... knot-looseners, seized, beheaded—there is no end known.
>
> Suffering—intense, harsh, bitter—has been experienced. Blood has flowed, gushed forth, which is more than the water in the four great oceans...
>
> For this body is of much danger, a collection of bones, bound by sinews, smeared with flesh, wrapped in skin, covered by hide, full of holes, inhabited by hosts of worms, useless for beings, a basis for afflictions and karma...
>
> What is your peace regarding sensual pleasures, O monk? What seduces you? How are you led, infatuated, attached, having attained attachment? When I am fully nirvāṇa-ed, and the true Dharma has disappeared, and you, having pursued sensual pleasures, have gone to ruin—when will you liberate yourself from old age and death?
>
> Enough, O monk! Restrain your mind from sensual pleasures! This is not the time for the pursuit of sensual pleasures. This is the time for the pursuit of Dharma."

---

## Part X: The Harm of Obstructing Bodhisattvas

Another harm is stated in the Praśāntaviniścayaprātihāryasūtra:¹⁰

> "Whoever, O Mañjuśrī, whether son of good family or daughter of good family, should deprive all beings of Jambudvīpa of life and take all their property—or whoever else, O Mañjuśrī, should create an obstacle for even one wholesome thought of a bodhisattva, even for [a being in] animal birth, even for one root of virtue accompanied by a single mouthful of food—this one produces incomparably more evil.
>
> Why is that? He becomes an obstructor of roots of virtue that generate the arising of Buddhas.
>
> Whoever, O Mañjuśrī, should create jealousy and stinginess toward a bodhisattva in other families—for him, at that time, from that very cause, three fears are to be expected: fear of rebirth in hell, fear of birth blind, fear of rebirth in border regions..."

> *"With a great self-body, in the hells, that evil-minded one,*
> *Ruined in self, is reborn; he experiences painful feeling.*
>
> *500 yojanas is his height when born.*
> *Surrounded by krores, he is eaten constantly by dogs.*
>
> *5,000 heads are born to this slanderer.*
> *And 500 tongues are born on each head.*
>
> *On each tongue, 500 flame-mouthed [devices]*
> *Carry ploughs for him, having spoken evil speech.*
>
> *In Pratāpana [hell] they are cooked, in intense suffering and fire,*
> *Those who create oppression for bodhisattvas, the unassociated.*
>
> *Animal birth with hell—for them it is not hard to obtain!*
> *For 100,000 koṭis of kalpas, hundreds and niyutas—*
>
> *Fallen from there, they become snakes of fierce poison, terrifying.*
> *Overcome by hunger and thirst, they do dreadful deeds.*
>
> *Having obtained even food and drink, they never attain satisfaction.*
>
> *Fallen from there, if reborn among humans,*
> *Born blind, of poor understanding, of evil mind, unrestrained.*
>
> *Having spoken words displeasing and harsh to the noble ones,*
> *Fallen from humans also, again he goes to bad destinies.*
>
> *For 100,000 koṭis of kalpas, born, he does not see the Buddha."*

---

## Part XI: Merit of Protecting Bodhisattvas

The ripening of honoring [bodhisattvas] is stated in the Praśāntaviniścayaprātihāryasūtra:¹¹

> *"Whoever creates righteous protection for these speakers of Dharma,*
> *Abandoning all bad destinies, he becomes Śakra, king of gods.*
>
> *Brahmā too, [lord of] Yāma, Tuṣita, and Vaśavartī, again and again.*
>
> *Reborn among humans, he is born a wheel-turning [monarch].*
> *A merchant, a householder too, he becomes wealthy, of great wealth.*
>
> *Endowed with wisdom and memory, happy, free from affliction."*

---

## Part XII: The Śūraṅgamasamādhi Teaching

As stated in the Śūraṅgamasamādhisūtra:¹²

> "What, O Dṛḍhamati, is the prediction of one whose bodhicitta has not been generated?
>
> Here, O Dṛḍhamati, that person is reborn in the five-fold saṃsāra—whether in hells, in animal birth, in the world of Yama, among gods, or among humans. And that person is of sharp faculties, of exalted aspiration.
>
> The Tathāgata knows him: 'This man-person, after so-many 100,000 niyutas of kalpa-koṭis, will generate the thought [aimed at] supreme perfect enlightenment. And after so-many 100,000 asaṃkhyeya kalpas, he will awaken to enlightenment.' This, O Dṛḍhamati, is called the prediction of the bodhisattva whose bodhicitta has not been generated.
>
> Then the venerable Mahākāśyapa said to the Blessed One: 'From today onward, O Blessed One, we should generate the perception of Teacher toward all beings. Why is that? For this knowledge does not operate for us: which bodhisattva has faculties that ripen enlightenment? Which does not? Therefore, O Blessed One, not knowing, we might generate the perception of [them being] inferior toward such persons. By that we would be destroyed.'
>
> The Blessed One said: 'Well said, well said, Kāśyapa! This word of yours is well-spoken. And seeing this very point, Kāśyapa, this Dharma has been taught by me to you: O monks, a person should not discriminate through another person. For a person discriminating through a person is quickly destroyed. Either I would discriminate a person, or one who would be like me.
>
> By this teaching, Kāśyapa, by a bodhisattva or a śrāvaka, the perception of Teacher should be generated toward all beings... For there might be some person of the bodhisattva vehicle. Therefore, the self should be protected in that matter.' And [toward one] who certainly has the mark of attaining enlightenment, all the more should disdain be guarded against."

---

## Part XIII: The Dharma-Rejection Teaching

Another harm is stated in the Āryasarvadharmavaipulyasaṃgrahasūtra:¹³

> "Subtle, O Mañjuśrī, is the karma-obstruction of rejecting the true Dharma.
>
> Whoever, O Mañjuśrī, in a dharma spoken by the Tathāgata, makes the perception 'this is beautiful' for some, and 'this is not beautiful' for some—he rejects the true Dharma. By him rejecting the true Dharma, the Tathāgata is falsely accused, the Dharma is rejected, the Saṅgha is slandered.
>
> One who says 'this is proper, this is improper'—he rejects the true Dharma...
>
> I have not taught any separate dharma—whether connected with the Śrāvaka vehicle, the Pratyekabuddha vehicle, or the Mahāyāna. Those confused men will make this dharma of mine diverse: 'This was taught for Śrāvakas, this for Pratyekabuddhas, this for bodhisattvas.'
>
> By the perception of diversity, he rejects the true Dharma—'this is the training of the bodhisattva, this is not the training of the bodhisattva'—thus he rejects the true Dharma.
>
> 'The Dharma-preacher has eloquence; he has no eloquence'—thus he rejects the true Dharma.
>
> He speaks the Dharma in terms of [mere] dharma—thus he rejects the true Dharma.
>
> 'When the arising of a Buddha has passed, there is no obtaining of dhāraṇī'—thus he rejects the Dharma.
>
> 'There is no obtaining of dhāraṇī for the Dharma-preacher'—thus he rejects the Dharma.
>
> He finds fault with the practice of the Dharma-preacher—'the Dharma-preacher is not accomplished in practice'—thus he rejects the Dharma.
>
> He accuses him through heedlessness, through deportment, through conduct with letters, through moral failure—thus he rejects the Dharma...
>
> Whoever, O Mañjuśrī, whether monk, nun, layman, or laywoman, thinks 'this is the form of the Dharma-preacher,' speaks it—all of these reject the true Dharma..."

---

*Thus ends the Fourth Chapter, Avoidance of Harm, in the Śikṣāsamuccaya.*

---

## Footnotes

¹ **[Structural]** Chapter 4 begins the enumeration of specific harms (*anartha*) to be avoided, expanding from the general protection discussed in Chapter 3.

² **[Source]** *Ākāśagarbhasūtra*: "Sūtra of Space-Treasury"—enumerates root offenses for kings (5+) and beginners (8).

³ **[Sanskrit]** *Niḥsaraṇa*: Escape, way out—the remedy for root offenses through confession to Ākāśagarbha Bodhisattva.

⁴ **[Verse]** Summary verses (*saṃgrahakārikā*) in śloka meter for memorization.

⁵ **[Source]** *Kṣitigarbhasūtra*: Even an unworthy monk in dyed robes should not be harmed—his visible renunciation plants merit in viewers.

⁶ **[Source]** *Saddharmasmṛtyupasthāna*: "Foundations of Mindfulness of True Dharma"—extensive hell descriptions by karmic cause.

⁷ **[Key Concept]** *Kāmamūla*: All harms have sensual desire as their root—the logic for the extended anti-kāma passages.

⁸ **[Source]** *Saptamaithunsaṃyuktasūtra*: Seven ways celibacy can be joined with sexual dharma—extending beyond physical intercourse to gazing, playing, remembering, savoring sounds, etc.

⁹ **[Source]** *Kāmāpavādakasūtra*: "Sūtra Rejecting Sensual Pleasures"—vivid similes (trap, net, fishhook, glue, fire) and extended condemnation.

¹⁰ **[Source]** *Praśāntaviniścayaprātihāryasūtra*: Creating even one obstacle for a bodhisattva's merit is worse than killing all beings of Jambudvīpa.

¹¹ **[Contrast]** Protecting Dharma-speakers leads to rebirth as Śakra, Brahmā, wheel-turning monarch, or wealthy householder.

¹² **[Source]** *Śūraṅgamasamādhisūtra*: Even unawakened persons may be future bodhisattvas—treat all with teacher-perception.

¹³ **[Key Concept]** *Saddharmpratikṣepa*: Rejecting true Dharma—subtle, including saying "this is proper, this improper" about different Buddhist teachings.

---

## Key Concepts

- **Mūlāpatti** (मूलापत्ति): Root offense, fundamental transgression
- **Vastupatita** (वस्तुपतित): Fallen in ground, defeated
- **Parājika** (पाराजिक): Defeat, expulsion-worthy offense
- **Ānantarya** (आनन्तर्य): Immediate [heinous deed]—5 actions with immediate hell-rebirth
- **Kāmamūla** (काममूल): Sensual desire as root of all harm
- **Saddharmpratikṣepa** (सद्धर्मप्रतिक्षेप): Rejection of true Dharma

---

## Soul-Check Hints for Blues Version

- **Root offenses are specific and enumerated**—kings and beginners have different lists
- **The hell descriptions are graphic and detailed**—Fire-Crest birds, Skull-Interior birds, tongue-extraction
- **Sensual desire is the root of all harms**—the extended kāma-rejection passages are central
- **Even disdaining a future bodhisattva is catastrophic**—treat all as potential teachers
- **Dharma-rejection is subtle**—saying "this teaching is good, that is bad" is rejection

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-5'></div>

# Chapter 5: Avoidance of Harm in the Perfection of Morality (Śīlapāramitāyām Anarthavarjana)

---

*The fifth chapter of Śāntideva's Compendium of Training, on how to avoid harm within the context of moral perfection—through vows, reverence for bodhisattvas, avoidance of gain-seeking, and renunciation of fruitless activities.*

---

## Introduction

Harm has been stated in brief [in the previous chapter].¹ Its avoidance is as follows.

---

## Part I: The Vows of Harm-Avoidance

As stated in the Adhyāśayasaṃcodanasūtra:²

> "Beginner bodhisattvas, fearing the hearing of such harms, should do just as they have undertaken. For thus have they spoken:
>
> 'We, O Blessed One, from this day forward, make the following undertaking before the Tathāgata:
>
> **Vow 1:** If we, from this day forward, should accuse a bodhisattva-vehicle person—whether householder or renunciant—of an offense, whether truly or falsely, we would deceive the Tathāgata, Arhat, Samyaksaṃbuddha.
>
> **Vow 2:** If we, from this day forward, should disdain a bodhisattva-vehicle person and speak ill of him, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 3:** If we, seeing a bodhisattva-vehicle person—whether householder or renunciant—playing with, enjoying the five strands of sensual pleasure, should generate disaffection, or generate vexation of mind, or generate disrespect, or not generate the perception of Teacher toward him, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 4:** If we, from this day forward, due to patronage-families or alms-families, should create bodily or mental affliction for bodhisattva-vehicle persons, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 5:** If we, from this day forward, seeing a bodhisattva-vehicle person, should address him with even one unpleasant word, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 6:** If we, from this day forward, should not bow to a bodhisattva-vehicle person three times at night and three times during the day, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 7:** If we, from this day forward, should not give up kingdom, wealth, or body and life for the sake of this vow-undertaking, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 8:** If we, from this day forward, should disdain a Śrāvaka-vehicle or Pratyekabuddha-vehicle person [thinking] "we are more distinguished, not they," we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 9:** If we, O Blessed One, should not dwell with lowly minds, with minds like outcastes, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 10:** If we, from this day forward, should exalt ourselves or disparage others, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 11:** If we, from this day forward, should not flee a yojana or a hundred yojanas from fear of hatred and conflict, being driven, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 12:** If we, from this day forward, should proclaim ourselves as moral, or as learned, or as endowed with ascetic practices, or should display ourselves with one or another quality, we would deceive the Tathāgata.
>
> **Vow 13:** If we, from this day forward, should not dwell with concealed good and revealed evil, we would deceive the Tathāgata, Arhat, Samyaksaṃbuddha...'"
>
> There the Blessed One addressed Maitreya the bodhisattva mahāsattva: "By a son of good family or daughter of good family wishing to exhaust karma-obstruction, O Maitreya, such an undertaking should be made, just as it has been made by these sons of good family."

---

## Part II: Reverence for All Bodhisattvas

In the Sarvadharmāpravṛttinirdeśa it is also stated:³

> *Three times at night and three times during the day,*
> *he should bow to bodhisattvas with his head.*
>
> *He should not search for any fault of theirs;*
> *let them practice their conduct as they wish.*
>
> *When he sees [one] delighting in sensual pleasures,*
> *he should not search for any fault of his.*
>
> *With infinite qualities, that excellent bodhisattva practice—*
> *even this one, in time, will touch it.*
>
> *By gradual reasoning, by gradual action,*
> *one does not become a Victor by a single word.*
>
> *Many koṭis and niyutas of kalpas—*
> *he who has set out with armor does not become otherwise.*

And here it is also stated:

> "Those sons of good family who are free from such karma-obstruction should not find fault with any conduct of another bodhisattva. All his actions should be released. The thought should be generated thus: 'I do not know another's mind. The conduct of beings is hard to know.'
>
> And seeing this very point, the Tathāgata teaches this Dharma: 'A person should not be measured by [another] person. Either I would measure a person, or one who would be like me.'
>
> By one, O son of good family, wishing to protect himself, no one's conduct should be discriminated. No disparagement of others should be made: 'This one is like this, that one is like that.' One should be devoted to the Buddha-dharma, day and night, with a mind grasping the Dharma."

---

## Part III: The Kṣitigarbha Vows

In the Kṣitigarbhasūtra too it is stated:⁴

> Then at that very time, many hundreds of thousands of learned beings, rising from their seats, joining palms toward the Blessed One, spoke thus:
>
> "We, O Venerable Blessed One, make this aspiration before the Blessed One:
>
> 'For as long as we wander in saṃsāra—having obtained even a little patience—may we NOT obtain the station of a king, may we NOT obtain the station of a minister, may we NOT obtain the station of a town-elder, may we NOT obtain the station of a village-elder, may we NOT obtain the station of a regional-elder, may we NOT obtain the station of a chief-priest, may we NOT obtain the station of a general... may we NOT obtain any station as a leader over beings—until we have obtained patience.
>
> For from such [stations], we might create such extremely heinous karma as to damage the teaching of the Buddhas...'"

---

## Part IV: The Candrapradīpa Verses

In the Candrapradīpasūtra too, avoidance of harm is stated:⁵

> *"There is no evil that will not be done by them, O Kumāra, in the future evil time.*
> *Do not have intimacy with them, O Kāli, in the last [age].*
>
> *If you should speak or converse, you would create disrespect toward them.*
> *Unshrunken, you should honor [them], due to their not-yet-awakening.*
>
> *Having asked about the number of rains [ordination seniority], whoever is your elder—*
> *create respect there, bowing at his feet with your head.*
>
> *Do not see any fault in those who behold the seat of enlightenment.*
> *Do not generate opposition; always be of loving mind.*
>
> *If you see a fault in them, do not proclaim their faults.*
> *Whatever karma one does, such fruit one will obtain.*
>
> *With a smiling moon-face, toward elders and newcomers,*
> *be always the first to speak, with pride destroyed, patient.*
>
> *With robes and alms-food, make assistance for them.*
> *Thus place your mind: "All will become Leaders."'"

---

## Part V: Praise of Bodhicitta

For one in whom reverence and faith do not arise toward one who has generated bodhicitta, for the sake of protecting oneself from falling into bad destinies, for the sake of experiencing joy in seen and unseen [matters], for the sake of clarifying one's own murky mind, for the sake of obtaining pliancy and workability of mind—one should meditate on the qualities of one who has generated bodhicitta, as the noble Maitreya displayed to the noble Sudhana in the Āryagaṇḍavyūha:⁶

> *He, having seen beings afflicted by old age and disease,*
> *assailed by hundreds of sufferings,*
> *tormented by the fear and grief of birth and death—*
> *for their sake he practices, with a mind of compassion.*
>
> *Having seen the world tormented by the machine of suffering*
> *in the wheel-turn of the five destinies,*
> *he [seeks] that firm knowledge-vajra,*
> *the shattering of the machine-wheel of suffering.*
>
> *Having seen the field of beings with the thorns, splinters, and prickles of desire and hatred,*
> *entangled in the dense undergrowth of views and attachments,*
> *seeking to purify the field of beings,*
> *he firmly seeks the plough of wisdom.*
>
> *The world, with its jungle of delusion and ignorance,*
> *with wisdom-eye destroyed, without a guide—*
> *He will become a lord, a guide showing safety,*
> *a caravan-leader for the world.*
>
> *Wearing the armor of patience, mounted on the three liberations,*
> *with the sword of knowledge, vanquishing the enemy afflictions,*
> *having become a hero, a giver of fearlessness—*
> *he will become a guide for the world.*
>
> *He launches the Dharma-boat,*
> *well-trained in the path of the knowledge-ocean,*
> *a leader to the peace-jewel's excellent island—*
> *he is a helmsman in the triple ocean.*
>
> *He, with the disk of knowledge-rays and aspiration,*
> *illuminating all being-worlds,*
> *rising from the ocean of the dharma-realm sky—*
> *he is proclaimed as the Buddha-sun.*
>
> *Cool as sandalwood of loving-kindness,*
> *of equal, benevolent mind toward all beings,*
> *with the full disk of white dharmas—*
> *he is proclaimed as the Buddha-moon.*
>
> *Established on a firm foundation of aspiration,*
> *having gradually risen in bodhisattva practice,*
> *a mine of all dharma-jewels—*
> *he will become the supreme knowledge-ocean.*
>
> *Born from the nāga-king of bodhicitta,*
> *risen in the sky of the dharma-realm,*
> *raining Dharma-clouds simultaneously,*
> *increasing all white-fruit harvests.*
>
> *The lamp-wick of faith, destroying the darkness of the three stains,*
> *a firm vessel of loving-kindness, oil, and mindfulness,*
> *bright with the stainless fire of bodhicitta—*
> *he will light the Dharma-lamp.*
>
> *Bodhicitta is the embryonic mass, compassion is the membrane,*
> *loving-kindness is the flesh, [with] immovable, firm aspiration,*
> *limbs of awakening gradually arising—*
> *this Buddha-embryo increases.*
>
> *He will increase the womb of merit,*
> *he will purify the womb of wisdom,*
> *he is proclaimed as the womb of knowledge—*
> *one born from the womb of aspiration.*
>
> *Such ones, armored with compassion and loving-kindness,*
> *with minds intent on liberating beings, with benevolent intent—*
> *rare in the world with its gods and humans—*
> *such as this one, with purified mind.*
>
> *Established in such a root of aspiration,*
> *increased in such firm exertion,*
> *such a one, with light covering the three realms—*
> *the knowledge-tree bearing fruit is extremely rare.*
>
> *He, seeking the arising of all qualities,*
> *seeking to question all dharmas,*
> *seeking to split all doubts—*
> *he serves all friends, untiring.*
>
> *He is the subduer of Māra, the Kali-age [destroyer], and afflictions,*
> *he is the purifier of the stains of views and craving,*
> *he is the one intent on liberating the entire world—*
> *he is always your distinguished sage.*
>
> *He will purify the bad destinies,*
> *he will display the path to heaven,*
> *he will lead the world to the path of liberation—*
> *such a one, established in the path of qualities.*
>
> *He is the liberator from suffering in all destinies,*
> *he is the giver of happiness in all destinies,*
> *he is the cutter of the snares of all becomings—*
> *he will become the destroyer of the courses of becoming."*

---

## Part VI: Four Dharmas of Harm-Avoidance

By this meditation, avoidance of harm becomes easy to accomplish.⁷

In the Adhyāśayasaṃcodanasūtra too, avoidance of harm is stated:

> "By four dharmas, O Maitreya, a bodhisattva-vehicle person, in the last five hundred [years] when the true Dharma is being lost, will be liberated unharmed, uninjured, safely. By which four?
>
> 1. By examining one's own faults
> 2. By not accusing other bodhisattva-vehicle persons of offenses
> 3. By not looking at patronage-families and alms-families
> 4. By refraining from heedless speech
>
> By another four:
>
> 1. By avoiding beings of little learning
> 2. By not taking up assemblies [i.e., not building a following]
> 3. By dwelling in border lodging-places
> 4. By being devoted to the practice of self-discipline and tranquility"

---

## Part VII: Dangers of Gain and Honor

And here it is also stated:⁸

> "A beginner bodhisattva who has attained the power of wisdom, O Maitreya, should be one who has avoided gain and honor, seeing the danger in gain and honor.
>
> One should be free from delight in gatherings, seeing the danger in delight in gatherings.
>
> One should be free from delight in talk, seeing the danger in delight in talk.
>
> One should be free from delight in sleep, seeing the danger in delight in sleep.
>
> One should be free from delight in work, seeing the danger in delight in work.
>
> One should be free from delight in elaboration, seeing the danger in delight in elaboration...
>
> Here, O Maitreya, gain and honor should be examined by the bodhisattva mahāsattva as:
>
> - Generating desire (*rāgasaṃjanana*)
> - Destroying mindfulness (*smṛtividhvaṃsanakara*)
> - Causing rising and falling by gain and non-gain (*lābhālābhatayā unnāmāvanāmakara*)
> - Generating delusion (*mohotpādana*)
> - Resolving on stinginess toward families (*kulamātsaryādhyavasāna*)
> - Generating deceit for the sake of accomplishing one's own aims (*ātmārthaniṣpādanatayā śāṭhayotpādana*)
> - Avoiding the four noble lineages, generating shamelessness (*caturāryavaṃśaparivarjanatayā āhīkyānapatrāpyasaṃjanana*)
> - Not approved by all Buddhas (*sarvabuddhānanujñāna*)
> - Generating pride and intoxication (*mānamadotpādana*)
> - Disdaining teachers (*gurūṇāmavamānana*)
> - The side of Māra (*mārapakṣa*)
> - The sole root of heedlessness, stealing roots of virtue (*ekāntapramādamūlaḥ kuśalamūlāpaharaṇa*)
> - Like lightning, a wheel, a thunderbolt (*vidyucakrāśanisadṛśa*)
> - Polluted by many obstacles, looking at patronage-families and alms-families, generating depression (*daurmanasyasaṃjanana*)
> - Confusing the intellect (*buddhivibhrāmaṇa*)
> - Becoming a vessel for grief due to transformation of pleasant things (*priyavastupariṇāmanatayā śokabhājana*)
> - Stealing the four foundations of mindfulness, weakening white dharmas (*catuḥsmṛtyupasthānasaṃbhoṣaṇaḥ śukladharmadurbalīkaraṇa*)
> - Causing loss of the four right exertions (*catuḥsamyakprahāṇaparihāṇakaraṇa*)
> - Causing loss of supernormal powers and higher knowledges (*ṛddhyabhijñāparihāṇakaraṇa*)
> - Creating disrespect after prior respect (*pūrva satkārapaścādasatkārakaraṇa*)
> - Honoring enemies, abandoning friends (*amitrasatkaraṇamitraprahāṇa*)
> - Like a courtesan, through seeking connection with others (*parābhisaṃdhānatayā gaṇikāsadṛśa*)
> - Avoiding dhyānas and immeasurables (*dhyānāpramāṇaparivarjana*)
> - Casting into hell, animal births, and the world of Yama (*narakatiryagyoniyamalokaprapātana*)
> - Like the conduct of Devadatta and Udraka (*devadattodrakasamācāra*)"

---

## Part VIII: The Benefits of Few Desires

> "Such, O Maitreya, are the dangers in gain and honor that should be examined by the bodhisattva. Having examined them, one should delight in few desires (*alpecchatā*), not be tormented.
>
> Why is that? For one of few desires, O Maitreya, such faults do not arise, nor do obstructions to Buddha-dharmas arise.
>
> He is not disgusted with householders and renounciants.
> He is to be protected by gods and humans, established in pure intention.
> He is unafraid of falling into any bad destiny.
> He is unshaken, having transcended threats.
> He is unmoved, freed from the domain of Māra.
> He is unassailable by all calamities.
> He is sought after by gods and humans, established in the practice of dhyāna.
> He is clear, having abandoned deceit and guile.
> He is unheedless, seeing the danger in the five strands of sensual pleasure.
> He is one who practices as he speaks, established in the noble lineage.
> He is sought after by learned fellow-practitioners.
>
> Knowing such benefits, O Maitreya, a wise bodhisattva should delight in few desires with full intention. Few desires should be cultivated for the sake of abandoning all gain and honor."

---

## Part IX: Dangers of Delight in Gatherings

Regarding delight in gatherings:⁹

> *"Having abandoned desire, having abandoned hatred—the yogī does not dwell among gatherings.*
> *He becomes inclined in that direction, bending that way—by this fault one should not delight [in them].*
>
> *Excitement, laughter, and thoughts—all of these arise among gatherings.*
> *Of mixed behavior he becomes, unrestrained, who does gatherings that are without essence.*
>
> *Fools delight in worldly mantras [gossip]; fools diminish in foremost talk [Dharma].*
> *They increase hostility, thoughts of excess—by this fault one does not shine there.*
>
> *The monk does not increase in learning, having generated delight in improper mantras.*
> *Therefore, abandoning improper mantras, may you always find delight in Dharma.*
>
> *Thousands of my own bones have I abandoned, aspiring to enlightenment.*
> *I am not satisfied [even now] hearing the Dharma; they become weary hearing the Dharma.*
>
> *Entirely to be avoided are improper mantras and undisciplined mantras.*
> *Generate delight there in the excellent Dharma, which is hard to obtain in many hundreds of kalpas.*
>
> *For one dwelling in the forest, seeking qualities, the faults of others are not to be observed.*
> *"I am distinguished, I alone am best"—such a thought should not be taken up.*
>
> *This is pride, the root of all heedlessness; inferior monks are not to be disdained.*
> *This is gradual in this teaching; one does not attain enlightenment in a single birth."*

---

## Part X: Dangers of Delight in Talk

Regarding delight in talk:¹⁰

> *"He becomes disrespectful, intoxicated with learning, dwelling in disputatious mantras.*
> *With stolen mindfulness, without clear comprehension—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *He becomes far from inner contemplation; neither mind nor body are peaceful.*
> *He goes through many risings and fallings—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *Destroyed in the mind of true Dharma, the fool becomes very harsh, with unlubricated mind.*
> *Far from insight and calm—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *He becomes always disrespectful to teachers, generating delight in polluting mantras.*
> *Standing in what is without essence, with wisdom destroyed—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *He becomes not honored by hosts of gods, they do not generate aspiration for him.*
> *He becomes deficient in analytical knowledge—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *He is criticized by wise men—whoever is an individual witness.*
> *His life becomes pointless—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *He grieves when the fool does—"I am without practice, what should I do now?"*
> *He becomes very miserable, without obtaining firm ground—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *He becomes unsteady, like a straw stirred [by wind]; that one doubts, no great doubt.*
> *He never has firm understanding—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *Like an actor standing on stage, he speaks of the qualities of other heroes,*
> *but he himself is without practice—these are the faults of one delighting in talk.*
>
> *He becomes deceitful, fickle, hopeless; again and again he resorts to dispute.*
> *He becomes far from the noble Dharma—these are the faults of one delighting in talk...*
>
> *Having delighted in talk for a long time, one does not find the bliss of self-joy here.*
> *Better indeed is contemplation on a single quarter-verse, where one may obtain infinite bliss in [that] quarter-verse.*
>
> *There is nothing whatsoever of essence in the rind of sugarcane; in the middle is that essence, most delightful.*
> *Having eaten the rind, then one cannot here again obtain the superior sugarcane-juice.*
>
> *Just as the rind—so understand talk; just as the juice—so is contemplation on meaning here.*
> *Therefore, having abandoned delight in talk, may you always contemplate meaning, unheeding."*

---

## Part XI: Dangers of Delight in Sleep

Regarding delight in sleep:¹¹

> *"He increases a great net of delusion; he becomes doubtful, grasping views.*
> *Many views arise for him who does not proceed to delight in torpor [but wrongly]...*
>
> *His wisdom becomes very weak, his understanding diminishes and is not his.*
> *He is always diminished in knowledge, who does not proceed to delight in torpor...*
>
> *Lazy, unknowing, slothful, unwise—non-human beings obtain access to him.*
> *They harass him dwelling in the forest, who does not proceed to delight in torpor...*
>
> *Uninterested in wholesome mind always, he has no desire for Dharma.*
> *He becomes more and more desirous of non-dharma, who does not proceed to delight in torpor...*
>
> *Confused, bereft of desire for true Dharma, the fool is diminished in all qualities.*
> *His white [virtue] goes and he attains darkness, who does not proceed to delight in torpor...*
>
> *He becomes lacking in skill, with a withdrawn mind; he never has constant joy.*
> *Overcome by sleep, with slack limbs—who does not proceed to delight in torpor...*
>
> *Having known that the self is reached by laziness, he envies those endowed with the power of vigor.*
> *He speaks ill of those endowed with vigor, who does not proceed to delight in torpor...*
>
> *That which destroys all suffering and darkness, which is the root for avoiding bad destinies,*
> *always praised by all Buddhas—cultivate always that noble vigor."*

---

## Part XII: Dangers of Delight in Work

Regarding delight in work:¹²

> *"He becomes hard to admonish when urged by teachers, he does not take instruction right-handedly.*
> *He quickly becomes one of failed morality—these are the faults of one delighting in work.*
>
> *He is always anxious, always thinking of householder-tasks.*
> *He has nothing to do with dhyāna-abandonment—these are the faults of one delighting in work.*
>
> *Intense desire arises in him for tastes and non-tastes; seized, he is infatuated.*
> *He is not satisfied with one thing or another—these are the faults of one delighting in work.*
>
> *He is pleased with a large assembly; he becomes miserable when deprived of it.*
> *He becomes mixed, like a donkey here—these are the faults of one delighting in work...*
>
> *Day and night, with mind on nothing else, he is constantly [thinking] about food and robes.*
> *He is always interested in his own aims with all qualities—these are the faults of one delighting in work.*
>
> *He asks about worldly tasks and proceeds to delight in improper mantras.*
> *He does not find delight in proper mantras—these are the faults of one delighting in work..."*

Then the bodhisattva mahāsattva Maitreya said to the Blessed One: "Those bodhisattvas, O Blessed One, will be of very little wisdom, of diminished wisdom, who, having avoided foremost dharmas, will take up inferior works."

Thus addressed, the Blessed One said to the bodhisattva mahāsattva Maitreya: "So it is, Maitreya, so it is, as you say... But I tell you, Maitreya, I declare to you: Those bodhisattvas are not gone forth in the Tathāgata's teaching who have no yoga, no dhyāna, no abandonment, no study, no search for learning.

But, O Maitreya, the Tathāgata's teaching is empowered by dhyāna and abandonment, constructed by knowledge, established by knowledge, empowered by application—not empowered by householder-work, service, and drudgery...

This is the work of those unyoked to yoga, who delight in saṃsāra—namely, service, pollution by worldly tasks. The bodhisattva should not generate aspiration for that...

If, O Maitreya, a service-delighting bodhisattva should fill this three-thousand great thousand world-system with stūpas made of the seven jewels, I would not be pleased by him, not honored, not respected...

There, Jambudvīpa should be filled with service-doing bodhisattvas. All of them should do attendance and service for one bodhisattva devoted to recitation and study. And by Jambudvīpa-sized [numbers of] bodhisattvas devoted to recitation and study, attendance and service should be done for one bodhisattva devoted to reflection...

Why is that? This is difficult work—namely, wisdom-work, superior, unsurpassed, distinguished above the whole triple world, transcendent. Therefore, O Maitreya, by a bodhisattva seeking yoga, wishing to undertake vigor, wisdom should be engaged in."

---

## Part XIII: Dangers of Delight in Elaboration

Regarding delight in elaboration (*prapañcārāma*):¹³

> *"The eight unfavorable states (*akṣaṇa*) are not far from him; the excellence of favorable moment (*kṣaṇasaṃpad*) is not supreme for him.*
> *These harms arise for him always—these are the faults of one going about in elaboration...*
>
> *Having properly understood these faults, the wise one should avoid all elaborations.*
> *Harms are easy for one going about in elaboration; therefore, one should not cohabit with elaboration.*
>
> *Go a hundred yojanas—that is better—wherever there is elaboration or conflict.*
> *Do not make a dwelling there, not a residence, not even for a moment where there is affliction.*
>
> *Those who have gone forth are not interested in wealth, they are interested in qualities; do not create conflict with evil minds.*
> *You have no field, no agriculture, no commerce—for whose sake are these elaborations?*
>
> *You have no son, no daughter, no wife; no friend, no band of relatives.*
> *You have no female servants, no male servants, no lordship—do not create conflict, having gone forth.*
>
> *Having taken the dyed robes with faith—they are cultivated by the peaceful and very peaceful.*
> *Be peaceful and very peaceful, tranquil! Having avoided elaboration, generate patience.*
>
> *Guard against poisonous snakes with fierce minds, and hells and animal realms and Yama's domain—*
> *they are not far from one going about in elaboration; therefore, generate vigor in patience...*
>
> *By this yoga one obtains purity, having exhausted karma-obstruction without remainder.*
> *He opposes Māra with army and vehicle—whoever, the wise one, generates patience for that."*

---

## Part XIV: The Root Verse

In brief, avoidance of harm is stated thus:¹⁴

> **By what is this attained? By avoidance of all fruitless activity.** (Kārikā 7)

"By what is this attained?" By avoidance of all fruitless activity (*sarvaniṣphalaspandavarjana*). The fruit here is for the sake of others. Whatever activity does not conduce to that—being fruitless—is to be avoided.

As the Candrapradīpasūtra teaches regarding bodily restraint:

> "He is not one whose hands are restless, not one whose feet are restless; restrained in hands and feet..."

And in the Daśadharmakasūtra too it is taught:

> "Waving the hands, waving the feet, running, running about, jumping, leaping—this is called bodily agitation."

---

## Part XV: All Action for Beings' Sake

In the Āryādharmasaṃgītisūtra, how for bodhisattvas no action other than for others' sake is appropriate, is clearly illuminated:¹⁵

> "Whatever bodily action, O Blessed One, of bodhisattvas; whatever verbal action; whatever mental action—all of that proceeds with observation of beings, with great compassion as chief, with the aim of establishing beings' welfare, proceeding with the intention of welfare and happiness for all beings.
>
> He, with such benevolent intention, with such perception, [thinks]: 'That practice should be practiced by me which brings welfare and happiness to all beings...'
>
> The practice is observation of sense-bases like an empty village. And he does not aspire to abandonment of sense-bases."

In the Āryagaganagañjasūtra it is also stated:¹⁶

> "Just as wind enters through a crack, so from wherever there is a crack in the mind, from there Māra obtains access. Therefore, the bodhisattva should always be of crackless mind. That cracklessness of mind is the fulfillment of the emptiness of all-aspect knowledge."

---

*Thus ends the Fifth Chapter, Avoidance of Harm in the Perfection of Morality, in the Śikṣāsamuccaya.*

---

## Footnotes

¹ **[Structural]** Chapter 5 gives practical guidance on avoiding harm within moral cultivation, complementing the theoretical exposition in Chapter 4.

² **[Source]** *Adhyāśayasaṃcodanasūtra*: "Sūtra of Awakening Higher Intention"—contains the 13 vows of harm-avoidance.

³ **[Source]** *Sarvadharmāpravṛttinirdeśa*: Verses on bowing to bodhisattvas three times daily and not searching for faults.

⁴ **[Source]** *Kṣitigarbhasūtra*: Learned beings vow NOT to attain positions of power until they have patience, fearing they might harm Buddha's teaching.

⁵ **[Source]** *Candrapradīpasūtra*: "Moon-Lamp Sūtra"—verses on respecting ordination seniority and not finding faults.

⁶ **[Source]** *Āryagaṇḍavyūha*: The famous praise of bodhicitta by Maitreya to Sudhana—the Buddha-sun, Buddha-moon, knowledge-tree, dharma-boat, etc.

⁷ **[Key Concept]** Four dharmas for safe liberation: (1) examining own faults, (2) not accusing others, (3) not looking at patron-families, (4) refraining from heedless speech.

⁸ **[Key Concept]** *Lābhasatkāra* (gain and honor): Extensive list of 23+ dangers, culminating in "like the conduct of Devadatta."

⁹ **[Source]** Verses on dangers of *saṃgaṇikārāma* (delight in gatherings): excitement, laughter, thoughts, worldly gossip, diminished Dharma-talk.
