¹⁰ **[Source]** Verses on dangers of *bhāṣyārāma* (delight in talk): 11 faults, ending with "like an actor speaking of others' heroism while himself without practice."

¹¹ **[Source]** Verses on dangers of *nidrārāma* (delight in sleep): delusion-net, weak wisdom, non-human harassment, envy of the vigorous.

¹² **[Source]** *Karmārāma* (delight in work): Maitreya's question and Buddha's answer—"This is the work of those unyoked to yoga."

¹³ **[Source]** *Prapañcārāma* (delight in elaboration): The eight unfavorable states, conflict-avoidance ("Go 100 yojanas!").

¹⁴ **[Kārikā 7]** The root verse: "By avoidance of all fruitless activity"—fruit means benefit to others; everything else is to be avoided.

¹⁵ **[Source]** *Āryādharmasaṃgītisūtra*: All bodhisattva action proceeds with great compassion as chief, aimed at beings' welfare.

¹⁶ **[Source]** *Āryagaganagañjasūtra*: Wind enters through cracks; so Māra enters through mind-cracks. Therefore, cultivate crackless mind through sarvākārajñatā-śūnyatā.

---

## Key Concepts

- **Mūlāpatti-vivarjana** (मूलापत्तिविवर्जन): Avoidance of root offenses (from Chapter 4)
- **Alpecchatā** (अल्पेच्छता): Few desires—the antidote to gain-seeking
- **Lābhasatkāra** (लाभसत्कार): Gain and honor—23+ dangers listed
- **Saṃgaṇikārāma** (संगणिकाराम): Delight in gatherings—to be avoided
- **Bhāṣyārāma** (भाष्याराम): Delight in talk—11 faults
- **Nidrārāma** (निद्राराम): Delight in sleep—wisdom-weakening
- **Karmārāma** (कर्माराम): Delight in work—inferior to wisdom-work
- **Prapañcārāma** (प्रपञ्चाराम): Delight in elaboration—conflict-generating
- **Sarvaniṣphalaspandavarjana** (सर्वनिष्फलस्पन्दवर्जन): Avoidance of all fruitless activity (Kārikā 7)

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-6'></div>

# Chapter 6: Protection of One's Person (Ātmabhāvarakṣā)

---

*The sixth chapter of Śāntideva's Compendium of Training, on how to protect one's person through mindfulness, calm, mind-training, right speech, proper use of food and medicine, and protective mantras.*

---

## Introduction: The Twelve Mindfulnesses

Avoidance of fruitless activity has been stated [in the previous chapters].¹ How is this accomplished? He says:

> **This is accomplished by constant mindfulness.** (Kārikā 8a)

Twelve kinds of mindfulness (*smṛti*) conduce to the avoidance of fruitless activity:

1. **Mindfulness of respecting the ripening consequence of not transgressing the Tathāgata's command**
2. **Mindfulness that the entire body is established in immovable nature**
3. **Mindfulness of steady, deliberate action**—when there is benefit for beings, whatever limb is not useful should be firmly kept still, with one's intention dependent on stronger mindfulness and equanimous aspiration
4. **Mindfulness of the beginner when confused by fear, celebration, etc.**—not letting the limbs move freely
5. **Mindfulness of examining the four postures** (walking, standing, sitting, lying)
6. **Mindfulness of observing excellence in the three postures** for the sake of protecting them intermittently
7. **Mindfulness of restraining**—when speaking, not making excessive non-pleasing gestures of hands, feet, head, and face due to over-excitement, agitation, partiality, etc.
8. **Mindfulness of speaking in a volume** such that the hearer understands the meaning conveyed, not excessively—except when there would be a fault from another's suspicion
9. **Mindfulness of purifying one's own and others' minds** when in crowded gatherings of untrained people
10. **Mindfulness of the mind-elephant**—always bound to the post of calm (*śamatha*)
11. **Mindfulness of repeatedly examining the state of the mind**
12. **Mindfulness of intent**—in large gatherings, even abandoning other tasks, protecting mindfulness as stated

---

## Part I: Mindfulness Arises from Earnestness

Thus, by these mindfulnesses, avoidance of fruitless activity is accomplished. And:²

> *Mindfulness arises from intense earnestness.* (Kārikā 8b)

Therein, earnestness (*ādara*) means complete orientation toward tasks in every way—the antidote to contempt. And this:

> **Earnestness arises from ardor, knowing the greatness of calm.** (Kārikā 8cd)

---

## Part II: What Is Calm?

What is this "calm" (*śama*)? That which is called *śamatha* in the Āryākṣayamatisūtra:³

> "What is the inexhaustible calm? It is:
> - Peace of mind, thorough peace, non-distraction
> - Sense-restraint
> - Non-agitation
> - Non-inflation
> - Non-fickleness
> - Non-restlessness
> - Gentleness
> - Guardedness
> - Workability
> - Thoroughbred-ness
> - One-pointedness
> - Delight in solitude
> - Avoidance of gatherings
> - Joy in seclusion
> - Seclusion of body
> - Non-confusion of mind
> - Mental orientation toward forest
> - Few desires
> - Guarding the postures
> - Knowing the right time
> - Knowing the occasion
> - Knowing the measure
> - Knowing liberation
> - Being easy to support
> - Being easy to maintain..."

---

## Part III: The Greatness of Calm

What is the greatness of this calm? It is **the power to generate knowledge of things as they are**. For:⁴

> *"One who is concentrated knows things as they are,"* said the Sage.

As stated in the Dharmasaṃgīti:

> "For one of concentrated mind, there is seeing of things as they are. For the bodhisattva who sees things as they are, great compassion operates toward beings. And thus for him: 'This samādhi-gate and seeing all dharmas as they are—I must accomplish this for all beings.'
>
> He, urged by that great compassion, fulfilling the training in higher morality, higher mind, and higher wisdom, awakens to the four right complete awakenings. 'Therefore I must be well-established in morality, unshaking, unbent.'
>
> This is the greatness of calm."

Understanding this greatness—the transcendence of one's own and others' endless suffering in bad destinies, the attainment of the summit of endless worldly and supramundane happiness—ardor should be cultivated by desiring it, like one inside a burning house desiring cool water. By that, intense earnestness arises toward the trainings. By that, mindfulness stands firm. With mindfulness firm, one avoids the fruitless. And one who avoids—for that one, harms do not arise.

**Therefore, by one wishing to protect one's person, having traced out the root of mindfulness, one should always be of established mindfulness.**

---

## Part IV: Mindfulness for Householders and Renounciants

Hence in the Ugraparipṛcchā, regarding the householder bodhisattva, it is stated:⁵

> "By him, having refrained from the heedlessness-ground of liquor, wine, and intoxicants, being un-intoxicated, un-mad, non-fickle, non-restless, non-confused, non-chattering, non-inflated, non-agitated, of established mindfulness and clear comprehension..."

And here, regarding the renunciant bodhisattva:

> "Non-distraction in mindfulness and clear comprehension..."

---

## Part V: What Is Right Mindfulness?

Mindfulness is described in the Āryaratnacūḍasūtra:⁶

> "That mindfulness by which the arising of all afflictions does not occur;
> That mindfulness by which one does not give access to all works of Māra;
> That mindfulness by which one does not fall into a wrong path or a bad road;
> That mindfulness which, acting as gatekeeper, does not give opportunity to all unwholesome mental and mental-associated dharmas—
> This is called right mindfulness."

---

## Part VI: What Is Clear Comprehension?

Clear comprehension (*saṃprajanya*) is stated in the Prajñāpāramitā:⁷

> "Walking, he knows 'I am walking.' Standing, he knows 'I am standing.' Lying down, he knows 'I am lying down.' Sitting, he knows 'I am sitting.' However his body is disposed, so he knows it...
>
> He is one who acts with clear comprehension in going forward, in going back, in looking, in bending, in stretching, in wearing the saṃghāṭī, the pātra, the cīvara, in eating, drinking, chewing, in dispelling drowsiness and fatigue, in coming, going, standing, sitting, sleeping, waking, speaking, and being silent, in withdrawal."

---

## Part VII: Morality and Concentration

For morality conduces to concentration. As stated in the Candrapradīpasūtra:⁸

> *Quickly he obtains concentration, unobstructed—these are the benefits of purified morality.*

From this it is understood: Whatever applications are causes of concentration—all of them are included within morality. Therefore, by one desiring concentration, one should have morality of mindfulness and clear comprehension.

Likewise, by one desiring morality too, effort should be made in concentration, due to the statement in that very sūtra. Regarding the benefits of dhyāna:

> *He is not of bad conduct, well-established in [good] conduct.*
> *The yogī ranges in proper pasture, avoiding improper pasture.*
>
> *Dwelling without burning, guarded, with senses restrained.*

---

## Part VIII: Mind-Training Is the Bodhisattva Training

By these two—morality and concentration—mutually increasing each other, the completion of the work of the mind is accomplished. And this is the extent of the bodhisattva training: **mind-training** (*cittaparikarma*), since this is the root of all benefit for beings.⁹

For it is stated in the Āryaratnamegha:

> "All dharmas are preceded by mind. When mind is fully known, all dharmas are fully known."

And:

> *"The world is led by mind; mind does not see mind.*
> *By mind karma is accumulated, whether wholesome or unwholesome.*
>
> *Mind whirls like a firebrand. Mind rushes like a horse.*
> *Mind burns like a forest fire. Mind sweeps away like a great flood."*

Examining thus, he dwells with mindfulness well-established in the mind, not going under the power of mind. Rather, the mind comes under his power. When mind is under his power, all dharmas come under his power.

---

## Part IX: The City of the Mind

In the Āryagaṇḍavyūhasūtra too it is described:¹⁰

> "All bodhisattva conduct is based on one's own mind; all ripening and disciplining of beings is based on one's own mind..."
>
> "One's own mind must be supported with all roots of virtue. One's own mind must be moistened with Dharma-clouds. One's own mind must be purified from obstructive dharmas. One's own mind must be strengthened with vigor..."

And here, when the noble Sudhana became confused upon seeing Māyādevī, this instruction was given by the city-goddess Ratnanetrikā regarding seeing her:

> "By you, O son of good family, there should be skill in guarding the city of the mind—by not dwelling with delight in the objects of all saṃsāra.
>
> By you there should be engagement in adorning the city of the mind—by relying on the ten Tathāgata powers.
>
> By you there should be engagement in purifying the city of the mind—by removing envy, stinginess, and guile.
>
> By you there should be engagement in increasing the city of the mind—by increasing the great vigor of the accumulations of omniscience.
>
> By you there should be engagement in making the city of the mind impregnable—by not being crushed by all affliction-Māras, bodily evil-friend Māras, and Māra-armies.
>
> By you there should be engagement in expanding the city of the mind—by suffusing all worlds with great loving-kindness.
>
> By you there should be engagement in covering the city of the mind—by accomplishing the great Dharma-umbrella as antidote to all unwholesome dharmas.
>
> By you there should be engagement in opening the city of the mind—by causing all worlds to obtain internal and external things...
>
> Thus, O son of good family, for the bodhisattva engaged in purifying the city of the mind, all roots of virtue can be attained. Why is that? For when the bodhisattva's city of the mind is thus purified, all obstructions do not stand before him—neither the obstruction to seeing Buddhas, nor the obstruction to hearing Dharma..."

---

## Part X: Mind Does Not Waver from Calm

Therefore it is established:

> **Mind-training is indeed the bodhisattva training.** (Kārikā 9ab)

And this is for one of non-fickle mind:

> **And mind does not waver from calm, due to cessation of external activities.** (Kārikā 9cd)

For one dependent on non-clear-comprehension, whose mindfulness is stolen—the mind wavers, being drawn elsewhere from the intended object. But when by mindfulness and clear comprehension external activities are ceased, then, due to being under their control, bound to a single object, [the mind] remains as long as wished. And from that, the detailed benefits as before.

---

## Part XI: Attracting Beings

Even now, one becomes capable of accomplishing beings' aims, due to generating faith:¹¹

> *Non-fickle everywhere, by slow, affectionate, gentle speech,*
> *one attracts worthy people and becomes one whose [instruction] is accepted.* (Kārikā 10)

And this is the bodhisattva's very task—the attraction of beings. As illuminated by the bodhisattva Āryapriyadarśana in the Āryādharmasaṃgītisūtra:

> "Thus, O Blessed One, the bodhisattva should practice so that merely by seeing him, beings generate faith. Why is that? For the bodhisattva, O Blessed One, there is no other task except the attraction of beings. The ripening of beings—this, O Blessed One, is the bodhisattva's Dharma-assembly."

---

## Part XII: The Danger of Displeasing Others

What is the fault if one does not act thus?

> *But one whose [instruction] is not accepted—the world, having disdained that seed of the Victor,*
> *like fire hidden by ash, is cooked in hells and such.* (Kārikā 11)

As previously indicated. And the cause of his disdain is this harm:

> *In the Ratnamegha the Victor stated the summary restraint:*
> *That by which beings become displeased—that one should carefully avoid.* (Kārikā 12)

As He said:¹²

> "And what are those bodhisattva behaviors? Here the bodhisattva does not dwell in an improper place, not at an improper time. He is not one who speaks at the wrong time, not one who does not know the time, not one who does not know the place. From whatever cause beings might become displeased toward him—he, for the sake of protecting all beings and for the sake of fulfilling his own accumulation of awakening, becomes one of accomplished deportment, soft-spoken, gentle-spoken, not associated with gatherings, oriented toward seclusion, with a pleasing face."

Hence in the Dharmasaṃgītisūtra it is taught:

> "One who protects beings protects morality."

By this slight [rule], protection of the world is accomplished regarding private sitting with young women, etc. Likewise, in water and land places for enjoyment, one should not void urine, feces, phlegm, pus, etc.—disgusting things—secretly or openly, for the sake of protecting the minds of gods and humans.

---

## Part XIII: Rules of Speech

In the Bodhisattvaprātimokṣa too, avoidance of displeasing [behavior] is stated:

> "One should not chew a tooth-stick in front [of others]; one should not spit in front [of others]..."

This rule of respect and modesty should be applied everywhere, not only among fellow-practitioners. But in this sūtra, regarding fellow-practitioners, [it is specified] to show the fear of greater offense.

And as stated here:

> "One should not be loud-speaking..."

And this rule is not limited to one place.

Likewise in the Brahmaparipṛcchā it is also stated:

> "The bodhisattva should not be like an executioner."

And also from the Prātimokṣa, seeking out and avoiding what displeases the world. There:

> *One should not eat with mouth full, noisily, with extended face.*
> *One should not sit with dangling legs, nor rub the arms simultaneously.*

Thus, by one's own inference, seeing, and hearing, one should protect against displeasing the world.

---

## Part XIV: The Sāgaramati Precepts on Speech

Avoidance of displeasing speech is not easy, so it is shown for the purpose of awakening memory. As taught in the Āryasāgaramatisūtra:¹³

> "He is not of sunken speech. Not of scattered speech. Not of flowing speech. Not of blazing speech. Not of speech led by desire. Not of vulgar speech. Not of unguarded speech. Not of speech kindling hatred. Not of restless speech. Not of fickle speech. Not of actor-stage speech. He is not one who places false testimony on [another's] face."

---

## Part XV: The Tathāgataguhya Precepts on Speech

In the Āryatathāgataguhyasūtra it is also said:¹⁴

> "Indeed, O son of good family, the bodhisattva's speech is not desirous, not hateful, not deluded, not afflicted, not of crushed-answer, not speech exalting one's own side, not speech suppressing the other's side, not speech favoring one's own appearance, not speech opposing the other's appearance, not speech just for winning the debate, not speech of proud explanation."

---

## Part XVI: The Daśabhūmika Precepts on Speech

In the Āryadaśabhūmakasūtra too it is stated:¹⁵

> "That speech which is unpleasant, destroying one's own and others' continuum—having abandoned such speech, he utters speech which is affectionate, soft, pleasant, sweet, dear-making, mind-pleasing, beneficial, ear-pleasant, heart-going, love-producing, clear in sound, knowable, worth hearing, not dependent, desired by many, pleasing to many, mind-pleasing to many, praised by the wise, bringing welfare and happiness to all beings, uplifting the mind, gladdening the mind, delighting one's own and others' continuum, pacifying desire, hatred, delusion, and all afflictions—he utters such speech. He avoids even speech with legends and history, avoiding it."

---

## Part XVII: Protecting Beings by Examining One's Own Faults

In brief, protecting the disaffection of others is taught in the Āryasāgaramatisūtra:¹⁶

> "Another single dharma conduces to the Mahāyāna-synthesis: **by examining one's own faults, one protects all beings.**"

---

## Part XVIII: Summary Verse on Protecting One's Person

> *This is the protection of one's person—* (Kārikā 13a)

—how one is not destroyed by others, and how one does not destroy others. The condensed meaning of this extensive text should always be held in mind by the bodhisattva:

> *"Firmly stable, well-pleased, steady, with earnest respect,*
> *modest, with fear, peaceful, intent on pleasing others,*
> *always under control of self and beings in blameless matters,*
> *I hold this mind like a magical creation, without pride."*

---

## Part XIX: Protection Through Medicine, Clothing, Etc.

Is this the extent of protecting one's person? No. What then?

> *...with medicine, clothing, etc.* (Kārikā 13b)

Therein, medicine is of two kinds: constant medicine and illness-condition medicine.¹⁷

Regarding constant medicine, it is stated in the Āryaratnamegha:

> "Therefore, of the alms-food, one portion he sets aside for fellow practitioners. A second [portion] for the suffering. A third for the destitute. The fourth he himself partakes. Partaking, he partakes without desire, without attachment, without greed, without clinging—only for the sake of the body's maintenance, for its support.
>
> He partakes in such a way that he is neither too emaciated nor too heavy-bodied. Why is that? One who is too emaciated turns away from the wholesome side. One who is too heavy-bodied is overcome by torpor. Therefore, having partaken of that alms-food, one should be oriented toward the wholesome side..."

---

## Part XX: The Dharma-Armor for Alms-Gathering

In the Āryaratnarāśi it is also stated:¹⁸

> "By him, going to village, town, or hamlet for alms, the Dharma-armor should be donned. What is that Dharma-armor?
>
> Having seen unpleasant forms, one should not be repelled. Having seen pleasant [forms], one should not be attracted. Thus regarding pleasant and unpleasant sounds, smells, tastes, and touchables—one should not be attracted, not be repelled.
>
> With senses well-restrained, with eyes not upturned, looking only a plow-length ahead, with a trained thoroughbred mind, not abandoning the mental orientation to prior dharmas, not with a continuum cast toward baits—thus one should go for alms, going house-to-house.
>
> Wherever alms-food is obtained, there one should not generate attachment. Wherever not obtained, there one should not generate aversion. In ten houses' entry, if from the eleventh house alms-food is not obtained, even then one should not be distressed. And the thought should be generated: 'These householders and brahmins have many tasks. They are not obliged to give to me. It is already wonderful that they notice me—let alone that they will give alms.'
>
> Thus, not distressed, one should go for alms.
>
> And whatever beings come within the range of one's eyes—women, men, boys, girls, even animals—toward them the thought of loving-kindness and compassion should be generated: 'May I act so that those beings who come within range of my eyes, who will give me alms-food, I will cause to go to good destinations. Such a yoga I will pursue.'
>
> Then, having gathered the alms-food, coarse or refined, one should look about in the four directions: 'Who here in this village is a poor being to whom I will give a share of this alms-food?' If one sees a poor being, a share should be given from that alms-food.
>
> If one does not see any poor being, the thought should be generated: 'There are beings not in visible range who do not come within range of my eyes. To them I offer the first portion of this alms-food. Having taken it, may they partake.'
>
> Then, having taken that alms-food, ascending to that forest-hermitage, with washed hands, with fine conduct, endowed with the appearance of renunciant practice, sitting with legs crossed, that alms-food should be partaken...
>
> While partaking, this mental orientation should be generated: 'There are in this body eighty thousand families of worms. May they, by this very nourishment, dwell happy and comfortable. Now I will gather them with materiality; having attained awakening, I will gather them with Dharma.'
>
> If his alms-food is coarse, the thought should be generated: 'By coarse food, my body will become light, fit for exertion. My excretion will be little. Faith-offerings will be little. I will have lightness of body and lightness of mind. I will have little sickness and torpor.'
>
> When his alms-food is abundant, one should be an eater of measure, one whose nature is to relinquish. Then, descending onto some rock from that alms-food, the thought should be generated: 'Whatever flocks of deer and birds are interested in material food—may they, having taken it, partake.'"

---

## Part XXI: Avoidance of Meat

Thus, protection of one's person should be done with constant medicine. But not with fish and meat, since it is prohibited in the Laṅkāvatārasūtra:¹⁹

> "All meat is not to be eaten by a bodhisattva of compassionate nature—so I declare..."
>
> *Due to one's own birth, due to misconduct,*
> *due to arising from semen and blood,*
> *as horrifying to beings—*
> *the yogī should avoid meat.*
>
> *Meat, onions, various liquors,*
> *garlic, and leeks—*
> *the yogī should always avoid.*
>
> *One should avoid anointing with oil;*
> *one should not sleep among creatures pierced by thorns,*
> *nor in places of great danger for beings with hollows and non-hollows...*
>
> *For gain, life is killed; for meat, wealth is given.*
> *Both those evil-doers are cooked in Raurava and such...*
>
> *Whoever, transgressing the Sage's word, foolishly eats meat—*
> *for the destruction of both worlds, initiated in Śākya's teaching—*
>
> *They go to the supreme terrible hell;*
> *those evil-doers are cooked in fierce Raurava and such, the meat-eaters.*
>
> *There is no meat that is three-fold pure—*
> *not prepared, not wished for, not caused.*
> *Therefore, meat should not be eaten...*
>
> *By one dwelling in loving-kindness always, it is entirely censured by me.*
> *One would be together with lions, tigers, deer, etc.*
>
> *Therefore, one should not eat meat, which horrifies people.*
> *Being obstructed from liberation-dharma—this is the flag of the noble ones.*

Regarding what is recited in the Jñānavatīparivarta about eating meat—that is without fault because it accomplishes a great purpose.

---

## Part XXII: Illness-Condition Medicine

Constant medicine has been stated. Illness-condition medicine—that should certainly be used.²⁰

Even in the Śrāvaka-vinaya, for one's own sake, for the sake of the holy life, even selling the bowl and robe for the maintenance of the body is stated—how much more for the sake of saving limitless people, the bodhisattva's body. "Rare is the festival of obtaining such a moment-opportunity."

And for showing this, the Blessed One himself demonstrated the use of medicine there.

---

## Part XXIII: The Mind of a Servant

> *But from enjoying with self-craving, afflicted offense arises.* (Kārikā 13cd)

For everything has been relinquished by the bodhisattva to all beings. Therein, if forgetfully, with craving for self-maintenance, he partakes of another's property, he incurs afflicted offense.²¹

But if, free from craving, attached to another or remembering the task for beings, he partakes—he does not incur afflicted offense.

If perceiving it as another's property, he partakes for his own sake—he incurs the offense of theft. In the earlier rule, in the Prātimokṣa, this is a *pārājika* defeat.

But with possessions owned by beings, the person (*ātmabhāva*) owned by beings is itself protected—so there is no fault. For a servant, always occupied with the master's work, has no own-property by which he would live.

As stated in the Dharmasaṃgītisūtra:

> "By the bodhisattva there should be dwelling like a servant—causing the accomplishment of all beings' service."

---

*Thus ends the Sixth Chapter, Protection of One's Person, in the Śikṣāsamuccaya.*

---

## Footnotes

¹ **[Chapter Structure]** Chapter 6 follows the pattern: mindfulness → earnestness → calm → mind-training → practical applications (speech, food, medicine, mantras).

² **[Kārikā 8]** Root verse: "This is accomplished by constant mindfulness. Mindfulness arises from earnestness. Earnestness arises from ardor, knowing the greatness of calm."

³ **[Source]** *Āryākṣayamatisūtra*: Comprehensive list of *śamathākṣayatā* (inexhaustible calm) qualities.

⁴ **[Key Teaching]** *samāhito yathābhūtaṃ prajānāti*: "One who is concentrated knows things as they are." This connects calm (śamatha) with insight (vipaśyanā).

⁵ **[Source]** *Ugraparipṛcchā*: Instructions for householder and renunciant bodhisattvas on mindfulness.

⁶ **[Source]** *Āryaratnacūḍasūtra*: Definition of *samyaksmṛti* (right mindfulness) as gatekeeper (*dauvārika*).

⁷ **[Source]** *Prajñāpāramitā*: The *saṃprajanya* (clear comprehension) passage—knowing every action in the moment.

⁸ **[Source]** *Candrapradīpasūtra*: Morality and concentration mutually support each other.

⁹ **[Key Teaching]** *cittaparikarma* (mind-training) is the essence of bodhisattva training because mind is the root of all dharmas.

¹⁰ **[Source]** *Āryagaṇḍavyūha*: The "city of the mind" (*cittanagara*) metaphor—guarding, purifying, expanding, making impregnable.

¹¹ **[Kārikā 10]** Attracting beings through gentle speech and non-fickle behavior. This is "the bodhisattva's very task."

¹² **[Source]** *Ratnamegha*: Summary restraint—"That by which beings become displeased—avoid it."

¹³ **[Source]** *Āryasāgaramatisūtra*: Twelve kinds of unwholesome speech to avoid.

¹⁴ **[Source]** *Āryatathāgataguhyasūtra*: Speech free from desire, hatred, delusion, victory-seeking.

¹⁵ **[Source]** *Āryadaśabhūmakasūtra*: The ideal speech—affectionate, sweet, heart-going, love-producing, affliction-pacifying.

¹⁶ **[Key Teaching]** "By examining one's own faults, one protects all beings." The single dharma that synthesizes Mahāyāna.

¹⁷ **[Medicine Teaching]** Two kinds: *satatabhaiṣajya* (constant medicine = alms-food) and *glānapratyayabhaiṣajya* (illness-condition medicine).

¹⁸ **[Source]** *Āryaratnarāśi*: Detailed instructions on alms-gathering, including the eighty thousand worm families, sharing with the poor, etc.

¹⁹ **[Source]** *Laṅkāvatārasūtra*: Famous prohibition of meat-eating for bodhisattvas of compassionate nature.

²⁰ **[Practical]** Even selling bowl and robe is permitted for body-maintenance in service of beings.

²¹ **[Kārikā 13]** "From enjoying with self-craving, afflicted offense arises." The bodhisattva's possessions belong to all beings.

---

## Key Concepts

- **Smṛti** (स्मृति): Mindfulness; 12 kinds enumerated
- **Śamatha** (शमथ): Calm, tranquility; generates yathābhūtajñāna
- **Saṃprajanya** (संप्रजन्य): Clear comprehension; knowing each action
- **Cittaparikarma** (चित्तपरिकर्म): Mind-training; the essence of bodhisattva training
- **Cittanagara** (चित्तनгर): City of the mind; to be guarded, purified, expanded
- **Ādara** (आदर): Earnestness; antidote to contempt
- **Lābhasatkāra** (लाभसत्कार): Gain and honor; to be avoided
- **Satatabhaiṣajya** (सततभैषज्य): Constant medicine; alms-food
- **Trikoṭipariśuddha** (त्रिकोटिपरिशुद्ध): Three-fold pure meat (but rejected)
- **Dāsopamatā** (दासोपमता): Servant-like nature; all belongs to beings

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*

<div id='chapter-7'></div>

# Chapter 7: Protection of Enjoyments and Merit (Bhogapuṇyarakṣā)

---

*The seventh chapter of Śāntideva's Compendium of Training, on how to protect enjoyments and merit through proper use of material goods, avoiding gain and honor, cultivating humility, and dedicating merit to awakening.*

---

## Introduction

Thus, protection of one's person is to be understood [as stated in the previous chapter].¹ But protection of enjoyments (*bhogarakṣā*) is to be stated. There:

> **By one who undertakes good deeds, and by one knowing measure in everything,**
> **from this training-precept, protection of enjoyments is not difficult.** (Kārikā 14)

---

## Part I: Knowing Measure in Enjoyments

For in the Ugraparipṛcchā, the training-precept is stated:²

> "Acting with well-considered action; acting with well-done action."

By this, examination of the improper disposition of enjoyments [is enjoined]. The prohibition of contempt becomes established. And by the presentation on calm, knowing measure (*mātrajñatā*) and knowing propriety (*yuktijñatā*) have been stated.

By this, the following is established: "Even when the task can be accomplished with little and low enjoyments, one should not—oneself or through others—neglect the destruction of many superior enjoyments."

---

## Part II: Proper Use of Servants and Possessions

Hence in the Ugraparipṛcchā it is stated:³

> "By the proper use of sons, wives, female servants, male servants, workers, and employees..."

Likewise, neither relinquishing nor non-relinquishing should be done when it causes obstruction to one's own and others' awakening-factors, learning, etc.

For a bodhisattva of greater or equal power in beings' welfare, neither relinquishing nor non-relinquishing should be done when it causes obstruction to greater or equal wholesome [deeds]—this is established.

---

## Part III: The Renunciant's Dharma-Gift

Regarding this, in the Bodhisattvaprātimokṣa it is stated:⁴

> "What indeed, Śāriputra, should a bodhisattva who has gone forth from household life, devoted to the limbs of awakening—how should he give gifts? What gifts should he give? How much should he give?...
>
> He should be a Dharma-giver, a Dharma-patron.
>
> And, Śāriputra, if a householder bodhisattva, having filled buddha-fields equal to the sands of the Ganges with the seven treasures, should give this gift to the Tathāgatas, Arhats, Samyaksaṃbuddhas—and if a renunciant bodhisattva should illuminate a single four-line verse, this latter generates far more merit.
>
> **Śāriputra, material gift (*āmiṣadāna*) has not been approved by the Tathāgata for the renunciant.**...
>
> If, however, there should be gain that has come into the bowl, that is in the bowl, righteous, rightfully obtained—he should be one who eats in common with his fellow-practitioners.
>
> If someone should come asking for a bowl or robe, he should give what is superfluous beyond the Buddha-approved three robes, as long as it is not relinquished.
>
> But if his robe would be insufficient—that on which the holy life depends—that should not be relinquished. Why is that? The Tathāgata has said the three robes are not to be given away.
>
> But if, Śāriputra, a bodhisattva, having relinquished the three robes, should become one who is heavy toward beggars—by him few desires would not have been cultivated.
>
> By a bodhisattva who has gone forth from household life, the Dharma should be cultivated. He should be devoted to that."

---

## Part IV: The Danger of Obstructing Greater Good

For otherwise, for the sake of gathering one [person], great harm would be done to a great mass of beings—and to that being's own future preparation as a bodhisattva—harm to a great purpose.⁵

Hence, avoiding the excellent wholesome side is called "non-washing" (*apakṣāla*).

Thus, the prohibition of relinquishing [is stated]. The prohibition of non-relinquishing is also [stated], as in the Āryasāgaramatisūtra, where having many gains is seen among the obstacles to the Mahāyāna.

---

## Part V: The Vimalakīrti Exchange

In the Āryavimalakīrtinirdeśa too it is stated:⁶

> "What should one afraid of the fear of saṃsāra resort to?"
>
> He said: "One afraid of the fear of saṃsāra, O Mañjuśrī, the bodhisattva should resort to the greatness of the Buddha."
>
> "Where should one wishing to stand in the greatness of the Buddha stand?"
>
> He said: "One wishing to stand in the greatness of the Buddha should stand in equality with all beings."
>
> "Where should one wishing to stand in equality with all beings stand?"
>
> He said: "One wishing to stand in equality with all beings should stand for the liberation of all beings."

---

## Part VI: Dharmasaṃgīti on Beings' Liberation

In the Dharmasaṃgīti too, the bodhisattva Sārthavāha said:⁷

> "Whatever bodhisattva, O Blessed One, wishes for enlightenment first for all beings, not for himself... this, O Blessed One, is the Dharma-assembly."

And from the very relinquishment [of all to beings], the absence of self-interest is established. However, due to fear of harm to beings' welfare, one does not place one's own burden on an unsuitable being.

But where one does not see harm to beings' welfare, what difference is there whether one accomplishes the world's welfare oneself or through another? Because one does not relinquish one's own wholesome [deed] for the sake of accomplishing another bodhisattva's wholesome [deed].

If one fears one's own suffering in bad destinies—that very suffering belongs to the second [bodhisattva] as well. If one is indifferent [thinking] "That suffering does me no harm"—by the sūtras stated [above], one incurs offense.

---

## Part VII: The Four Bodhisattva-Pretenders

As in the Ratnakūṭasūtra:⁸

> "There are, O Kāśyapa, four who are pretenders (*pratirūpaka*) of bodhisattvas..."

It is stated: "He is one who seeks his own happiness, not one who seeks to remove the suffering of all beings."

Therefore, by the rule of the Ugraparipṛcchā, as before, oneself should be blamed.

---

## Part VIII: Protection of Merit

Protection of enjoyments has been stated in summary. Protection of merit (*puṇyarakṣā*) is to be stated.⁹

> **By freedom from craving for the ripening of self-interest, merit is well-protected.** (Kārikā 15ab)

As stated in the Nārāyaṇaparipṛcchā:

> "He does not protect morality for his own sake, not for the sake of heaven, not for the sake of Śakra-hood, not for the sake of enjoyments, not for the sake of power, not for the sake of form, not for the sake of beauty, not for the sake of fame... not afraid of hell does he protect morality... not afraid of animal birth does he protect morality.
>
> Rather, he protects morality for the sake of establishing the Buddha-guidance. He protects morality seeking the welfare, benefit, happiness, and security of all beings."

He, the bodhisattva endowed with such a morality-aggregate, does not diminish in ten dharmas. Which ten?
- He does not diminish from universal-monarch sovereignty
- He is unheedful, aspiring to awakening, aspiring to see Buddhas
- He does not diminish from Brahma-hood, from seeing Buddhas
- He does not diminish from Dharma-hearing
- He does not diminish from bodhisattva-vow undertaking for accomplishing practice as heard
- He does not diminish from all wholesome dharmas, dhyānas, due to unobstructed eloquence...

Thus, the bodhisattva mahāsattva established in the morality-aggregate is always honored by gods, always praised by nāgas, always honored by yakṣas, always worshipped by gandharvas, always respected by nāga-lords and asura-lords, always well-regarded by brahmins, kṣatriyas, merchants, and householders, always to be approached by the wise, always attended by Buddhas, approved by the Teacher for the world with its gods, and compassionate toward all beings...

He does not go to four destinies. Which four?
- He does not go to the unfavorable-moment destiny, except for the sake of ripening beings
- He does not go to a buddha-field empty of Buddhas
- He does not go to birth in a wrong-view family
- He does not go to any bad destiny.

---

## Part IX: No Regret After Giving

Thus, even merit that was previously relinquished, when taken up again under the power of afflictions—its protection should be made. Even from merit-giving, whatever merit [arises], from that too no ripening should be sought, except for others' welfare.¹⁰

Moreover, one wishing to protect merit:

> **Should not create regret afterward.** (Kārikā 15c)

As stated in the Ugraparipṛcchā:

> "And having given, one should not generate a regretful mind."

From weakness of the back comes weakness [of the whole]. From regret, even merit would be exhausted like evil—this is the intention.

---

## Part X: Do Not Proclaim Good Deeds

> **And having done, should not make it public.** (Kārikā 15d)

For in many ways by the Blessed One, having concealed good and revealed evil (*pracchannakalyāṇatā vivṛtapāpatā*) is praised.¹¹ Therein, the exhaustion of what is revealed is understood—of evil through sorrow, of merit through elation.

There is no offense for one who publicizes [good deeds] for beings' sake, with a mind without bait.

As in the Ratnamegha, by the physician analogy, self-exaltation is stated to be without fault.

---

## Part XI: Avoiding Gain, Honor, and Pride

Moreover, one wishing to protect merit:

> **Should be afraid of gain and honor, should always avoid exaltation;**
> **the bodhisattva should be serene, should abandon doubt in the Dharma.** (Kārikā 16)

In the Ratnakūṭa it is stated:¹²

> "By four dharmas, O Kāśyapa, a bodhisattva's arisen wholesome dharmas are diminished—by which [four] they do not increase in wholesome dharmas. Which four?
>
> 1. By the conceited one's search for *lokāyata* mantras (worldly philosophy)
> 2. By one intent on gain and honor, through looking at families
> 3. By hatred-accusation of bodhisattvas
> 4. By rejection of unheard and un-expounded sūtras"

---

## Part XII: Merit Exhausted by Ill-Will

In the Sarvāstivāda Vinaya it is recited:¹³

> "See, O monks, this monk generating faith by prostrating with his whole body at the stūpa of hair and nails? Yes, Venerable One. By this monk, O monks, as much earth as was touched—down eighty thousand yojanas to the golden wheel—as many sands as there are, so many thousands of universal-monarch sovereignties are to be enjoyed by this monk.
>
> Then the venerable Upāli, approaching the Blessed One, joining palms, said to the Blessed One: 'The Blessed One has said that this monk has such great roots of virtue. Where, O Blessed One, do these roots of virtue become thin, exhausted, depleted?'
>
> 'Upāli, I do not see such diminution and impairment as when a fellow-practitioner generates an ill mind toward a fellow-practitioner. There, Upāli, these great roots of virtue become thin, exhausted, depleted.'
>
> 'Therefore, Upāli, thus should you train: "We will not corrupt our minds even toward a burnt post—how much less toward a body possessing consciousness."'"

---

## Part XIII: Ill-Will Destroys a Kalpa's Merit

In the Āryamañjuśrīvikrīḍitasūtra it is also said:¹⁴

> "'Opposition, opposition'—it destroys roots of virtue accumulated over a hundred kalpas; therefore it is called 'opposition' (*pratigha*)."

In the Āryagaṇḍavyūhasūtra too, the night-goddess Samantasattvaparitrāṇyojaḥśrī, telling a previous story, stated:

> "They, by that root of unwholesomeness arisen from mutual disdain, were diminished even from their lifespan, from their beauty, from their strength, from their happiness..."

And here, "always" is stated to show that exaltation should never be made.

---

## Part XIV: When Gain May Be Accepted

Gain and honor, however, are sometimes accepted. As stated in the Āryaratnamegha:¹⁵

> "Here, O son of good family, a bodhisattva, receiving even a Sumeru-sized heap of jewels, accepts it; receiving even a lesser thing, [he accepts it].
>
> Why is that? Thus he thinks: 'These beings are stingy, greedy, overcome by greed. Due to that cause, that condition, that basis, they, like those held by a great mass of water, bob up and down in the ocean of saṃsāra. This will be for their long-term welfare, benefit, and happiness.'
>
> Having accepted everything, he does not make it his own. He does not generate a greedy mind. Rather, he makes an offering to Buddha, Dharma, and Saṅgha in common with all beings. He makes it a means of livelihood for all suffering beings. And he encourages and delights that patron."

And here it is also stated:

> "And by that gift he is not exalted."

And again:

> "If, due to that cause, that condition, that basis, fame-word-renown arises for him—there he is not exalted-born, not proud-born, not intoxicated-born. Thus he thinks: 'Before long, he for whom this fame-word-renown has arisen, and this fame-word-renown itself—all three will entirely cease to be. What person of wise nature, among dharmas that are impermanent, unstable, not lasting, would generate a mind of attachment, be exalted, or be proud and arrogant?'
>
> Thus the bodhisattva dwells with mindfulness well-established regarding gain, honor, fame-word-renown."

---

## Part XV: Dwelling Like an Outcaste Youth

And again:¹⁶

> "They dwell in the world like outcaste youths, with a low, low mind; they become ones who have arrived at pride, intoxication, and arrogance, due to the constant presence of the perception of slander."

And again:

> "Here, O son of good family, a renunciant bodhisattva who has gone forth from household life, [thinking] 'I am like a corpse to friends, relatives, and kin,' is one whose pride has been struck down.
>
> 'Disfigurement has arisen in me, discolored garments are worn by me, another appearance has befallen me'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'With shaved head, bowl in hand, I go from family to family for alms, because of alms, due to alms'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'With a low, low mind, like an outcaste youth, I go for alms'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'I have become a bowl-liver; my livelihood depends on others'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'I accept what is rejected, what is disdained'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'My teachers, masters, and venerable ones are to be pleased'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'My fellow-practitioners are to be satisfied—by such and such conduct, pasture, and behavior'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'I shall attain Buddha-dharmas not yet attained'—his pride is struck down.
>
> 'Among beings who are angry, with corrupted minds, I shall dwell abounding in patience'—his pride is struck down."

---

## Part XVI: The Ten Māra-Works

In the Āryalokottaraparivarta it is stated:¹⁷

> "These, O son of the Victor, are ten Māra-works of bodhisattvas. Which ten?
>
> 1. **Disrespect** toward teachers, venerable ones, ācāryas, fathers, mothers, śramaṇas, brāhmaṇas, and those rightly going, rightly practicing—this is Māra-work.
>
> 2. **Not bowing** to Dharma-preachers, to those who have attained distinguished dharmas, to expounders of excellent dharmas, to those mounted on the Mahāyāna, to those who know the procedure of the nirvāṇa-path, to those who have obtained dhāraṇī-sūtra-supremacy; being haughty and stiff; not generating respect for the Dharma-preacher, not causing service, not causing appreciation—Māra-work. Seated in a Dharma-hearing conversation, when the great Dharma-wave arises, not giving applause to the Dharma-preacher, [thinking] 'May no one praise this one'—Māra-work.
>
> 3. **Generating conceit** and grasping oneself, not grasping others; not entering into self-knowledge; not generating mental satisfaction—Māra-work.
>
> 4. **Generating pride**, not knowing, not understanding, concealing the praises of praise-worthy persons, speaking ill of them, and not being delighted by another's quality-praise—Māra-work.
>
> 5. **Knowing** 'This is Dharma, this is Vinaya, this is true Buddha-word,' yet making Dharma-hatred due to person-hatred, rejecting the true Dharma, and causing others to quarrel—Māra-work.
>
> 6. **Seeking high position**, not seeking what counteracts diminution, approaching and delighting in others' service, not rising for or going out to meet old, long-practiced holy-lifers—Māra-work.
>
> 7. **Being frown-faced**, not smile-faced; not of affectionate, sweet speech; always hard-minded, seeking faults, looking for entry-points—Māra-work.
>
> 8. **Having fallen into conceit**, not approaching the wise, not serving, not attending, not waiting upon, not asking, not inquiring: 'What is wholesome, what is unwholesome? What, done, is for long-term welfare, benefit, and happiness? What, not done, is for long-term non-welfare, non-benefit, and non-happiness?' He becomes stupid, even more stupid, wrapped in delusion, grasping pride, not seeing escape—Māra-work.
>
> 9. **Overcome by pride**, he becomes disgusted with Buddha-arising, exhausts former roots of virtue, does not raise new ones, expounds what is not to be expounded, undertakes quarrels, and is full of disputes. Living thus, there is occasion for him to fall into a wrong great precipice. And yet, by the power of the bodhicitta-commitment, he attains wonder. He does not encounter Buddha-arising for a hundred thousand kalpas—let alone Dharma-hearing. This is the tenth Māra-work.
>
> These, O son of the Victor, are the ten Māra-works which, having avoided, bodhisattvas obtain ten knowledge-works. And here, among the knowledge-works, it is stated: 'Humility toward all beings.'"

---

## Part XVII: The Rāṣṭrapāla Verse

In the Āryarāṣṭrapālasūtra too it is stated:¹⁸

> *Bad-destiny-ground, unfavorable-moment destinies,*
> *poverty, birth in a low family,*
> *blind-birth, weakness, and little strength—*
> *those fools, overcome by pride, grasp these.*

---

## Part XVIII: Beings as Buddha-Field

In the Dharmasaṃgītisūtra too it is stated:¹⁹

> "The being-field is the bodhisattva's buddha-field, since from the buddha-field comes the acquisition of Buddha-dharmas. I am not fit to err regarding it.
>
> And thus he thinks: 'All good conduct and bad conduct proceed depending on beings. From the basis of bad conduct, bad destinies proceed; from the basis of good conduct, gods and humans [proceed].'"

---

## Part XIX: The Ten Mind-States Toward Beings

Hence, in the Ratnolkādhāraṇī too it is stated:²⁰

> "Here, O sons of the Victor, a bodhisattva of first thought-arising, from the very beginning, generates toward all beings a ten-fold mind. Which ten?
>
> 1. Welfare-mind
> 2. Happiness-mind
> 3. Nurse-mind
> 4. Affectionate-mind
> 5. Dear-mind
> 6. Assistance-mind
> 7. Protection-mind
> 8. Equal-mind
> 9. Ācārya-mind
> 10. Teacher-mind
>
> This ten-fold mind he generates."

---

## Part XX: Servant to All Beings

In the Śraddhābalādhānāvatāramudrāsūtra too it is stated:²¹

> "'I am established in the undertaking of being a student of all beings. And I shall establish others in the undertaking of being students of all beings'—thus he obtains confidence...
>
> 'I am established in bowing and prostrating to all beings'—as before."

Therein, bowing and prostrating to all beings is humility toward all beings.

Likewise, in the Āryavimalakīrtinirdeśa, teacher-love toward all beings is stated for the sake of arising in a pure buddha-field.

For the sake of protecting the world's faith, even by one doing foot-showing work [i.e., menial service] on a seat, mentally, toward women or those who have attained unfavorable moments or the fallen—the bodhisattva should practice love and respect.

---

## Part XXI: The Blessed One's Service

For it is stated in the Gaṇḍavyūha, when that company sat on that great lion-throne, all that crowd stood facing, palms joined, honoring that very king...

And as stated in the Bhikṣuprakīrṇaka:²²

> "The Blessed One said: 'Do not fear, monk, do not fear, monk! I, O monk, will attend you. Bring, O monk, the robes, that I may wash them.'
>
> Thus addressed, the venerable Ānanda said to the Blessed One: 'May the Blessed One not wash the impurity-stained robes of this sick one. I, O Blessed One, will wash them.'
>
> The Blessed One said: 'Then, Ānanda, you wash this monk's robes. The Tathāgata will pour the water.'
>
> Then the venerable Ānanda washed the robes of that sick monk. The Blessed One poured the water...
>
> Then the venerable Ānanda, having properly and well taken up that sick monk, having brought him outside, bathed him. The Blessed One poured the water."

---

## Part XXII: Verses on Serving Beings

And it is said:²³

> *Those whom, having pleased, one attains greatness; having opposed, one attains terrible ruin—*
> *is it not right to satisfy them even by abandoning life?*
>
> *These are those beings by pleasing whom many have attained success.*
> *There is no field of success other than beings in the world.*
>
> *These are wish-fulfilling jewels, good vessels, and wish-granting cows.*
> *Like a teacher, like a deity—therefore they are to be pleased.*
>
> *Moreover, for those who are genuine relatives, who bring immeasurable benefit,*
> *having abandoned pleasing beings, what other atonement could there be?*
>
> *One should carry on one's head, as formerly the Protector did as wished,*
> *the beings who dwelt in his matted hair, having become [such], with firm effort.*
>
> *Those for whose sake one breaks the body and enters Avīci—*
> *when that is done for them, [the task] is done.*
>
> *Therefore, toward even great harm-doers, one should practice only good.*
>
> *My very masters, after all—for whose sake I am indifferent even to myself—*
> *how can I make pride toward those various masters, not servant-nature?*
>
> *Those in whose happiness the sage-lords go to joy,*
> *those in whose suffering they enter anger—*
> *from satisfying them, satisfaction of all sage-lords;*
> *in harm to them, harm done to the sages.*
>
> *Just as for one whose body is entirely on fire,*
> *there is no well-being from all pleasures—*
> *so too when beings are suffering,*
> *there is no means of joy for the greatly compassionate.*
>
> *Therefore, whatever suffering I have created for beings,*
> *which has saddened all the greatly compassionate—*
> *that evil I now confess;*
> *may the sages, whom I have grieved, forgive me.*
>
> *And for the sake of pleasing the Tathāgatas,*
> *I undertake servitude to the world with my entire being.*
> *Let crowds of people place their feet on my head,*
> *or let them strike—may the World-Protector be pleased.*
>
> *This entire world has been taken as their own by those of compassionate nature—*
> *of this there is no doubt.*
> *Are these not seen in the form of beings—*
> *those very Protectors? Why disrespect here?*
>
> *This very thing is pleasing of the Tathāgatas;*
> *this very thing is accomplishing one's own aim;*
> *this very thing removes the suffering of the world—*
> *therefore, may this be my vow.*
>
> *Just as a single king's man assaults a great crowd,*
> *acts cruelly—and the far-seeing crowd cannot [resist],*
> *since he is not alone—the king's power is his power—*
> *so one should not disdain any weak offender.*
>
> *For the guardians of hell and the compassionate—that is their power.*
> *Therefore, one should please beings as a servant [pleases] a fierce king.*
>
> *What would an angry king do that would equal the suffering of hell*
> *which is experienced from creating sorrow for beings?*
>
> *What would a pleased king give that would equal Buddha-hood*
> *which is experienced from creating joy for beings?*
>
> *Let alone future Buddha-hood arising from pleasing beings—*
> *do you not see in this very life good fortune, fame, and well-being?*
>
> *Beauty, health, joy, and long life—*
> *the patient one, wandering [in saṃsāra], obtains abundant universal-monarch happiness.*
>
> *That loving-intention is to be honored—that is the very greatness of beings.*
> *That merit from Buddha-faith—that is the very greatness of the Buddha.*

---

## Part XXIII: Loving-Kindness Surpasses All Worship

Hence, in the Candrapradīpasūtra, the fruit of loving-kindness meditation is displayed:²⁴

> *Whatever immeasurable, various worships*
> *in a hundred fields, in niyutas and crores—*
> *having made that worship of incomparable Leaders,*
> *it does not equal a fraction of the mind of loving-kindness.*

Therefore, toward such great venerable ones, one should always avoid exaltation.

---

## Part XXIV: Avoiding Improper Mental Attention

And this exaltation arises from improper mental attention (*ayoniśomanaskāra*).²⁵

Effort should be made toward its non-arising. As stated in the Ratnamegha:

> "And how, O son of good family, is a bodhisattva free from improper mental attention? Here, a bodhisattva, alone, gone to a secluded place, established in solitude, does not generate such a thought: 'I am one who dwells unmixed. I am established in solitude. I am one who practices in the Tathāgata's Dharma-Vinaya. But other śramaṇas and brāhmaṇas—they all dwell mixed, are full of association, are disturbed from the Tathāgata's Dharma-Vinaya.'
>
> Thus a bodhisattva is free from improper mental attention."

---

## Part XXV: Not Exalted by Vigor

And here again:²⁶

> "Here, a bodhisattva, undertaking vigor, does not relish that great vigor. He does not exalt himself by that vigor. He does not disparage others. Thus he thinks: 'What person of wise nature, devoted to one's own work, would reproach others?'
>
> Thus a bodhisattva is one of non-exalted vigor."

---

## Part XXVI: The Summary of Merit-Protection

This, then, is the summary of merit-protection: **dedication to awakening (*bodhipariṇāmanā*)**.²⁷

For thus it is stated in the Āryākṣayamatisūtra:

> "For there is no intermediate exhaustion of a root of virtue dedicated to awakening, until sitting on the seat of enlightenment. Just as, O venerable Śāradvatīputra, for a drop of water that has fallen into the great ocean, there is no intermediate exhaustion until the end of the kalpa."

---

*Thus ends the Seventh Chapter, Protection of Enjoyments and Merit, in the Śikṣāsamuccaya.*

---

## Footnotes

¹ **[Chapter Structure]** Chapter 7 covers two topics: protection of enjoyments (*bhogarakṣā*) and protection of merit (*puṇyarakṣā*).

² **[Source]** *Ugraparipṛcchā*: "Well-considered action, well-done action"—the basis for proper use of enjoyments.

³ **[Practical]** Proper use of sons, wives, servants, workers—neither over-attachment nor neglect.

⁴ **[Source]** *Bodhisattvaprātimokṣa*: For the renunciant, Dharma-gift surpasses material gift. The Tathāgata has not approved material gift for renounciants.

⁵ **[Key Teaching]** *Apakṣāla* ("non-washing"): Avoiding the excellent wholesome side is a form of spiritual pollution.

⁶ **[Source]** *Vimalakīrtinirdeśa*: The famous exchange—fear of saṃsāra → Buddha-greatness → equality with beings → liberation of beings.

⁷ **[Source]** *Dharmasaṃgīti*: The bodhisattva Sārthavāha states the essence—awakening first for beings, not oneself.

⁸ **[Source]** *Ratnakūṭa*: The four bodhisattva-pretenders, including "one who seeks his own happiness."

⁹ **[Kārikā 15ab]** Root verse on merit-protection: freedom from craving for self-benefit.

¹⁰ **[Source]** *Nārāyaṇaparipṛcchā*: Ten dharmas from which the morality-practitioner does not diminish.

¹¹ **[Key Teaching]** *Pracchannakalyāṇatā vivṛtapāpatā*: Concealed good, revealed evil—the bodhisattva's way.

¹² **[Source]** *Ratnakūṭa*: Four causes of diminishing wholesome dharmas—conceit, gain-seeking, bodhisattva-hatred, sūtra-rejection.

¹³ **[Source]** *Sarvāstivāda Vinaya*: The prostrating monk's merit is exhausted by ill-will toward a fellow-practitioner.

¹⁴ **[Source]** *Mañjuśrīvikrīḍitasūtra*: *Pratigha* (opposition) destroys a hundred kalpas' merit.

¹⁵ **[Source]** *Ratnamegha*: When gain and honor may be accepted—for beings' benefit, not making it one's own.

¹⁶ **[Source]** *Ratnamegha*: The eleven forms of "pride struck down" for the renunciant—like a corpse, like an outcaste youth.

¹⁷ **[Source]** *Lokottaraparivarta*: The ten Māra-works of bodhisattvas—disrespect, not bowing, conceit, pride, Dharma-hatred, seeking position, frown-face, not approaching the wise, overcome by pride, disgusted with Buddha-arising.

¹⁸ **[Source]** *Rāṣṭrapālasūtra*: Verse on fools grasping bad destinies through pride.

¹⁹ **[Key Teaching]** "The being-field is the bodhisattva's buddha-field"—beings are the source of Buddha-dharmas.

²⁰ **[Source]** *Ratnolkādhāraṇī*: The ten mind-states toward beings—welfare, happiness, nurse, affection, dearness, assistance, protection, equality, ācārya, teacher.

²¹ **[Source]** *Śraddhābalādhānāvatāramudrā*: "I am established in being a student of all beings."

²² **[Source]** *Bhikṣuprakīrṇaka*: The Blessed One washes the sick monk's robes, demonstrating service.

²³ **[Verse Summary]** Extended verses on serving beings—wish-fulfilling jewels, satisfying the Tathāgatas, servant-nature, confession.

²⁴ **[Source]** *Candrapradīpasūtra*: Loving-kindness meditation surpasses worship in a hundred buddha-fields.

²⁵ **[Key Teaching]** *Ayoniśomanaskāra*: Improper mental attention—thinking "I am superior to other practitioners."

²⁶ **[Practical]** Even when undertaking vigor, don't relish it, don't exalt yourself, don't disparage others.

²⁷ **[Key Teaching]** *Bodhipariṇāmanā* (dedication to awakening): The summary of merit-protection. Merit dedicated to awakening is inexhaustible until enlightenment, like a drop in the ocean.

---

## Key Concepts

- **Bhogarakṣā** (भोगरक्षा): Protection of enjoyments; proper, measured use
- **Puṇyarakṣā** (पुण्यरक्षा): Protection of merit
- **Mātrajñatā** (मात्रज्ञता): Knowing measure
- **Pracchannakalyāṇatā** (प्रच्छन्नकल्याणता): Concealed good
- **Vivṛtapāpatā** (विवृतपापता): Revealed evil
- **Pratigha** (प्रतिघ): Opposition, ill-will—destroys kalpas of merit
- **Mārakarma** (मारकर्म): Māra-work; ten types enumerated
- **Ayoniśomanaskāra** (अयोनिशोमनस्कार): Improper mental attention
- **Bodhipariṇāmanā** (बोधिपरिणामना): Dedication to awakening

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-8'></div>

# Chapter 8: Purification of Evil (Pāpaśodhana)

---

*The eighth chapter of Śāntideva's Compendium of Training, on how to purify evil through the four dharmas: confession, antidote practice, restraint-power, and refuge-power. Includes the famous Suvarṇaprabhāsottama confession verses and teachings on bodhicitta as the supreme purifier.*

---

## Introduction: Why Purification?

Protection of the three—person, enjoyments, and merit—has been stated.¹ Purification is now to be stated. For what purpose?

> **Of one whose person is purified, enjoyment will be wholesome,**
> **like food for embodied beings, rightly prepared, free from husk.** (Kārikā 17)

As stated in the Āryatathāgataguhyasūtra:²

> "And in great cities there are great charnel grounds, filled with hundreds of thousands of living beings. There too, the bodhisattva displays a great dead body of his own person. There, those beings born in animal wombs, having eaten their fill for a month, at the end of their lifespan, having died, are reborn among the gods of the happy destiny, the heavenly world. And he becomes the cause for them up to parinirvāṇa—namely, by the complete purity of that very bodhisattva's former aspiration. By him, for a long time, such an aspiration was made: 'Those who would eat my dead body for a month—may he become the cause for them for arising in heaven up to parinirvāṇa, for that morally disciplined one.' The prayer succeeds. The aspiration succeeds."

And again:

> "He, empowered by the Dharma-body, does benefit for beings even by being seen. He does benefit for beings by being heard, by being touched. Just as, O Śāntamati, the physician-king Jīvaka, having gathered all medicines, made a girl-form composed of medicine-trees—pleasing, beautiful, well-made, well-finished, well-adorned. She comes, goes, stands, sits, and lies down. Those who come—great beings, kings, ministers, merchants, householders, officers—that physician-king Jīvaka joins them with that medicine-girl. Having joined with her, all their diseases are calmed, and they become healthy, happy, without affliction.
>
> Just so, O Śāntamati, that bodhisattva empowered by the Dharma-body—whatever beings touch his body, women, men, boys, girls, afflicted by desire, hatred, and delusion, for them, having been touched, all afflictions are calmed, and they perceive the body as free from burning—namely, by the complete purity of that very bodhisattva's former aspiration."

For this purpose, one's person should be purified.

---

## Part I: The Danger of Not Purifying

And He states the fault of not purifying:

> **Just as grain covered by grass withers by diseases and does not thrive,**
> **so the Buddha-sprout, covered by afflictions, does not go to growth.** (Kārikā 18)

The intention is: for the purpose of growth, by dispelling the antidote.

---

## Part II: What Is Purification?

> **What is purification of person? Purification from evil and affliction.**
> **By the essence of the Perfectly Awakened One's statement; but without effort, one goes to bad destinies.** (Kārikā 19)

---

## Part III: The Four Dharmas for Overcoming Evil

Therein, purification of evil is taught in the Caturdharmasūtra:³

> "By four dharmas, O Maitreya, is a bodhisattva mahāsattva endowed who overcomes evil that has been done and accumulated. Which four?
>
> 1. **Condemnation-practice** (*vidūṣaṇāsamudācāra*)
> 2. **Antidote-practice** (*pratipakṣasamudācāra*)
> 3. **Restraint-power** (*pratyāpattibala*)
> 4. **Refuge-power** (*āśrayabala*)
>
> Therein, **condemnation-practice**: one who has done unwholesome karma becomes remorseful regarding it again and again.
>
> **Antidote-practice**: having done unwholesome karma, one engages intensely in wholesome karma.
>
> **Restraint-power**: from undertaking restraint, obtaining the restraint of non-doing.
>
> **Refuge-power**: going to refuge in Buddha, Dharma, and Saṅgha, and not abandoning bodhicitta.
>
> With a strong support, one cannot be overcome by evil.
>
> By these four dharmas, O Maitreya, is a bodhisattva mahāsattva endowed who overcomes evil that has been done and accumulated."

---

## Part IV: The Suvarṇaprabhāsottama Confession

How should condemnation-practice be cultivated? As stated in the Suvarṇaprabhāsottamasūtra:⁴

> *May the Buddhas attend to me, with minds of compassion and care.*
> *May they accept my transgression, established in the ten directions.*
>
> *And those who stand in the ten directions of the world, supreme among two-footed beings—*
> *whatever evil karma I have done before, most terrible,*
> *all that I will confess, standing before those of ten powers.*
>
> *Not knowing parents, not knowing Buddhas,*
> *not knowing the wholesome—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *Drunk with the intoxication of lordship, with the intoxication of family and enjoyments,*
> *drunk with the intoxication of youth—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *By ill-thought, ill-spoken, and by ill-done action,*
> *not seeing danger—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *By the conduct of a fool's understanding, with mind covered by ignorance,*
> *by the power of evil companions, with mind disturbed by afflictions—*
>
> *By the power of play and delight, by the power of sorrow and disease,*
> *by the fault of unsatisfied wealth—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *By association with ignoble people, by the cause of envy and stinginess,*
> *by the fault of guile and poverty—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *In this time of adversity's approach, by the cause of fear of desires,*
> *even by one without mastery—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *By the power of an unsteady mind, by the power of desire and anger,*
> *even afflicted by hunger and thirst—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *For the sake of drink, for the sake of food, for the sake of clothes, for the cause of women,*
> *by various affliction-torments—whatever evil was done by me.*
>
> *The evil of body, speech, and mind, the threefold misconduct accumulated—*
> *whatever was done with such forms, all that I confess.*
>
> *Whatever disrespect may have been done toward Buddhas, toward Dharma, and toward Śrāvakas—*
> *all that I confess.*
>
> *Whatever disrespect may have been done toward Pratyekabuddhas, or toward Bodhisattvas—*
> *all that I confess.*
>
> *Whatever disrespect may have been done toward Dharma-preachers, or toward those of other virtues—*
> *all that I confess.*
>
> *If the True Dharma was rejected by me, always not knowing,*
> *disrespect toward parents—all that I confess.*
>
> *By foolishness, by childishness, covered by pride and arrogance,*
> *by desire, hatred, and delusion—all that I confess.*
>
> *May the Buddhas look upon me, with minds of attention.*
> *May they accept my transgression, with minds offered to compassion.*
>
> *Whatever evil was done before by me, in hundreds of kalpas,*
> *on account of that I am sorrowful-minded, pitiful, tormented by fear.*
>
> *I fear the evil karma, constantly with a despairing mind.*
> *Wherever I will go, there is no power for me anywhere.*
>
> *All Buddhas are compassionate, all are removers of fear in the world.*
> *May they accept my transgression and release me from fear.*
>
> *May the Tathāgatas wash away the fruit of affliction and karma for me.*
> *May the Buddhas bathe me with the waters of the river of compassion.*
>
> *All evil I confess, whatever was done before by me.*
> *Whatever evil is mine now—all that I confess.*
>
> *For the future, I undertake restraint from all evil actions.*
> *I do not hide that evil, whatever misconduct may have been mine.*
>
> *The threefold bodily karma, and the fourfold of speech,*
> *by three kinds from mind—all that I confess.*
>
> *Done by body, done by speech, and thought by mind,*
> *the tenfold karma done—all that I confess.*
>
> *Whatever evil karma of mine, giver of undesired fruit—*
> *all that I will confess, standing before the Buddhas.*

---

## Part V: The Upāli Teaching on Offenses

Especially, the confession of bodhisattva offenses, heavy and light, is stated in the Āryopāliparipṛcchā:⁵

> "What is the heavy root offense? Generally, it is stated there: 'If, O Upāli, a bodhisattva mahāsattva set out in the Mahāyāna should incur offenses connected with desire equal to the sands of the Ganges, and should incur a single offense connected with hatred, taking the bodhisattva-vehicle as authority... this [latter] is a heavier offense.
>
> Why is that? Hatred, O Upāli, conduces to the abandonment of beings. Desire conduces to the gathering of beings. And, O Upāli, with regard to the affliction that conduces to gathering beings—there the bodhisattva has no deception, no fear...'
>
> Therefore, O Upāli, whatever offenses are connected with desire—all those I call non-offenses."

What is the intention here? It is because one who gathers beings is preeminent. For this teaching is for one with sincere intention and compassion.

For immediately after, He says:

> "Therein, O Upāli, bodhisattvas who are unskillful in means fear offenses connected with desire. But bodhisattvas who are skillful in means fear offenses connected with hatred, not those connected with desire."

---

## Part VI: Who Is Skillful in Means?

Who are skillful in means? Those who are restrained from abandoning beings by wisdom and compassion.⁶ For abandonment of beings occurs in two ways: by wisdom alone, from understanding the emptiness of suffering; and by compassion alone. By the power of affliction, compassion is quickly lost.

As stated in the Upāyakauśalyasūtra:

> "Just as, O son of good family, a man skilled in mantra-lore, bound by the king with a five-fold binding, when he wishes to escape, by the power of a single mantra-lore cuts all bonds and escapes—just so, O son of good family, a bodhisattva skilled in means finds delight in the five strands of desire, and dwells surrounded by them. But when he wishes, by the power of wisdom-commitment and by one omniscience-mind, having enjoyed all strands of desire, he dies and is reborn in a Brahma-world."

---

## Part VII: The Story of Jyotirmāṇavaka

In the Upāyakauśalyasūtra, regarding the brahmin youth Jyotirmāṇavaka who practiced celibacy for forty-two thousand years:⁷

> "Standing at the seventh step, compassion arose [in him]. 'Even if I, having broken this vow, would be destined for hell—still I am able to experience hellish suffering. And may this woman be happy. May she not make an end [of herself].'
>
> Thus, O son of good family, the brahmin youth Jyotirmāṇavaka, turning back, taking that woman by the right hand, said thus: 'Rise, O beautiful one. I will do whatever you wish.'...
>
> And I, O son of good family, by a great-compassion-mind-arising, though urgent, connected with desire, turned back for ten thousand kalpas.
>
> See, O son of good family: what for others is karma conducing to hell, for the bodhisattva skillful in means is karma conducing to arising in a Brahma-world."

---

## Part VIII: The Ratnamegha Permission

Clearly in the Āryaratnamegha, permission is given for killing a man about to commit an immediate retribution [crime].⁸ Even in the Śrāvaka-vinaya, in the very situation of a root offense, non-offense is stated for releasing a deer, etc., out of compassion.

---

## Part IX: Antidote-Practice

Condemnation-practice has been stated. Antidote-practice is stated.⁹ Therein, by familiarity with profound sūtras, exhaustion of evil occurs.

As stated in the Vajracchedikā:

> "O Subhūti, whatever sons or daughters of good family will take up these sūtras of such a kind, up to mastery—they will become despised, greatly despised. Why is that? Whatever karma of those beings done in former births, conducing to bad destinies—by that state of being despised, in this very life they will exhaust it, and will attain Buddha-awakening."

---

## Part X: Purification by Emptiness-Intent

By intent on emptiness, too, purification of evil occurs. As stated in the Tathāgatakoṣasūtra:¹⁰

> "If, O Kāśyapa, a single being should be endowed with these ten unwholesome karma-paths, extremely serious:
>
> - This is the supreme taking of life: if his father were a Pratyekabuddha, and he killed him
> - This is the supreme taking what is not given: stealing the property of the Three Jewels
> - This is the supreme sexual misconduct: if the mother were an arhat-woman
> - This is the supreme false speech: slander of the Tathāgata
> - This is the supreme divisive speech: disparaging the Noble Saṅgha
> - This is the supreme harsh speech: reviling the Noble Ones
> - This is the supreme idle chatter: distracting those who desire the Dharma
> - This is the supreme ill-will: dragging [others] into immediate-retribution [crimes]
> - This is the supreme covetousness: mind of taking the gains of those rightly going
> - This is the supreme wrong view: the entangled-view
>
> These, O Kāśyapa, are the ten unwholesome karma-paths, extremely serious.
>
> If, O Kāśyapa, a single being endowed with these ten unwholesome karma-paths—he should enter the Tathāgata's Dharma-teaching connected with cause and condition: 'There is no self here, no being, no life, no person who acts or experiences. Thus, uncreated, uncompounded, dream-nature, non-affliction-nature, naturally luminous nature of all dharmas he enters; originally pure, all dharmas he trusts-in, believes-in—I do not say that being goes to bad destinies.'"

---

## Part XI: The Trisamayarāja Antidote

In the Trisamayarāja too, antidote-practice is stated:¹¹

> "Closing the eyes, with mind focused on Buddhas and Bodhisattvas, one should recite the hundred-syllable [mantra] eight thousand times. With eyes closed, one sees Buddhas and Bodhisattvas; one becomes free from evil.
>
> Or, circumambulating a caitya, one should recite eight thousand times. With a caitya-image or true Dharma-book as focus, this is the very procedure."

---

## Part XII: The Cundā Dhāraṇī

Or one should recite the Cundā Dhāraṇī until one sees the signs of evil's exhaustion in dreams:¹²

> "Namely: from weeping, shelter, eating yogurt, milk, etc., one becomes free from evil. Or from vomiting, or from seeing sun and moon, from traveling through space, from defeating blazing fire, black buffalo, black man; from seeing the assembly of monks and nuns; from ascending a milk-tree, mountain, lion-throne, palace, or ship; and from hearing the Dharma—exhaustion of evil should be discerned."

---

## Part XIII: The Bhaiṣajyaguru Teaching

In the Bhaiṣajyaguruvaidūryaprabharājasūtra too:¹³

> "Those who hold the five training-precepts, those who hold the ten training-precepts, those who hold the bodhisattva-vow of four hundred training-precepts, those renunciants who, as monks, hold two hundred fifty training-precepts, those nuns who hold five hundred training-precepts—and those who, having undertaken training-restraint, have fallen from some training-precept—if those, afraid of bad-destiny fear, should hold the name of that Blessed One, the Tathāgata Bhaiṣajyaguru Vaidūryaprabharāja, and should worship according to their capacity, no going to bad destinies is to be expected for them ever again."

---

## Part XIV: Restraint-Power

Antidote-practice has been stated in summary. Restraint-power (*pratyāpattibala*) is now stated.¹⁴

As stated in the Āryakṣitigarbhasūtra:

> "A bodhisattva mahāsattva, having refrained from taking life, gives fearlessness to all beings—non-terror, non-disturbance, non-hair-raising.
>
> By that root of virtue, by that karma-ripening, whatever karma-obstruction, affliction-obstruction, dharma-obstruction was done or caused to be done or approved, by body, speech, and mind, by the cause of taking life, mounted on the five-destiny wheel since beginningless time, tossed in the river of saṃsāra—all that he crushes by the wheel of refraining from taking life, up to making it ripen without remainder.
>
> He becomes dear to gods and humans of similar class, without affliction, long-lived..."

And likewise for the remaining ten wholesome karma-paths, each crushing its opposite unwholesome [karma].

---

## Part XV: Refuge-Power and the Pig-Story

Restraint-power has been stated. Refuge-power (*āśrayabala*) is to be stated.¹⁵ Here the pig-story is to be cited:

> *Those who go to the Buddha for refuge do not go to bad destinies.*
> *Having abandoned human bodies, they obtain divine bodies.*

Thus, taking Buddha, Dharma, and Saṅgha as objects, evil is exhausted.

---

## Part XVI: Bodhicitta as Supreme Purifier

In the Āryamaitreyavimokṣa, purification of evil by bodhicitta is stated:¹⁶

> "Like a kalpa-end fire, by burning all misconduct.
> Like the netherworld, by consuming all unwholesome dharmas...
>
> Just as, O son of good family, there is a gold-substance called Hāṭakaprabhāsa. One pala of it makes a thousand iron palas into gold. And that pala cannot be consumed by those thousand iron palas, cannot be made into iron.
>
> Just so, a single omniscience-mind-arising substance, held by the knowledge of dedicating roots of virtue, consuming all karma-and-affliction-obstruction-iron, makes all dharmas into the color of omniscience. And the omniscience-mind-arising substance cannot be afflicted or consumed by all karma-affliction-iron.
>
> Just as, O son of good family, a single lamp, in whatever house or cave it is brought, having been brought in, even darkness accumulated for a thousand years it dispels, and makes light.
>
> Just so, a single omniscience-mind-arising-lamp, in whatever being's thicket of ignorance-darkness it is brought—having been brought in, it dispels even karma-affliction-obstruction-darkness accumulated for ineffable hundred thousand kalpas, and makes the light of knowledge.
>
> Just as, O son of good family, for great nāga-kings crowned with the wish-fulfilling-jewel-king-diadem, there is no fear of attack from others—just so, for bodhisattvas crowned with the bodhicitta-great-compassion-wish-fulfilling-jewel-king-diadem, there is no fear of attack from bad destinies."

---

## Part XVII: The Upāli Teaching on Bodhicitta Purification

In the Āryopāliparipṛcchā too:¹⁷

> "Here, O Upāli, a bodhisattva set out in the Mahāyāna—if he should incur an offense in the morning period, and in the midday period should dwell not separated from the omniscience-mind—the bodhisattva's morality-aggregate is boundless.
>
> If he should incur an offense in the midday period, and in the evening period should be not separated from the omniscience-mind—the bodhisattva's morality-aggregate is boundless.
>
> Thus, the procedure is stated for each watch.
>
> Thus, O Upāli, the training has a remedy for bodhisattvas set out in the Mahāyāna.
>
> There, the bodhisattva should not generate excessive remorse, should not be excessively regretful.
>
> But if a Śrāvaka-vehicle person should incur offense again and again—the morality-aggregate of that Śrāvaka person is to be known as destroyed."

---

*Thus ends the Eighth Chapter, Purification of Evil, in the Śikṣāsamuccaya.*

---

## Footnotes

¹ **[Chapter Structure]** Chapter 8 addresses purification (*śodhana*) after the three protections (person, enjoyments, merit) in Chapters 6-7.

² **[Source]** *Tathāgataguhyasūtra*: The bodhisattva's purified body benefits beings even after death—animals eating it are reborn in heaven.

³ **[Key Teaching]** *Caturdharmasūtra*: The four dharmas for overcoming evil—condemnation, antidote, restraint, and refuge.

⁴ **[Source]** *Suvarṇaprabhāsottamasūtra*: The famous confession verses—comprehensive enumeration of evil's causes and confession before the Buddhas.

⁵ **[Source]** *Upāliparipṛcchā*: Hatred-offenses are heavier than desire-offenses because hatred abandons beings while desire gathers them.

⁶ **[Key Teaching]** Skillful means: those restrained from abandoning beings by wisdom and compassion together.

⁷ **[Source]** *Upāyakauśalyasūtra*: Jyotirmāṇavaka's story—breaking celibacy vow out of compassion leads to Brahma-world, not hell.

⁸ **[Source]** *Ratnamegha*: Permission to kill someone about to commit immediate-retribution crime (out of compassion).

⁹ **[Source]** *Vajracchedikā*: Familiarity with profound sūtras causes evil to be exhausted in this life through being despised.

¹⁰ **[Source]** *Tathāgatakoṣasūtra*: Even one who committed the ten extreme evils—if entering non-self understanding—does not go to bad destiny.

¹¹ **[Source]** *Trisamayarāja*: The hundred-syllable mantra recited 8,000 times brings sight of Buddhas and freedom from evil.

¹² **[Source]** *Cundā Dhāraṇī*: Dream-signs indicating evil's exhaustion—vomiting, seeing sun/moon, hearing Dharma, etc.

¹³ **[Source]** *Bhaiṣajyaguruvaidūryaprabharājasūtra*: Holding the Medicine Buddha's name protects from bad destinies.
