*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-11'></div>

# Chapter Eleven: The Exposition of Dwelling in the Wilderness (*Araṇyasaṃvarṇana*)

## [The Call to the Wilderness]

Now, it is established that one who has heard the Dharma, having contemplated the faults of household life according to the instruction given in the *Ugradattaparipṛcchā*, should resort to the forest in order to purify his mind.[^1] And thus it is stated in the *Candrapradīpasūtra*:

> "Never indeed, indulging in desires, having generated craving for sons and wives, and having dwelt in a house, which is despicable, will he attain the supreme enlightenment.
> Those who abandon desires like a pit of fire, having generated craving for sons and wives, and, terrified, depart from the house—for them this supreme enlightenment is not difficult to obtain.
> No Buddha existed in the past, nor will one arise in the future, who, remaining [at home], attained this supreme, highest enlightenment.
> Having abandoned a kingdom like a lump of phlegm, let him dwell in the forests, desiring seclusion (*viveka*). Having abandoned the defilements, having crushed pride, they awaken to the stainless, unconditioned enlightenment." ... [*peyālaṃ*]
> "The best of men, the Jinas, are not served with food, drink, robes, flowers, perfumes, and unguents as they are when, having gone forth, they practice the Dharma.
> And he who, longing for enlightenment, is wearied of the evil constructions [of the world] for the sake of beings, should take seven steps toward the forest; he then becomes distinguished by merit."

If, however, due to affection for beings of similar disposition, or due to desire for an assembly, or due to desire for gain, etc., he should delay in entering seclusion, for that purpose it is stated here:

> "The wise do not quarrel with fools; having honored fools, they avoid them.
> The wise do not serve fools, knowing the natural continuity of fools (*bālāna svabhāvasaṃtatim*).
> However long one may serve a fool, they again become like enemies.
> The wise do not trust fools here, knowing the natural continuity of fools.
> Fools are broken by nature (*svabhāvabhinnā*); where is there a friend for ordinary people?
> Spoken to with righteous words, they manifest anger, hatred, and disbelief; pure ones (*vijñā*) knowing this meaning, do not trust fools.
> Fools match with fools, just as impurity with impurity;
> But the wise match with the wise, just as ghee with the cream of ghee."

And thus it is said again here:

> "Happy always are those men in the world for whom there is no beloved or unloved anywhere.
> And those who do not delight in caves experience the great happiness of the ascetic life (*śrāmaṇakaṃ susukhaṃ*).
> For whom there is no 'mine' anywhere, for whom there is no possession at all,
> They wander the world like a rhinoceros (*khaṅgasamā*), just as the wind wanders in the sky.[^2]
> Happy indeed would be those men in the world whose mind does not stick to the world.
> Their mind is always like the wind, and no attachment to beloved or unloved is found.
> For those to whom living with the suffering is unpleasant, and separation from the suffering is pleasant—
> Abandoning these two extremes, happy are those men who delight in the Dharma."

Again it is said here:

> "He is constantly yoked to few duties (*alpakṛtyayogī*), having avoided all extensive qualities and faults.
> He never disputes, being yoked to the yoke (*yuktayogī*); these qualities become his in forest-dwelling.
> He is always weary of the conditioned (*saṃskṛta*); he has no desire anywhere in the world.
> And there is no increase of inflows (*āsravas*) for him; living in the forest, these are his benefits.
> No dispute (*adhikaraṇa*) ever arises for him; always delighting in peace, walking in seclusion.
> Restrained in speech, mind, and body, many qualities become his in forest-dwelling.
> And liberation (*mokṣa*) becomes favorable for him; he quickly penetrates the peaceful resolve (*adhimukti*).
> Cultivating the resolve of wandering in the forest, all these qualities become his, the forest-dweller."

He says again:

> "Resort to forest thickets (*vanaṣaṇḍa*), always secluded, having abandoned going to villages and towns.
> Be without a second, like a rhinoceros, always; before long you will obtain the supreme samādhi."

In the *Ārya-Rāṣṭrapālasūtra* it is also said:

> "Having abandoned the home, a thicket of endless faults, always regardless of anxiety,
> They obtain delight in the wilderness, virtuous, with peaceful senses, gentle.
> No enjoyment of women or men is found anywhere for them;
> They dwell alone (*ekākī*), like a rhinoceros, with pure intentions, stainless.
> They are not thrilled by gains, nor do they cower at lack of gain in their own mind;
> Of few desires, satisfied with whatever comes, free from deceit and hypocrisy."

In the *Ugradattaparipṛcchā* it is also said: "Association with beings is not to be done by me, for I should not generate the roots of virtue of a single being," etc.

If, however, a learned one (*śrutavān*), having obtained this perfection of the moment (*kṣaṇasaṃpad*), attached to gain and the like, should not purify his mind, he alone in the world with its gods would be deceived. For it is said in the *Ārya-Ratnakūṭa*:

> "Just as, Kāśyapa, a certain man, being carried away by a great flood of water, should die of thirst for water, just so, Kāśyapa, here some ascetics and brahmins, having grasped and mastered many Dharmas, do not dispel the thirst for passion, do not dispel the thirst for hatred, do not dispel the thirst for delusion. Being carried away by the great flood of Dharma, they die of the thirst for defilements and go to a bad destiny, a downfall."

Therefore, one must necessarily resort to the forest. And he should resort to such places where the alms-round is not too far and not too near, where waters are clear, pure, stainless, involving little trouble, and pleasant to use. And he should resort to such places that are endowed with trees, flowers, fruits, and leaves, free from dangerous beasts, endowed with caves and mountain slopes, pleasant to wander in, peaceful, and solitary (*advitīya*).

Dependent on those places, whatever he has previously recited, that he recites three times in the night and three times in the day—not with too high a voice, not too low, not with excited senses, not with a scattered mind—living on faith, supporting the text, grasping the signs, driving away torpor.

If a king, or a royal minister, or others—brahmins, kṣatriyas, citizens, or country folk—should approach the forest-dwelling monk, he should welcome them with respect. And he should speak to him thus: "Sit, O Great King, on the seat prepared." If he sits, he [the monk] should also sit. If he does not sit, neither should sit. If his senses are agitated, he should be calmed. "Those gains are well-gained by you, O Great King, in whose territory virtuous, qualified, learned ascetics and brahmins dwell, unmolested by thieves, soldiers, and the like."

If he is steady, disciplined, with peaceful senses, and capable of Dharma teaching, then a varied Dharma teaching should be offered to him. If he does not like the varied one, a Dharma teaching conducive to agitation (*saṃvega*) should be offered. If he does not like the one conducive to agitation, the lofty greatnesses of the Tathāgata should be taught. Suitable actions should also be offered to brahmins, kṣatriyas, citizens, and country folk who approach. Being thus learned, he becomes capable of propitiating the mind of the hearers of Dharma. And those beings obtain joy, faith, and gladness in his presence.

In the *Ugradattaparipṛcchā* it is also said: "Furthermore, O householder, a bodhisattva who has gone forth and dwells in the forest should examine thus: 'For what purpose do I dwell in the forest? One does not become an ascetic merely by forest-dwelling. Even many untamed, undisciplined, unyoked, unexerting ones dwell here—namely, herds of deer, monkeys, birds, thieves, and caṇḍālas dwell here. And they are not endowed with the qualities of an ascetic. But rather, that purpose for which I dwell in the forest, that purpose must be fulfilled by me—namely, the purpose of asceticism (*śrāmaṇyārtha*).'" ... [*peyālaṃ*]

"Furthermore, O householder, a bodhisattva who has gone forth and dwells in the forest should examine thus: 'For what purpose have I come to the forest?' He should investigate thus: 'I have come to the forest terrified by fear. Terrified by fear of what? Terrified by the fear of society (*saṃgaṇikā*). Terrified by the fear of association, by the fear of lust, hatred, and delusion... by the fear of pride, intoxication, and hypocrisy... by the fear of greed, jealousy, and miserliness... by the fear of form, sound, smell, taste, and touch. Terrified by the fear of I-making and mine-making (*ahaṃkāramamakāra*)... by the fear of restlessness and doubt... by the fear of the Māra of the aggregates (*skandhamāra*)... the Māra of defilements (*kleśamāra*)... the Māra of death... the Māra of the sons of gods. Terrified by the fear of the perversion of permanence in the impermanent... self in the non-self... purity in the impure... happiness in suffering. Terrified by the fear of mind, thought, and consciousness... by the fear of hindrances, obstructions, and outbursts... by the fear of the view of the transitory collection (*satkāyadṛṣṭi*)... by the fear of bad friends... by the fear of gain and honor... by the fear of untimely counsel... by the fear of 'seen' in the unseen, 'heard' in the unheard, 'thought' in the unthought, 'known' in the unknown... by the fear of the intoxication of asceticism in the non-ascetic... by the fear of mutual hatred... by the fear of the desire realm, form realm, and formless realm... by the fear of rebirth in all states of existence... by the fear of the realms of hell, animals, and the world of the fathers. Simply put, terrified by the fear of all unwholesome mental engagements. Terrified by these fears and terrors of such a kind, I have resorted to forest-dwelling.'"

... [*peyālaṃ*] "Furthermore, O householder, a bodhisattva standing in forest-dwelling, terrified or frightened, should train thus: 'Whatever fears arise, all of them arise from grasping at a self (*ātmagrāha*).' ... [*peyālaṃ*] 'If, while dwelling in the forest, I should not abandon self-grasping, self-adherence, self-possession, self-cause, self-thirst, self-perception, self-view-appropriation, self-view, self-basis, self-imagination, self-protection—my forest-dwelling would be meaningless. But rather, O householder, there is no forest-dwelling for one who perceives a self, no forest-dwelling for one who perceives an other.' ... [*peyālaṃ*] 'Forest-dwelling, O householder, is called the dwelling of non-arising in all dharmas, the dwelling of non-attachment in all dharmas.' ...

Just as, O householder, in the forest, grass, shrubs, herbs, and trees dwelling there do not fear, are not terrified, do not tremble, do not fall into terror, just so, O householder, a bodhisattva who has gone forth and dwells in the forest should produce a body of perception similar to grass, shrubs, herbs, trees, sticks, and walls—similar to a reflection of oneself. He should produce a mind equal to an illusion (*māyā*). Who fears here? Who trembles in this? By him, terrified or frightened, the body should be examined wisely thus: 'There is no self, or being, or life, or soul, or person, or human in this body. This is a false imagination (*abhūtaparikalpa*), namely, fear. That false imagination should not be imagined by me.' By him, knowing that just as grass, shrubs, herbs, and trees dwell in the forest without 'mine', without possession, just so, without 'mine', without possession, the forest is just all dharmas—knowing this, having attained it, he should dwell. For what reason? Forest-dwelling is the cutting of conflict (*raṇaccheda*), without 'mine', without possession."

... [*peyālaṃ*] "Furthermore, O householder, knowing 'Forest-dwelling is permitted by the Buddha,' one should dwell in the forest. For here the fulfillment of pure dharmas occurs. Having supported the roots of virtue, afterwards, having descended to villages, towns, markets, kingdoms, and capitals, I will teach the Dharma." ... [*peyālaṃ*] "If, however, O householder, the bodhisattva who has gone forth descends to a group for the sake of recitation or study, he should be respectful there, with deference to teachers and preceptors, to elder, middle, and new monks, acting rightfully, not lazy, acting himself, not annoying others. And he should not be one who values attendance. And he should examine thus: 'Even the Tathāgata, the Arhat, the Perfectly Awakened One, worshipped by the world with its gods, Māras, and Brahmas, by the generation with its ascetics and brahmins, worthy of gifts from all beings—even he does not accept attendance from anyone; how much less we, untrained, wishing to train? Rather, we ourselves will become attendants for all beings. We ourselves will perform attendance and service for others, and we will not accept attendance and service from anyone. For what reason? For, O householder, the monk who is burdened with attendance loses the beneficial influence of virtuous qualities. And for those whom he supports, they think thus: "For the sake of attendance, he supports us."'"

He further says here: "If, O householder, a forest-dwelling Bodhisattva, desirous of hearing the Dharma, or wishing to see a teacher or preceptor, or inquiring after the sick, should come to a dwelling on the outskirts of a village, he should produce the thought of returning in the evening and of setting out [early]. If, however, his recitation or study is dependent on another, then, dwelling in the monastery, he should have a mind inclined to the forest. This itself is his forest-dwelling: the perception of all things as the forest and insatiety in the search for Dharma."

In the *Ārya-Ratnarāśisūtra* [Note: Source likely *Ratnarātri-sūtra* per text, but *Ratnarāśi* is a known text on this topic] it is also said: "If, while he is dwelling in that forest abode, as an ordinary person who has not attained the fruit, poisonous beasts should come, he should not produce fear or terror there. And he should produce this thought: 'Formerly, I have already renounced body and life; I have approached forest-dwelling. I should not be afraid here, not terrified; rather, having produced loving-kindness, I will avoid fault, I will remove fear. Even if, having done so, those poisonous beasts should deprive me of life and eat me, then I should produce this thought: "My gains are easily obtained, by which the essence will be taken from this essence-less body. But I am not able to satisfy these poisonous beasts with flesh. Having eaten my flesh, they will dwell experiencing pleasant contact."'"

... [*peyālaṃ*] "But if, while dwelling in that forest retreat, non-humans should approach—whether fair or foul in appearance—he should neither be drawn in nor repulsed by them. If deities who have seen Buddhas of the past should approach a forest-dwelling monk and ask him questions, then that forest-dweller should, according to his ability, his strength, and his realization of the Dharma, teach the Dharma to those deities. But if they ask very profound questions which that forest-dwelling monk is unable to answer, he should speak after manifesting a created form (*nirmāṇa*); he should not be disparaged for being unlearned. [He should think:] 'I will strive, I will exert myself in the Buddha’s teaching. There will come a time, an occasion, when, having heard and mastered the Dharma, I will be able to answer all questions. Meanwhile, may the inspiration for listening to the Dharma arise in you.'"

"Furthermore, dwelling in that forest retreat, one should take as an object of contemplation (*nimittaṃ grahītavyam*) the grasses, shrubs, herbs, and trees. How do these come to be? Just as these beings, which are ownerless (*asvāmikānām*), without a ‘mine’ (*amamānām*), without appropriation (*aparigrahāṇām*), thus motionless (*niśceṣṭānāṃ*) and inactive (*nirvyāpārāṇāṃ*), have production and dissolution, and yet there is no one who produces them or stops them, so too this body is like an apparition of grass, sticks, and a wall—ownerless, without a ‘mine’, without appropriation, motionless, and inactive. It arises due to the assemblage of causes and conditions, and ceases due to the deficiency of causes and conditions. Yet ultimately, no dharma whatsoever arises or ceases (*na punaratra kaściddharmaḥ paramārthata utpadyate vā nirudhyate*)."

Furthermore, it is said: "Dwelling in that forest retreat, one should produce this thought: 'Although I have come to the forest alone, without a companion, I have no companion who would accuse me of good or bad deeds. Yet, indeed, there are for me deities, Nāgas, Yakṣas, and Buddhas, the Blessed Ones, who know the intent of my mind. They are my witnesses (*sākṣiṇaḥ*). So I, dwelling here in the forest retreat, go under the power of an unwholesome mind ($akuśalacitta*). If I, having come so far, alone, without a second, unsupported, without 'mine', without possession, should think a thought of desire, or should think a thought of malice or violence, or should think another unwholesome thought, I would be no different from those beings delighting in association and society. And those deities, Nāgas, and Yakṣas would be deceived by me, and the Buddhas, the Blessed Ones, would be unpropitiated."

*Thus ends the eleventh chapter, named "The Description of the Forest," in the Śikṣāsamuccaya.*

---

## Scholarly Apparatus

### Source Sūtras Cited
The *Śikṣāsamuccaya* is a compendium of sūtra quotations. This chapter cites the following texts:

1.  **Candrapradīpasūtra** (a.k.a. *Samādhirājasūtra*): Cites verses on the necessity of leaving the household life and the faults of desire.
2.  **Ārya-Rāṣṭrapālasūtra** (or *Rāṣṭrapālaparipṛcchā*): Cites verses on the virtues of the solitary bodhisattva ("like a rhinoceros").
3.  **Ugradattaparipṛcchā**: Extensive citations (prose) on the motivation for forest dwelling, fearlessness, and the analysis of fear as self-grasping (*ātmagrāha*).
4.  **Ārya-Ratnakūṭa** (likely the *Kāśyapaparivarta* section): Cites the analogy of dying of thirst while being carried away by a flood (dying of defilements while holding the Dharma).
5.  **Ārya-Ratnarātrisūtra** (some editions read *Ratnarāśi*): Cites passages on dealing with fear of wild beasts and non-humans.

### Key Sanskrit Terms
*   **Araṇya** (अरण्य): The forest, wilderness; etymologized as *a-raṇa* (without conflict/passion).
*   **Viveka** (विवेक): Seclusion, discrimination, solitude. The physical and mental separation from the "crowd" (*saṃgaṇikā*).
*   **Khaṅgasama** (खङ्गसम): "Like a rhinoceros." A famous simile for solitary wandering (reference to the *Rhinoceros Sutra*).
*   **Ātmagrāha** (आत्मग्राह): Grasping at a self. Identified in the *Ugradattaparipṛcchā* citation as the root of all fear.
*   **Asvāmika** (अस्वामिक): Ownerless. A descriptor for the body and phenomena, likened to grass and trees—functioning without a controller.
*   **Nirmāṇa** (निर्माण): Created form/emanation. The forest monk uses this to teach deities when his own form is insufficient.

### Critical Notes
[^1]: **Ugradattaparipṛcchā**: This sūtra is central to the *Śikṣāsamuccaya*'s treatment of the lay vs. monastic bodhisattva vocation. Here it establishes the premise: the household is full of faults (*gṛhadoṣa*).
[^2]: **Khaṅgasamā**: This echoes the famous refrain of the *Pratyekabuddha* vehicle ("wander alone like a rhinoceros"), but here it is integrated into the Bodhisattva path as a necessary phase of intensification.




---



<div id='chapter-12'></div>

# Chapter 12: Preparation of the Mind (Cittaparikarma)

---

**Summary**: Having established the practice of forest dwelling in Chapter 11, Śāntideva now describes the internal work required: the **Preparation of the Mind** (*Cittaparikarma*). This involves achieving one-pointed concentration (*samādhāna*), restraining the senses, and applying specific antidotes (*pratipakṣa*) to the defilements: foulness meditation for desire, loving-kindness for hatred, and dependent origination for delusion.

---

### 1. Sense Restraint and Concentration

Dwelling in the forest, one should devote oneself to concentration (*samādhāna*).
As stated in the *Bhagavatī (Prajñāpāramitā)*:¹

> "For the sake of those very beings, practicing the perfection of meditation (*dhyānapāramitā*), he becomes one of undistracted mind (*avikṣiptacitta*). Why?
>
> Even worldly meditation (*laukikī dhyānopapatti*) is hard to obtain for one of distracted mind—how much more the supreme perfect awakening! Therefore, I must be of undistracted mind."

The text continues:
> "He does not grasp at signs or features (*nimittānuvyañjanagrāhī*) when seeing forms with the eye. For if he dwelt with the eye-faculty unguarded, covetousness and dejection (*abhidhyā-daurmanasya*) would overflow the mind.
>
> Walking, standing, sitting, lying down, or speaking, he does not abandon the state of concentration (*samāhitāvasthā*).
>
> He is not restless of hand, foot, or face. Not scattered in speech. Not agitated, not inflated, not fickle, not lazy.
> In gain and loss (*lābhalābha*), pleasure and pain, praise and blame, fame and disrepute, he is even (*sama*), unchanged (*nirvikāra*), not elated or dejected (*anunnatānavanata*)."

### 2. Antidotes to Torpor and Agitation

If the mind sinks into **torpor** (*līna*), he should stimulate it with the cultivation of joy (*muditā*).
If the mind becomes **agitated** (*uddhata*), he should calm it with attention to impermanence (*anityatā*).

For the antidote to both, one should remember the verses from the *Ārya-Rāṣṭrapāla Sūtra*:²

> "After hundreds of millions of kalpas,
> Once in a while a Buddha arises, a Great Sage.
> That supreme moment (*kṣaṇa*) has been obtained today;
> If you desire liberation, abandon heedlessness (*pramāda*)."

And from the *Ārya-Lalitavistara Sūtra*:

> "The triple world is ablaze with the fire of death...
> Life passes quickly like lightning in the sky,
> Like water rushing down a mountain river.
>
> The pleasures of desire (*kāmaguṇā*) are like a dung-pit, like slight-points,
> Like honey on a razor's edge.
> Like dreams, like echoes, like mirages."

### 3. The Four Great Terrors (Death Meditation)

The *Rājāvavādaka Sūtra* provides a powerful contemplation on death through the metaphor of the **Four Mountains**:³

> "Just as four great solid mountains coming from the four directions would crush everything—grass, wood, and living beings—and escape by speed or force is impossible; so come four great terrors:
> 1.  **Old Age** crushing youth.
> 2.  **Disease** crushing health.
> 3.  **Death** crushing life.
> 4.  **Loss** (vipatti) crushing prosperity.
>
> Just as a lion seizes a helpless deer, so death seizes the living being.
>
> Alone, without companion, leaving this world, entering the great darkness...
> At that time, there is no other protection, no other refuge, except the Dharma."

### 4. Antidote to Desire: Foulness Meditation

For the defilement of **desire** (*rāga*), the antidote is **Foulness Meditation** (*aśubhā bhāvanā*).
As stated in the *Ārya-Ratnamegha Sūtra*:

> "He examines this body: 'In this body there are hair, nails, teeth, dirt, skin, flesh, bones, sinews, kidneys, heart, liver, lungs, intestines, feces, urine, pus, blood, bile, phlegm...'
>
> Examining thus, he thinks: 'Even a fool knowing these substances would not generate desire—how much less a wise one!'"

The *Prajñāpāramitā* adds the "Butcher's Eye" analogy:
> "Just as a butcher, having killed a cow and cut it into four pieces, examines it not as a 'cow' but as meat; so the Bodhisattva examines the body as elements (*dhātu*): earth, water, fire, wind."

And the "Charnel Ground" contemplations:
> "Seeing corpses in the charnel ground—swollen, blue, devoured by animals, skeletonized, scattered, pulverized to dust—he applies this to his own body:
> **'This body too is of that nature (*evaṃdharmā*), of that character, has not escaped that law.'**"

### 5. Antidote to Hatred: Loving-Kindness

For the defilement of **hatred** (*dveṣa*), the antidote is **Loving-Kindness** (*maitrī*).
As stated in the *Ārya-Akṣayamati Sūtra*, maitrī is of three kinds:
1.  **With beings as object** (*sattvārambaṇā*): For beginners.
2.  **With dharmas as object** (*dharmārambaṇā*): For those practicing the path.
3.  **With no object** (*anārambaṇā*): For those with the patience of non-arising.

The practice begins with dear ones, then neutral ones, then enemies, expanding to the ten directions.

### 6. Antidote to Delusion: Dependent Origination

For the defilement of **delusion** (*moha*), the antidote is the vision of **Dependent Origination** (*pratītyasamutpāda*).
This is detailed in the *Śālistamba Sūtra* (Rice Seedling Sutra):

> "Ignorance conditions dispositions (*saṃskāra*)... up to birth conditions old age and death.
> But Ignorance does not think 'I produce dispositions.'
> Nor do dispositions think 'We are produced by ignorance.'
>
> Just as the earth element supports the body's solidity without thinking 'I support'; just as the sprout arises from the seed without the seed thinking 'I produce'—so does the causal chain operate without an agent (*kāraka*)."

*(The chapter concludes with a detailed exposition of the twelve links of dependent origination through the metaphor of seed and sprout.)*

---

### Footnotes

1.  **Sense Restraint**: This passage parallels the Pali *Indriya-saṃvara* teachings, emphasizing that "grasping at signs" (*nimittaggāha*) leads to the influx of defilements.
2.  **Rarity of the Buddha**: The "Blind Turtle" analogy is implied here. The *kṣaṇa* (opportune moment) is hard to obtain.
3.  **Four Mountains**: A famous simile found also in the Pali *Pabbatūpama Sutta* (SN 3.25).




---



<div id='chapter-13'></div>

# Chapter 13: Foundations of Mindfulness (Smṛtyupasthāna)

---

**Summary**: Building on the mind-training of Chapter 12, Chapter 13 details the **Four Foundations of Mindfulness** (*Smṛtyupasthāna*). Unlike the Śrāvaka version which focuses on impermanence, the Mahāyāna version presented here emphasizes **emptiness (*śūnyatā*)** and the **non-obtaining (*anupalabdhi*)** of any self-nature in the body, feelings, mind, or dharmas.

---

### 1. Mindfulness of Body (Kāyasmṛtyupasthāna)

The Bodhisattva establishes mindfulness in the body.
As stated in the *Dharmasaṃgīti Sūtra*:¹

> "This body is a collection of feet, toes, shins, thighs... skull and head. It is accumulated by karma and housing a hundred thousand defilements.
> It contains various substances: hair, nails, teeth, bones, pus, blood, mucus, brain.
>
> Examining it, he thinks: 'What here is the body?'
> He realizes: **'This body is like space (*ākāśasama*).'**
> He establishes mindfulness in the body as space. Because of this full knowledge (*parijñāna*), his mindfulness does not scatter."

The *Vīradattaparipṛcchā* provides sixty-one metaphors for the body, including:
*   Like an anthill (housing serpents).
*   Like an ungrateful friend (*mitradrohin*).
*   Like a foam ball (*phenapiṇḍa*).
*   Like an empty village (*śūnyagrāma*).
*   Like a potter's vessel (fragile).

> "Just as a fool rubs a piece of coal thinking 'I will make it white,' but it only goes to destruction; so one who bathes and perfumes this body cannot make it pure. It goes to destruction, not purity."

### 2. Mindfulness of Feelings (Vedanāsmṛtyupasthāna)

The Bodhisattva establishes mindfulness in feelings.
As stated in the *Ārya-Ratnacūḍa Sūtra*:

> "When feeling a **pleasant feeling**, he generates great compassion for beings of **desire-nature**, and abandons his own tendency to attachment (*rāgānuśaya*). He knows it as **impermanent**.
>
> When feeling a **painful feeling**, he generates great compassion for beings of **hatred-nature**, and abandons his own tendency to aversion (*pratighānuśaya*). He knows it as a **thorn** (*śalya*).
>
> When feeling a **neutral feeling**, he generates compassion for beings of **delusion-nature**, and abandons his own tendency to ignorance (*avidyānuśaya*). He knows it as **peace/non-self** (*śānta/anātma*)."

The *Dharmasaṃgīti Sūtra* adds the non-dual observation:
> "Feeling is experienced. But by what?
> **The feeler (*vedaka*) is not found separate from the feeling.**"

### 3. Mindfulness of Mind (Cittasmṛtyupasthāna)

The Bodhisattva searches for the mind.
As stated in the *Ārya-Ratnakūṭa (Kāśyapaparivarta)*:²

> "He searches for the mind. Which mind? Past, future, or present?
> Past mind is destroyed. Future mind has not arrived. Present mind has no stability (*sthiti*).
>
> Mind, O Kāśyapa, is not found inside, not outside, not in between.
> **Mind has not been seen by any Buddhas, is not seen, will not be seen.**
> What is not seen—what process (*pracāra*) can it have?"

The text provides metaphors for the illusion-like nature of mind:
*   Like a river current (unstable).
*   Like a lamp flame (dependent on conditions).
*   Like lightning (momentary).
*   Like a thief (stealing wholesome roots).

Also, mind cannot see itself:
> "Just as a sword-edge cannot cut itself, just as a fingertip cannot touch itself; so mind cannot see mind."

### 4. Mindfulness of Dharmas (Dharmasmṛtyupasthāna)

The Bodhisattva establishes mindfulness in dharmas (phenomena).
As stated in the *Ārya-Ratnacūḍa Sūtra*:

> "Observing dharmas, he does not see any dharma...
> He enters the samādhi called 'No Obstruction.'
> He perceives: 'These dharmas are unafflicted (*niḥkleśa*). By awakening to these very defilements, there is awakening.'
> **The own-nature of defilements is the own-nature of awakening.**"

The *Lalitavistara* provides verses on the dependent origination of dharmas, using the examples of:
*   **Seed and Sprout**: Not the same, not different.
*   **Seal and Impression**: Appearance without transference (*saṃkrānti*).
*   **Eye and Form**: Consciousness arises from their meeting, but form represents no essence.
*   **Fire from Sticks**: Arises from conditions, goes nowhere when ceased.
*   **The Lute**: Sound arises from wood, strings, and effort; but sound is not in the wood or the strings.

### 5. The Emptiness of Names

The chapter concludes with the *Lokanāthavyākaraṇa*:

> "Dharmas are empty, nameless.
> In the name, no name-nature is found.
> Name is only a designation (*prajñapti*).
> All dharmas are nameless, yet illuminated by names."

---

### Footnotes

1.  **Body as Space**: The Mahāyāna move here is to dissolve the solidity of the body not just into "parts" (as in Early Buddhism) but into "space" (*ākāśa*), emphasizing emptiness.
2.  **Unfindability of Mind**: This is a classic *Kāśyapaparivarta* passage, central to Zen and Mahāmudrā discussions on looking for the mind and not finding it.




---



<div id='chapter-14'></div>

# Chapter 14: Purification of the Person (Ātmabhāva-pariśuddhi)

---

**Summary**: Having established the applications of mindfulness (*smṛtyupasthāna*) in the previous chapter, Śāntideva now moves to the ontological ground of purification: the realization of emptiness (*śūnyatā*). This chapter analyzes the person (*ātmabhāva*) through the six elements (*dhātu*), proving they are devoid of intrinsic nature (*svabhāva*). It employs the classic metaphors of the dream, the illusion, and the banana tree to deconstruct the "Self" (*ātman*), thereby purifying the bodhisattva's engagement with the world.

---

### 1. The Root of Purification: Emptiness

The applications of mindfulness have been stated. Thus, with a mind yoked to yoga, one should descend into the **emptiness of all dharmas** (*sarvadharmaśūnyatā*) for the sake of lifting the remaining world from the ocean of suffering, for the sake of the method of awakening (*abhisaṃbodhyupāya*), and for the sake of mastery over all dharmas in the three times as boundless as the sky space.¹

For in this way, the emptiness of the person (*pudgalaśūnyatā*) becomes established. And from that, due to the cutting of the root, the defilements (*kleśāḥ*) do not arise.

As stated in the *Ārya-Tathāgataguhya Sūtra*:²

> "Just as, Śāntamati, when the root of a tree is cut, all the branches, leaves, and foliage wither—just so, Śāntamati, from the pacification of the view of the existing body (*satkāyadṛṣṭi*), all defilements are pacified."

### 2. The Praise of Emptiness

The benefits (*anuśaṃsā*) of cultivating emptiness are endless. As the *Candrapradīpa Sūtra* says:³

> "He who trains in this does not tremble at the Sugatas' teaching;
> That hero never goes under the sway of women.
> He finds joy in the teaching of the Sugatas—
> He who knows the self-nature of dharmas as thoroughly pacified.
>
> Not long from now, he will become a Lord of Two-Legged Beings (Buddha);
> He will become a physician, a doctor, a giver of happiness.
> He extracts the dart of suffering entirely from those in pain—
> He who knows the self-nature of dharmas as thoroughly pacified.
>
> That Moon of Men is risen by the power of patience;
> Struck by clods and sticks, he does not get angry.
> Even if cut limb from limb, he is not agitated—
> He who knows the self-nature of dharmas as thoroughly pacified."

### 3. Analysis of the Elements (Dhātuvibhāga)

Now, how are all dharmas selfless (*nirātmāna*)? How is there non-contradiction of the karma-result connection (*karmaphalasaṃbandha*) despite absence of intrinsic nature (*niḥsvabhāvatā*)?⁴

As demonstrated in the *Pitṛputrasamāgama Sūtra*:

> "This person (*puruṣa*), Great King, consists of **six elements** (*ṣaḍdhātu*), **six spheres of contact** (*ṣaṭsparśāyatana*), and **eighteen mental approaches** (*aṣṭādaśamanaupavicāra*).
>
> What are the six elements?
> 1. Earth element (*pṛthivīdhātu*)
> 2. Water element (*abdhātu*)
> 3. Fire element (*tejodhātu*)
> 4. Wind element (*vāyudhātu*)
> 5. Space element (*ākāśadhātu*)
> 6. Consciousness element (*vijñānadhātu*)

#### A. The Earth Element (Solidity)
> What is the internal earth element? Whatever in this body internally is hard, solid, organically appropriated (*upātta*). Hair, nails, teeth, etc.
>
> When the internal earth element arises, it does not come from anywhere. When it ceases, it does not go to any accumulation (*saṃnicaya*).
>
> Consider the embryo. From the union of sperm and blood (*śukra-śoṇita*), the *kalala* arises. But in that union, neither the man exists (inherently) nor the woman. The intention (*saṃkalpa*) arises from the unreal. That intention does not exist by own-nature. Just so, the solidity arises from conditions, but has no arrival or departure.
>
> **Ultimately, the earth element is empty of earth-element-ness.** It is not perceived except by convention (*vyavahāra*).

#### B. The Water Element (Cohesion)
> What is the internal water element? Tears, sweat, saliva, mucus, oil, lymph, marrow, fat, bile, phlegm, pus, blood, urine.
>
> When one sees a beloved, tears flow. From grief or from religious emotion (*dharmasaṃvega*), tears flow. But this water element does not come from anywhere. When the body dries up at death, it does not go anywhere.
>
> Even the great oceans at the end of the kalpa dry up completely. **Ultimately, the water element is empty of water-element-ness.**

#### C. The Fire Element (Maturation)
> What is the internal fire element? That by which the body is heated, digested, by which what is eaten and drunk gets cooked.
>
> It arises, yet comes from nowhere. It ceases, yet goes nowhere. It exists having not existed (*abhūtvā bhāva*), and having existed, it disappears. Why? Because it is devoid of own-nature (*svabhāvarahita*).

#### D. The Wind Element (Motion)
> What is the internal wind element? Upward winds, downward winds, winds in the limbs, breath.
>
> Just as a great storm arises, knocks down trees, and vanishes without a trace—so too the internal wind.

#### E. The Space Element (Opening)
> What is the internal space element? The eye-holes, the mouth, the throat, the gaps where food is swallowed.
>
> Just like a man digging a well in dry ground: where does the space come from? Nowhere. Where does it go when filled? Nowhere. **Space is empty of space-element-ness.** It is motionless, immutable (*avikāra*).

#### F. The Consciousness Element (Cognition)
> What is the consciousness element? The recognition (*prativijñapti*) of the object dependent on the eye, etc.
>
> This consciousness element does not rely on the sense-organ, does not come from the object, does not stay in between. It is not inside, not outside.
>
> **Ultimately, the consciousness element is empty of consciousness-element-ness.**

### 4. The Dream Metaphor (Svapnopama)

The senses are like an illusion (*māyopamā*). The objects are like a dream (*svapnopamā*).

> "Consider, Great King, a man sleeping. In a dream, he enjoys himself with a beautiful, charming woman (*janapadakalyāṇī*). Waking up, he remembers her.
>
> Does that woman exist in the dream? No.
> Is the man wise if he clings to her? No.
>
> Just so, the childish ordinary person (*bālapṛthagjana*) sees forms with the eye and clings to them. Clinging, he generates passion. Passionate, he creates karma.
>
> But that karma is exhausted from the start. It doesn't reside in the East, West, North, or South.
>
> At the moment of death, the final consciousness (*carama-vijñāna*) ceases. Dependent on that, the first consciousness (*prathama-vijñāna*) of the next life arises.
>
> **No dharma goes from this world to the next.** Yet death and rebirth appear to happen.
>
> The final consciousness is empty of final consciousness.
> The first consciousness is empty of first consciousness.
> **There is no agent (*kartā*) here, no experiencer (*bhoktā*), except by name-convention (*nāmasaṃketa*).**"

### 5. The Truth of Convention and Ultimate

Conventional truth (*saṃvṛti*) was taught by the Buddha for the sake of the world's benefit. Ultimate truth (*paramārtha*) is inexpressible, unknowable, unmanifest.

> "The Lion of Men taught convention
> Speaking of the six destinies (*gati*),
> Hell-beings, ghosts, gods, and men,
> But ultimately, no being (*sattva*) is found."

### 6. The Emptiness of the Agent

The *Druma-Kinnararāja-Paripṛcchā Sūtra* uses the chariot metaphor:⁵

> "Look at the assembly of chariot-parts (*śakaṭāṅga*),
> And look at the action of the chariot.
> Just as fire does not turn back from the tree moved by wind,
> So too the agents of karma.
>
> **The doer (*kartā*) is not found in the ten directions.**"

### 7. Purification Through Realization

Therefore, the Perfection of Wisdom (*Prajñāpāramitā*) is to be cultivated by one desiring purification of the mind.

As the *Saṃgīti Sūtra* says:

> "The proponent of emptiness (*śūnyatāvādin*) is not shaken by worldly dharmas.
> He is not delighted by gain, not depressed by loss.
> He doesn't stick to pleasure, doesn't clash with pain.
>
> He who is not shaken by worldly dharmas—he knows emptiness."

---

## Scholarly Notes

1.  **Traikālya**: The three times (past, present, future). Mastery here implies the *dhāraṇī*-power of the bodhisattva.
2.  **Cutting the Root**: A crucial metaphor. While *śamatha* (calm) suppresses defilements like placing a rock on grass, *vipaśyanā* (insight into emptiness) cuts the root so they cannot grow back.
3.  **Candrapradīpa Sūtra**: Also known as the *Samādhirāja Sūtra*. It emphasizes the soteriological power of the emptiness view—it's not just philosophy, it's the mechanism of liberation.
4.  **Karmaphalasaṃbandha**: The connection between action and result. The Madhyamaka challenge is always: "If things are empty, how does karma work?" The answer lies in the dream metaphor—karma functions *precisely because* it is empty (dependent arising), not static.
5.  **Chariot Metaphor**: The classic reductionist analysis. A chariot is neither its wheels, nor axle, nor the collection, nor separate from them. It is a designation (*prajñapti*). So too the Person.

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-15'></div>

# Chapter 15: Purification of Goods and Merit (Bhogapuṇyaśuddhi)

---

**Summary**: Following the purification of the person (*ātmabhāva*), Śāntideva turns to the purification of the bodhisattva's resources (*bhoga*) and merit (*puṇya*). The core principle for goods is **Right Livelihood** (*samyagājīva*)—avoiding hypocrisy (*kuhanā*) and wrong means. The core principle for merit is **Sky-Like Purity** (*gaganasama-viśuddhi*)—practicing the six perfections without the three concepts (giver, gift, receiver), thereby making merit boundless and unpolluted.

---

### 1. Purification of Goods Through Right Livelihood

Immediately after the purification of the self, the purification of goods (*bhogaśuddhi*) is written here separately due to the absence of accumulation (*saṃcayābhāva*).¹

One should know the purification of goods through the purification of **Right Livelihood** (*samyagājīva*).

As stated in the *Ārya-Ugraparipṛcchā*:

> "Here, householder, the lay bodhisattva seeks enjoyments by the Dharma, not by adharma. By fair means (*samena*), not by unfair means (*viṣameṇa*). It becomes Right Livelihood, not wrong livelihood."

### 2. Avoiding Hypocrisy (Kuhanā)

The *Ārya-Ratnamegha Sūtra* details the avoidance of hypocrisy in seeking alms:²

> "A bodhisattva, seeing a donor or patron (*dānapati*), does not construct a posture (*īryāpatha*).
>
> **How does he not construct a posture?**
> He does not lift and place his feet slowly, slowly, sluggishly. Not looking only a yoke's length ahead (*yugamātraprekṣikayā*). But looking confidently, looking naturally (*anābhogaprekṣikayā*). Thus he does not perform **bodily hypocrisy** (*kāyakuhanā*).
>
> **How does he not perform vocal hypocrisy?**
> For the sake of gain, for the cause of gain, he does not become soft-spoken, gentle-spoken, sweet-spoken. He does not utter flattering words.
>
> **How does he not form mental hypocrisy?**
> Even when offered gain by a donor, he displays fewness of desires (*alpecchatā*). He does not generate longing in the mind.
>
> *'This is internal burning, son of good family: when there is fewness of desire in speech, but desire for gain in the mind.'*
>
> Thus the bodhisattva is free from hypocrisy and lapana (hinting/chatter)."

### 3. Avoiding Hinting and Hinting-Actions

> "He does not make a sign (*nimitta*) upon seeing a donor: 'I am distressed by lack of robes, I am distressed by lack of a bowl.'
>
> He does not ask or pray for anything. He does not utter words like: 'Such and such donor gave me such and such object... because he thought I was virtuous, learned, of few desires.'
>
> Thus he is free from **hinting-gain** (*nimittalābha*)."

### 4. Purification of Merit (Puṇyaśodhanam)³

> "From the activity born of the womb of Emptiness and Compassion, there is purification of merit." (Kārikā 21)

As stated in the *Ārya-Gaganagañja Sūtra*: The purity of merit is like the sky (*gagana*).

> "He gives that gift purified of 'I-making' (*ahaṃkāra*).
> Purified of 'Mine-making' (*mamakāra*).
> Purified of view, sign, and diversity.
> Purified of expecting a result (*vipākapratikāṅkṣaṇā*).
>
> **He gives a gift equal to the sky (*gaganasama*).**
>
> Just as the sky is boundless (*aparyanta*), so he gives with a boundless mind.
> Just as the sky is vast and unobstructed, so he gives a gift dedicated to awakening (*bodhipariṇāmita*).
> Just as the sky is formless (*arūpin*), so he gives not relying on any form.
>
> Just as a magically created man (*nirmita*) gives to a magically created man—without discrimination, without effort (*anābhoga*), free from mind-thought-consciousness—so the bodhisattva gives that gift purified by the self-nature of illusion (*māyālakṣaṇa*).
>
> Such a bodhisattva becomes a **Sky-Like Donor** (*gaganasamadāna*)."

### 5. The Sky-Like Perfections

The *Gaganagañja Sūtra* applies this sky-metaphor to all perfections:

*   **Sky-Like Morality (*Śīla*)**: Pure, stainless, calm, not elevated (*anunnata*), not dependent. "Whatever morality is sky-like, that is the bodhisattva's supreme morality."
*   **Sky-Like Patience (*Kṣānti*)**: Unobstructed like the sky. Equal toward all beings.
    *   *Metaphor*: "Just as if there were a great Sāla forest. Someone comes and cuts down a Sāla tree. The remaining trees do not think: 'He is cut, we are uncut.' They have no attachment or aversion. Just so is the sky-like patience."
*   **Sky-Like Vigor (*Vīrya*)**: Knowledge of the body as a reflection (*pratibhāsa*), speech as inexpressible, mind as untroubled.
*   **Sky-Like Meditation (*Dhyāna*)**: The meditation endowed with the best of all forms (*sarvākāravaropeta*) but accomplished through the aspect of emptiness.
    *   Calm by nature, but not calm (inactive) in karma and defilements.
    *   Indifferent to all dharmas, but attentive to Buddha-dharmas.
    *   Cool through peace, but blazing in ripening beings.

### 6. The Realm of the Bodhisattva

As stated in the *Vimalakīrtinirdeśa*:

> "The realm of turning the Wheel of the True Dharma, and the realm of demonstrating Great Parinirvāṇa, and the realm of not abandoning the bodhisattva practice—this too is the bodhisattva's realm (*gocara*)."

---

## Scholarly Notes

1.  **Saṃcayābhāva**: "Absence of accumulation." Unlike ordinary wealth which is hoarded (*saṃcita*), the bodhisattva's goods are pure because they are constantly flowing out to beings. They are "accumulated" only in the sense of merit, not possession.
2.  **Five Wrong Livelihoods**: The text alludes to the classic list: *kuhanā* (hypocrisy/posturing), *lapanā* (hinting/chatter), *naimittika* (fortune-telling/sign-reading), *niṣpeṣikā* (bullying/extortion), and *lābhena lābhaniścikīrṣā* (seeking gain with gain/interest).
3.  **Puṇyaśodhanam**: Purification of merit prevents it from becoming a cause for saṃsāric happiness (which is exhausted) and transforms it into a cause for awakening (which is inexhaustible). The mechanism is *pariṇāmanā* (dedication) sealed with *śūnyatā* (emptiness).

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-16'></div>

# Chapter 16: The Practice of Good Conduct (Bhadracaryāvidhi)

---

**Summary**: This chapter outlines the method (*vidhi*) for increasing (*vṛddhi*) the three purities established in the previous chapters: the Person (*ātmabhāva*), Goods (*bhoga*), and Merit (*puṇya*). It culminates in the liturgical practice of the *Bhadracaryāpraṇidhāna* (The Vows of Good Conduct). Key elements include the increase of strength (*bala*) and non-laziness (*anālasya*), the ten great vows from the *Daśabhūmaka Sūtra*, and the supreme dedication (*pariṇāmanā*) modeled on Mañjuśrī and Samantabhadra.

---

### 1. The Necessity of Increase (Vṛddhi)

Now, the increase of all three (Person, Goods, Merit) must be stated. Why?

> "There are many recipients. If this [offering] is small, what is the use?
> And it does not generate supreme satisfaction. Therefore, this must be increased." (Kārikā 22)

**"Supreme satisfaction"** (*atitṛpti*) refers to Buddhahood. That is not generated for beings by mere purity common to Śrāvakas; hence the need for increase.

### 2. Increasing the Person (Ātmabhāvavṛddhi)

What is the increase of the person? **The increase of Strength (*bala*) and Non-Laziness (*anālasya*).**

#### A. Increase of Strength
The *Ratnamegha Sūtra* defines this strength:
> "There is no being in the assembly of beings who can crush the bodhisattva's strength with their own strength."

How is it increased? As stated in the *Tathāgataguhya Sūtra* (Vajrapāṇi's teaching to Ajātaśatru), a bodhisattva attains such strength by being endowed with ten dharmas:

1.  **Giving body and life**, but not abandoning the True Dharma.
2.  **Bowing to all beings**, but not increasing pride (*māna*).
3.  **Enduring weak beings**, not generating hostility (*pratigha*).
4.  **Giving best food to the hungry**, and giving fearlessness to the frightened.
5.  **Zealous for healing the sick.**
6.  **Satisfying the poor** with enjoyments.
7.  **Plastering the Tathāgata-shrine** with lime/ointment.
8.  **Speaking words of joy** (*ānandavacana*) to beings.
9.  **Sharing enjoyments** with the poor and suffering.
10. **Carrying the burden** of the weary and exhausted.

#### B. Increase of Non-Laziness (Vigor)
What is the increase of non-laziness? **The increase of Vigor (*vīrya*).**

As stated in the *Sāgaramati Sūtra*:
> "Sāgaramati, a bodhisattva should be initiated in vigor (*ārabdhavīrya*), always with firm valor... For the lazy (*kusīda*), enlightenment is very far away. The lazy one has no giving... no wisdom, no benefit for others."

As the *Candrapradīpa Sūtra* says:
> "Like a lotus in the water, he grows gradually."

### 3. Increasing Goods (Bhogavṛddhi)

> "From giving born of the womb of Emptiness and Compassion, there is the increase of goods." (Kārikā 23)

As stated in the *Vajracchedikā*:
> "The bodhisattva who gives a gift while not established [in signs] (*apratiṣṭhita*)—it is not easy to measure his merit-heap."

And in the *Great Prajñāpāramitā*:
> "A bodhisattva desiring to perform immeasurable giving... should train in the perfection of wisdom."

### 4. Increasing Merit (Puṇyavṛddhi)

The increase of merit is the root of all increases.

> "Having made Effort (*vyavasāya*) and Intention (*āśaya*) firm at the very beginning,
> And placing Compassion (*karuṇā*) in front,
> One should strive for the increase of the auspicious." (Kārikā 24)

#### A. Making Effort Firm (Vyavasāya)
How does one firm up effort? Like Ārya Sudhana approaching Maitreya.
*   Thinking of the body's dedication to the limit of the past (*pūrvāntakoṭi*).
*   Correcting the mind (*cittamanasikāra*).
*   Disregarding the fatigue of useless worldly actions.
*   Raising the power of Right Resolve (*samyaksaṃkalpa*) for all bodhisattva practices.

#### B. Making Intention Firm (Āśaya)
The *Dharmasaṃgīti* says:
> "Intention (*āśaya*), Lord, is the root of Buddha-dharmas... Just as fire burns when fuel exists, so Buddha-dharmas blaze when intention exists."

What is this intention? The *Akṣayamati Sūtra* defines it as artificial-less (*akṛtrima*), pure, straight, honesty (*ṛjuka*), steadfast.

#### C. Placing Compassion in Front (Karuṇā)
The *Dharmasaṃgīti* states:
> "Lord, a bodhisattva need not train in too many dharmas. Only **one dharma** is to be guarded and penetrated. Which one? **Great Compassion** (*mahākaruṇā*).
>
> Just as when the wheel-turning monarch's chakra-jewel goes, the whole army follows—so where Great Compassion goes, all Buddha-dharmas follow."

### 5. The Practice of Good Conduct (Bhadracaryāvidhi)

With effort, intention, and compassion thus firmed up, one begins the increase of merit.

> "The Practice of Good Conduct (*Bhadracaryāvidhi*) is to be done:
> Worshipping (*vandanā*) and so forth, always with respect."

#### The Three Heaps (Triskandha)
The *Ugradattaparipṛcchā* prescribes the **Triskandhaka** practice (Three Heaps) three times in the day and three times in the night:
1.  **Confession of Sin** (*pāpadeśanā*).
2.  **Rejoicing in Merit** (*puṇyānumodanā*).
3.  **Requesting the Buddhas** (*buddhādhyeṣaṇā*).

(*Note: Worship/Vandanā is included in Confession.*)

#### The Offering
One offers all unowned flowers, fruits, and jewels in the universe (as stated in *Ratnamegha* and *Trisamayarāja*). Even mental offerings of the jewels in the ten directions are valid.

#### The Ten Great Vows (Daśa Mahāpraṇidhāna)
From the *Daśabhūmaka Sūtra*, the ten essential vows:
1.  **Puja:** To worship all Buddhas without remainder.
2.  **Dharma:** To uphold the Dharma-eye of all Tathāgatas.
3.  **Arising:** To accompany the Buddhas in all world-systems (birth to nirvāṇa).
4.  **Practice:** To engender the vast bodhisattva conduct (*caryā*).
5.  **Ripening:** To ripen all beings (*sattvaparipācana*) in all destinies.
6.  **World-Entry:** To enter all world-systems (*lokadhātu*) with knowledge.
7.  **Purification:** To purify all Buddha-fields (*kṣetrapariśodhana*).
8.  **Mahāyāna:** To enter the Great Vehicle with all bodhisattvas of one intention.
9.  **Unfailing Action:** To practice the bodhisattva deeds which are never fruitless (*amogha*).
10. **Enlightenment:** To manifest supreme awakening (*abhisambodhi*) in all worlds.

#### The Supreme Dedication (Pariṇāmanā)
Finally, one dedicates the merit using the verses of the *Bhadracaryā* (The King of Prayers):

> "Just as the hero **Mañjuśrī** knows,
> And **Samantabhadra** too—
> Following in their training,
> I dedicate all this merit.
>
> Whatever dedication is praised as supreme
> By the Victors of the three times—
> With that dedication,
> I dedicate all this merit to the Good Conduct (*Bhadracaryā*)."

---

## Scholarly Notes

1.  **Atitṛpti**: "Super-satisfaction" or "Surpassing Satisfaction." This distinguishes the Mahāyāna goal (benefiting all beings limitlessly) from the Hīnayāna goal (mere personal liberation).
2.  **Sāgaramati Sūtra**: Emphasizes *vīrya* (vigor) as the prerequisite for enlightenment. Without energy, the path is impossible.
3.  **Triskandhaka**: The "Three Heap" ritual is the liturgical core of Mahāyāna practice. It remains the standard "Seven Limb Prayer" practice in Tibetan Buddhism today.
4.  **Bhadracaryā**: Often called the *Praṇidhānarāja* (King of Prayers). It is the final section of the *Gaṇḍavyūha Sūtra* (Avataṃsaka). Śāntideva here explicitly instructs utilizing these verses for the ritual.

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-17'></div>

# Chapter 17: The Benefits of Worship (Vandanānuśaṃsā)

---

**Summary**: This chapter details the immense karmic fruits (*anuśaṃsā*) of the worship practices described in the previous chapter. Drawing primarily from the *Avalokana Sūtra* and *Ratnarāśi Sūtra*, it catalogs the physical and spiritual benefits of circumambulating stūpas, offering flowers, lamps, and flags. It concludes with the superior merit of **Rejoicing** (*anumodanā*) and **Great Compassion**, which surpass even material offerings in their capacity to generate Buddhahood.

---

### 1. The Power of a Single Moment (Ekakṣaṇa)

The method of worship has been stated. From where is it known that this causes the increase of merit? From the *Ārya-Avalokana Sūtra*:

> "He avoids the eight inopportune births (*akṣaṇa*)¹ taught by me,
> And he obtains the one opportune moment (*kṣaṇa*), a beautiful arising of a Buddha.
>
> Endowed with color, possessed of form, adorned with marks,
> Endowed with strength and power, he never falls into laziness.
> Wealthy, possessed of great riches, unassailable, and meritorious—
> Having pleased the Lamp of the World, he honors him again and again."

### 2. Benefits of Stūpa Circumambulation (Pradakṣiṇā)

> "It is not possible to exhaust the praise, even in kotis of kalpas,
> Of the man who would perform circumambulation of the Lord of the World's stūpa.
>
> He never becomes blind or crippled, for kotis of kalpas—
> He who, having generated the Awakening Mind (*bodhicitta*), worships the Teacher's stūpa.
>
> Firm in vigor, firm in stamina, heroic, firm in valor,
> He goes quickly to skillfulness (*kauśalya*), having done circumambulation of the stūpa."

### 3. Benefits of Offerings

#### A. Flowers (Puṣpa)
> "He who takes a flower-handful (*puṣpamuṣṭi*) and with an uplifted mind (*udagracitta*)
> Scatters it over the Lord of the World out of faith—
> He becomes meritorious in the human world,
> And having stood in protection, honors the Victor."

#### B. Garlands (Mālā)
> "Having given a garland to the stūpa, he is born among the Thirty-Three Gods.
> He obtains a divine lotus-pond...
> He becomes a Wheel-Turning King (*Cakravartin*), and Śakra, and Brahmā."

#### C. Incense (Dhūpa)
> "He who would offer incense—even smaller than a mustard seed—to the Lord's shrines:
> He wanders in all directions, free from disease, firm in mind, heedful.
> He dispels sorrow... he becomes dear to the multitude."

#### D. Robes (Cīvara) and Flags (Dhvaja/Patākā)
> "He becomes possessor of beautiful robes... golden colored...
> Having given a flag, he defeats the dust of passion... he obtains abundant wealth."

#### E. Lamps (Dīpa)
> "It is not possible to measure the life and glory
> Of one who offers a single lamp to the Buddha-stūpa.
> He attains the Eye... He obtains illumination (*ālokaprāpta*)."

#### F. Music (Vādya) and Parasols (Chatra)
> "Having offered a parasol... he is never burned by the fire of defilements.
> Having offered music... he obtains a melodious voice (*manojñaghoṣa*)."

### 4. Cleaning the Stūpa (Stūpa-saṃśodhana)

> "He who removes withered flowers from the shrine...
> He abandons attachment, hatred, and delusion.
> He abandons all diseases... He attains the unequaled state of a Buddha."

### 5. The Superior Merit of the Mind

The *Ratnarāśi Sūtra* contrasts material offerings with the mind of omniscience:

> "If all beings in the three worlds were to build stūpas as high as Mount Sumeru...
> And if a bodhisattva who **never lacks the Mind of Omniscience** (*avirahitasarvajñatācitta*) were to offer just a single flower...
> This produces greater merit than that previous heap of merit."

And regarding lamps:
> "If a bodhisattva who has left the household life makes a wick soaked in oil and offers it...
> The previous offering of lamps [by worldly kings] does not approach even a hundredth part of this wick."

### 6. The Benefits of Rejoicing (Anumodanā)

The *Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra* establishes the supremacy of **Rejoicing**:

> "If a son or daughter of good family rejoices in the arising of the thought (*cittotpāda*) of bodhisattvas... how much merit does he produce?
>
> The Lord said: 'It might be possible, Kauśika, to measure the weight of the Trisāhasra world system... but not to measure the merit of rejoicing.'
>
> Those who do not know this merit of rejoicing act on the side of Māra.
> Those who rejoice will quickly please the Tathāgatas. They will not see unpleasant forms... they will be born in heaven."

### 7. The Final Weighing

The text concludes by weighing the merit of the four dhyānas (meditations) against the merit of rejoicing:

> "Subhūti, if all beings were to establish themselves in supreme awakening and practice the four dhyānas for eons...
>
> And if a bodhisattva... were to rejoice in the aggregate of morality, concentration, and wisdom (*śīla-samādhi-prajñā-skandha*) of all Buddhas, Pratyekabuddhas, and Śrāvakas... and dedicate it to supreme awakening...
>
> The previous heap of merit does not approach even a hundredth part of this merit of rejoicing."

Thus, the greatest increase of merit comes from:
1.  **Bodhicitta** (The Mind of Awakening).
2.  **Great Compassion** (*Mahākaruṇā*).
3.  **Rejoicing** (*Anumodanā*) in the good of others.

---

## Scholarly Notes

1.  **Eight Akṣaṇas**: The eight inopportune births where one cannot hear the Dharma: hells, hungry ghosts, animals, long-lived gods, border regions, imperfect faculties, wrong views, and a world without a Buddha.
2.  **Stūpa**: The architectural embodiment of the Buddha's Dharmakāya. Worship here is not idolatry but a technique for *buddhānusmṛti* (recollection of the Buddha).
3.  **Pradakṣiṇā**: Circumambulation (keeping the object to one's right). A sign of supreme respect common in Indian culture.
4.  **Avalokana Sūtra**: A key text for lay devotion, detailing the karmic results of simple ritual acts.
5.  **Rejoicing**: This is the "economizer" of the Mahāyāna. By rejoicing in the merit of Buddhas, one cognitively participates in their infinite good, thereby generating immense merit without physical resources.

---

*Śikṣāsamuccaya Project • 2026*




---



<div id='chapter-18'></div>

# Chapter 18: Recollection of the Three Jewels (Ratnatrayānusmṛti)

---

**Summary**: Having detailed the benefits of worship in Chapter 17, Śāntideva now turns to the mental cultivation that sustains it: the **Recollection (*Anusmṛti*) of the Three Jewels** (*Ratnatraya*). This chapter is a treasure trove of Mahāyāna devotional literature, citing the *Rāṣṭrapāla Paripṛcchā* for the Buddha, the *Dharmasaṃgīti* for the Dharma, and the *Vimalakīrti Nirdeśa* for the Sangha. It concludes with a spectacular section from the *Ratnolkādhāraṇī* describing the infinite "Light Rays" (*raśmi*) of the Tathāgata that enact buddha-work across the cosmos.

---

### 1. The Necessity of Constant Practice

The increase of merit through the Practice of Good Conduct (*Bhadracaryā*) has been stated. Here is another cause for it: **the constant practice (*sadābhyāsa*) of faith (*śraddhā*) and the other faculties.**

As stated in the *Ārya-Tathāgataguhya Sūtra*:¹

> "Four things, Great King, lead to distinction (*viśeṣagāmitā*) and non-degeneration (*aparihāṇi*) for those set out in the Mahāyāna. What four?
> 1.  **Faith (*śraddhā*)**: By which one approaches the Noble Ones (*āryas*) and does not do what should not be done.
> 2.  **Respect (*gaurava*)**: By which one listens to the Well-Spoken (*subhāṣita*) and hears the Dharma with an attentive ear.
> 3.  **Humility (*nirmānatā*)**: By which one bows to and reveres the Noble Ones.
> 4.  **Vigor (*vīrya*)**: By which one gains physical and mental lightness (*laghutā*) and accomplishes all tasks."

### 2. The Five Faculties (Indriya)

One must constantly practice faith and the rest. Or, following the *Ārya-Akṣayamati Sūtra*, the **Five Faculties**:

1.  **Faculty of Faith (*Śraddhendriya*)**: Belief in the world-transcending Right View, karma and result, and the Bodhisattva conduct. One believes in the emptiness of the person and phenomena but does not fall into nihilism (*uccheda*).
2.  **Faculty of Vigor (*Vīryendriya*)**: Gathering those dharmas one believes in.
3.  **Faculty of Mindfulness (*Smṛtīndriya*)**: Not losing the dharmas one has gathered.
4.  **Faculty of Concentration (*Samādhīndriya*)**: Making the mind one-pointed (*ekāgra*) on the retained dharmas.
5.  **Faculty of Wisdom (*Prajñendriya*)**: Investigating and penetrating those concentrated dharmas.

### 3. Recollection of the Buddha (Buddhānusmṛti)

How should one recollect the Buddha?
As praised in the *Rāṣṭrapāla Sūtra*:²

> "I worship You, of golden hue and excellent marks,
> Face like a stainless moon, knowledge unequaled;
> None in the three worlds is like You, dustless One.
>
> Your hair is soft, beautiful, glossy, and pure;
> Your uṣṇīṣa is like the King of Mountains.
> None can look down upon Your crown;
> The ūrṇā (hair-tuft) shines between Your brows, O Sage.
>
> Eyes like blue lotuses, pure and white as conch or moon;
> With compassion You gaze upon this world—
> I worship You, O Jina, of stainless vision.
>
> Your tongue is broad, thin, copper-colored;
> With it, You cover Your own face.
> Speaking Dharma, You discipline the world—
> I worship You of sweet, glossy speech.
>
> This world is known by You as like an illusion (*māyopama*),
> Like a stage-play, a dream, a mirage.
> No self (*ātman*), no being, no life-course exists;
> Dharmas are like a mirage, like a flash of lightning.
>
> Seeing the world always confused by passion and a hundred ills,
> Like a peerless Physician, You wander,
> Liberating hundreds of beings, O Sugata."

### 4. Recollection of the Dharma (Dharmānusmṛti)

As stated in the *Dharmasaṃgīti Sūtra*:

> "Here, the Bodhisattva reflects:
> 'The Dharma is **Same** (*sama*); it functions equally toward beings.
> The Dharma is **Impartial** (*apakṣapatita*); it does not function with a view to pleasure.
> The Dharma is **Timeless** (*akālika*); not waiting for time.
> The Dharma is **Without High or Low**; it functions neither for the lofty nor the lowly.
> The Dharma is **Beyond Purity or Impurity** (*utkarṣāpakarṣāpagata*).
> The Dharma is **Solidly Established** (*sadādhiṣṭhita*); it functions night and day.
> The Dharma is **Unlimited** (*aprameya*); like space, it does not decrease or increase.
> The Dharma is **Not Protected**; it protects beings.
> The Dharma is **Not a Refuge Seeker**; it is the refuge of the whole world.'"³

### 5. Recollection of the Sangha (Saṅghānusmṛti)

The Sangha consists of those who speak Dharma, think Dharma, and are a Field of Dharma.
As stated in the *Arya-Vimalakīrti Nirdeśa*, the qualities of the Bodhisattvas should be cultivated:⁴

> "In a single moment (*ekakṣaṇa*), these confident Bodhisattvas
> Display the forms, sounds, and voices of all beings.
>
> They appear old, sick, or dead;
> For the ripening of beings, they play with illusion-dharmas.
>
> They show the burning of the kalpa, earth consumed by fire;
> To those with the notion of permanence, they show impermanence.
>
> Invited by hundreds of thousands of beings in one kingdom,
> They eat in everyone's house,
> And bend them all toward Awakening (*bodhi*).
>
> Whatever arts, sciences, or crafts exist,
> They have reached perfection in all, bringing happiness to beings.
>
> They become suns, moons, Indras, Brahmas,
> Lords of Creatures, Water, Fire, Earth, and Wind.
>
> During kalpas of disease, they become supreme medicines;
> During kalpas of famine, they become food and drink;
> During kalpas of weapons, they become meditators on Maitrī (Love)."

### 6. The Light Rays of the Tathāgata (Ratnolkādhāraṇī)

Just as the *Ratnolkādhāraṇī* describes the Bodhisattva qualities, so should they be cultivated:⁵

> "Releasing the ray called **Garland-Array** (*Mālyavyūhā*):
> It rains down garlands, ornaments, and canopies.
> With these scattered everywhere, Great Beings worship the Jina.
>
> Releasing the ray called **Peace-Maker** (*Śāntikarī*):
> Beings urged by its light become free of passion, hate, and delusion.
> Turning away from bad friends and crowds,
> They praise the peace of the forest (*araṇya*).
>
> Releasing the ray called **Produce-Promoter** (*Sasyasaṃpādanī*):
> It rains down rain, producing crops and herbs,
> Satisfying those tormented by hunger and thirst.
>
> Releasing the ray called **Insight-Lamp** (*Prajñāpradīpa*):
> It reveals dharmas as empty, selfless, unborn, like illusions.
> Beings understand that phenomena are like dreams or mirages.
>
> Releasing the ray called **Fear-Remover** (*Abhayaṃdadā*):
> Beings touched by it are freed from bonds, beatings, and threats.
> They refrain from taking life and become protectors of the fearful.
>
> Releasing the ray called **Wealth-Shower** (*Arthanidarśanī*):
> Lists of treasures appear to the poor.
> Obtaining inexhaustible wealth, they practice giving.
>
> Releasing the ray called **Sense-Purifier** (*Indriyaviśodhanī*):
> The blind see forms; the deaf hear sounds;
> The noseless smell fragrances; the mute speak sweetly;
> The crippled gain perfect bodies; the mad regain their minds."

### 7. Conclusion

Just as the wind, unlearned in the perfections, brings happiness to the world,
Just as the magician displays illusions without attachment,
Just as the sun shines on the blind without discrimination—
So do the Tathāgatas and their children (Bodhisattvas) perform their work.

**This is the Recollection of the Three Jewels.**

---

### Footnotes

1.  **Ārya-Tathāgataguhya Sūtra**: One of the earliest lists of the "Four Dharmas" (*catvāro dharmāḥ*) for Bodhisattva stabilization.
2.  **Rāṣṭrapāla Paripṛcchā**: A major source for the concept of the *kāyavibhāga* (physical characteristics) of the Buddha, emphasizing the 32 Marks (*lakṣaṇa*) as products of accumulated merit.
3.  **Dharma as Agent**: This section personifies the Dharma as an active, impartial cosmic force giving refuge, rather than just a passive teaching.
4.  **Vimalakīrti Nirdeśa**: This famous section lists the "Skillful Means" (*upāya*) of Bodhisattvas, including taking birth as prostitutes, gamblers, or kings to convert beings.
5.  **Ratnolkādhāraṇī**: This text provides the most elaborate "Light Ray" (*raśmi*) theology in the *Śikṣāsamuccaya*, linking specific colors/lights to specific ethical transformations (e.g., "Peace-Maker" light -> Forest dwelling).




---



<div id='chapter-19'></div>

# Chapter 19: The Increase of Merit (Puṇyavṛddhi)

---

**Summary**: This is the final chapter of the *Śikṣāsamuccaya*. Having established the protections and purifications, Śāntideva concludes with the **Increase of Merit** (*Puṇyavṛddhi*). The central theme is the Bodhisattva's transformation of everyday actions into merit through **dedication (*pariṇāmanā*)** and the cultivation of **emptiness/compassion**. It culminates in a spectacular praise of Mañjuśrī, the embodiment of wisdom and the patron of this text.

---

### 1. Dedication in Daily Life

Another cause for the increase of merit is the **welfare of beings in all states** (*sarvāvasthāsu sattvārthaḥ*).
As stated in the *Ārya-Ratnamegha Sūtra*:¹

> "When offering a flower or incense to a Tathāgata shrine, he dedicates it: 'May all beings be free from the stench of immorality (*dauḥśīlya*) and attain the fragrance of the Tathāgata's morality.'
>
> When sweeping or cleaning, he dedicates it: 'May I sweep away the ungraceful conduct of all beings.'
>
> When raising a parasol, he dedicates it: 'May I remove the burning heat of defilements (*kleśa-paridāha*) for all beings.'
>
> When entering a monastery: 'May I lead all beings into the city of Nirvāṇa.'
> When leaving: 'May I liberate all beings from the prison of Saṃsāra.'
>
> When opening a door: 'May I open the door to the Sugata ways with transcendental knowledge.'
> When closing a door: 'May I close the doors to all evil destinies (*apāya*).'
>
> When sitting down: 'May all beings sit on the seat of awakening (*bodhimaṇḍa*).'
> When lying on the right side: 'May I lead all beings to Parinirvāṇa.'
> When rising: 'May I raise all beings from the ambush of defilments.'
>
> When washing hands: 'May I wash away the residual traces (*vāsanā*) of defilements for all beings.'
> When washing feet: 'May I wash away the dust of defilements.'
> When cleaning teeth: 'May I remove the stains of defilements.'"

### 2. Fearlessness in the Wilderness

The Bodhisattva's merit increases through **fearlessness** (*atrāsa*).
As stated in the *Ārya-Prajñāpāramitā*:²

> "Śāriputra, a Bodhisattva in a wilderness full of wild beasts (*vyālakāntāra*) should not be afraid. Why? Because he has renounced everything for the sake of beings.
>
> He should think: 'If beasts eat me, may it be a gift to them. My perfection of giving (*dānapāramitā*) will be fulfilled.'
>
> If in a wilderness of thieves (*corakāntāra*): 'If they take my belongings, let it be a gift. If they take my life, let me generate no anger. Thus my perfections of giving, morality, and patience will be fulfilled.'
>
> If in a wilderness of waterless wastes (*pānīyakāntāra*): 'I must train to cut off the thirst of all beings.'
>
> If in a wilderness of famine (*bubhukṣākāntāra*): He should armor himself (*saṃnāha*): 'I will purify my Buddha-field so that such famines never exist there. I will ensure all beings are satisfied with food and drink thought of by mind alone, like the trāyastriṃśa gods.'"

### 3. The Gift of Dharma (Dharmadāna)

The **Gift of Dharma** (*dharmadāna*) without material desire (*nirāmiṣa*) is a supreme cause of merit.
As stated in the *Ārya-Adhyāśayasaṃcodana Sūtra*, there are twenty benefits, including:
*   Mindfulness (*smṛti*)
*   Intelligence (*mati*)
*   Protection by non-humans (*amanuṣya*)
*   Meeting the Buddhas

The *Ārya-Prajñāpāramitā* declares: teaching even a single verse of the Perfection of Wisdom to another Bodhisattva generates more merit than teaching the entire Śrāvaka-Dharma to the entire universe. Why? Because the Bodhisattva preserves the lineage of the Buddhas.³

**How to Give Dharma**:
As stated in the *Saddharmapuṇḍarīka (Lotus Sutra)*:
> "Entering the abode, wearing clean robes,
> Sitting on a comfortable seat,
> With a mind free from envy or fatigue,
> He should teach the Dharma day and night.
> He seeks nothing for himself—
> Only thinking: 'May I and these beings become Buddhas.'"

### 4. The Root of Merit: Bodhicitta

In summary, the **Bodhicitta** is the root of the increase of merit.
As stated in the *Ārya-Ratnakaraṇḍaka Sūtra*:

> "Just as aromatic trees grow from the four elements, Mañjuśrī, so the Bodhisattva's roots of good, gathered by Bodhicitta and dedicated to omniscience, grow continuously."

### 5. Final Arguments on Self and Other

Śāntideva concludes with verses (likely from the *Bodhicaryāvatāra* tradition) deconstructing the Self to justify altruism:⁴

> "If fear and suffering are disliked by me and others equally,
> What is so special about 'Me' that I protect myself and not the other?
>
> 'My future suffering does not harm me now'—this is a false assumption.
> The 'I' who acts is dead; the 'I' who suffers is another birth.
>
> The foot's pain is not the hand's pain, yet the hand protects the foot.
> Why? Because they are part of one organism.
> Thus should one view the world as an organism (*ātmabhāva*).
>
> 'Self' is a fiction. It is a heap of elements/aggregates.
> Suffering exists, but there is no 'Sufferer.'
> Therefore, suppress suffering simply because it is suffering.
> Do not ask 'Whose?'"

### 6. Homage to Mañjuśrī

The text concludes with a final dedication and praise of Mañjuśrī, the Prince of Wisdom:

> "I bow to Mañjuśrī, the condensed form of the world's welfare.
> He who walks in the ocean of Buddha-fields in all directions.
> He who destroys the armies of Māra.
> He who is the cool lake for those scorched by suffering.
> To Mañjughoṣa, the Teacher, I bow."

**This is the nineteenth chapter, The Increase of Merit.**

**Here ends the Śikṣāsamuccaya, the Compendium of Training.**

---

### Footnotes

1.  **Ritualization of Daily Life**: These verses from the *Ratnamegha* serve as the prototype for "Gāthās" used in Zen and Vinaya practice, transforming mundane acts (opening doors, washing) into Mahāyāna rituals.
2.  **Wilderness Training**: The *kāntāra* (wilderness) is the testing ground for the perfections. The Bodhisattva uses danger to radicalize his generosity.
3.  **Superiority of Mahāyāna Dharma**: The mathematical comparison (1 verse of Prajñā vs. whole Śrāvaka canon) emphasizes that the *cause* (Bodhisattva training) is more valuable than the *result* (Arhatship) because it leads to the total liberation of all beings.
4.  **Parātmasamatā**: These philosophical verses mirror *Bodhicaryāvatāra* Chapter 8, arguing that since "Self" is a delusion using temporal continuity (child -> adult), we can equally use spatial continuity (self -> other) to justify compassion.
